Adam Tahirkeli *insisted* that the death cult K be included in this file
(or the "ballot K" as he originally labeled it). Because I have a weak
will-to-power, I have relented to his demands; however, I have tried to
make it very clear that it's part of this file so that no one misses it.
Hopefully the amount of aff answers that I included will make actually
going for it too difficult.

I'm actually not sure if Andrew Yim contributed evidence to this file or
not, because Miao Sun compiled it and did most of the work (including
many requests for reorganization and more aff cards), but he was in the
Slack group chat for it, so I want to make sure he gets credit if he did
pitch in. Regardless, props to him for evidence contributed elsewhere
and for generally being a good labbie. EDIT: Apparently Andrew did not
contribute cards, but Matthew Ciancanelli did. Shout out to Matthew too!

If you have questions, especially about death cult, please email me at
<guyfry@umich.edu>.

## K\-\--Psychoanalysis

### 1NC

#### The 1AC is an act of ontological security, attempting to cohere and control the World via the establishment of fantasy in an attempt to reach the ephemeral Real. That reifies the Lack, creating psychic harm and reifies suffering.

Pasko **Kisić-Merino** 20**25**. He was a PhD candidate at the
Department of Political Science, Karlstad University, Sweden. He holds
degrees in Political Science and Global Studies.\"Enjoying the Fall; The
Normalisation of the Far-Right as an Algorithmically-Mediated Fantasy of
Ontological (In)Security,\" Karlstad University Studies,
https://doi.org/10.59217/ygdq9007. 苗

\[alternative access link:
https://www.diva-portal.org/smash/get/diva2:1935131/FULLTEXT01.pdf\]

[Ontological security]{.underline} provides a [valuable departure point
for discussing]{.underline} the [analytical array of
Lacanian]{.underline} [psychoanalysis]{.underline} and [orienting it
towards the phenomenon of far-right normalisation]{.underline} by
[focusing]{.underline} on the **[subject49 immersed in this
fantasy]{.underline}**. Contrary to [common
misunderstandings]{.underline} and [misreadings]{.underline},
[Lacanian]{.underline} psychoanalysis does not deal with the [subject as
pertaining to an individual but instead as lack]{.underline} -- as a
**[void bereft of essence]{.underline}** -- dependent or subjectified by
and to the Symbolic Order (Lacan, 1998, pp. 77--78; Stavrakakis, 1999,
pp. 36--37; Van Haute, 2001, pp. 159--161). [Lacan views the
subject]{.underline} (usually departing from the figure of an infant)
[as emerging through its entry into the Symbolic Order]{.underline} --
this is, its entry into the realm of language, culture, and law (2006,
pp. 12, 227; see also Van Haute, 2001, p. 10). This [entry is marked by
a traumatic split, where the now-subject loses its
pre-sociolinguistic]{.underline} [wholeness]{.underline}, generating a
[constitutive lack built around an ultimately unattainable
object-cause]{.underline} of [desire]{.underline}. Lacan [understands
this split as the **moment of subjectivity itself,**]{.underline} where
the ["I" is divided into]{.underline} [ego or
false/illusory]{.underline} self -- the mask with which we traverse
[sociosymbolic]{.underline} [space]{.underline} -- and the
**[unconscious]{.underline}** -- the [Symbolic's machination through/as
the subject (]{.underline}Evans, 2006; pp. 197-198; Lacan, 1998, p. 26).
Contrary to misconceptions stemming from psychological, behaviouralist,
and critical theory approaches, the [unconscious is not some "inner
world" holding the "true essence" of the individual]{.underline}, but
rather the [structuring machination of the Symbolic Order through the
subject]{.underline} (Fink, 1995, pp. 7--9; C. Jones, 2020; Lacan, 1998,
p. 77). The split subject is not reduced to what is commonly
conceptualised as an individual or "self" but rather encompasses more
complex assemblages of existence, like groups, families, nations,
political systems, and even technologies (see C. Jones, 2020; Millar,
2021; Stavrakakis, 1999, pp. 3--4). [The subject is, thus, not an innate
or universal human condition or entity but a **possibility structured as
sheer lack**]{.underline} and [conditioned by the process of
signification on whatever ontological assemblage it
encompasses]{.underline}. The subject is created upon the entity's entry
into the Symbolic realm, [which is irremediably signified in language
(e]{.underline}.g., "I am a Swede / Father / Protestant / Patriot"). The
subject does not exist in a traditional sense but is a naturalised
façade. [This mask does not cover a "real truth" or fixity]{.underline}
(Lacan, 1998, p. 48, 2006, p. 153), but rather is the [operation of the
unconscious through the illusion of the self.]{.underline} In short,
"[the subject is nothing but \[its\] split"]{.underline} (Fink, 1995, p.
45; see also Lacan, 2006, pp. 286, 436), and it is experienced as a
[structuring fantasy of ontological security]{.underline}.
Simultaneously, the [split's residual fantasy, the ego, is]{.underline}
the [stabilising]{.underline} [fantasy]{.underline} of the "self" and
thus governs the illusion of a "conscious sense of agency" (Evans, 2006,
pp. 197-198). [The identification with this self-image, through what
Lacan labels the infant's "mirror stage", as "whole]{.underline}" and
coherent represents the subject's inextricable misrecognition
(méconnaissance) since the [ego's formation through the split with the
unconscious can never be fully]{.underline} [reintegrated nor
grasped]{.underline} (Evans, 2006, p. 11; Kinnvall & Svensson, 2022;
Lacan, 2006, pp. 808-809). This [misrecognition]{.underline},
paradoxically, is what [enables the subject's sense or experience of
agency]{.underline}. [Agency is both "possible" and **ultimately
illusory**]{.underline} as it [resides in the subject's
inescapable]{.underline} [misrecognition]{.underline}, and thus, it is
curtailed by the unassailable current of the unconscious via the
Symbolic order (cf. Ruti, 2014; Sheikh, 2017). This paradoxical and
anti-essentialist quality of the subject acts as a departure point,
signifying the value of the post-clinical adaptation of Lacanian
psychoanalysis to the study of political phenomena (Stavrakakis, 1999,
p. 36). For Lacan, the condition and function of the subject are
organised in the interdependence and interplay of the three psychic
realms: The Symbolic, the Imaginary, and the Real. [The Symbolic Order,
the "linguistically-constituted social order"]{.underline} (Pohl &
Swyngedouw, 2023, p. 3), [governs intersubjectivity]{.underline} and
**[shapes reality]{.underline}** by [providing the coordinates for the
production of **fantasies like ontological security**]{.underline} (see
also Fink, 1995, p. 56). This Order plays a crucial and [paradoxical
role in the subject's]{.underline} [psyche]{.underline}. It structures
the possibility of pursuing desires through fantasies (Hoens, 2020, p.
48), yet this perpetually effectuates the lack and anxiety stemming from
the subject's entry into the sociosymbolic realm (Glynos, 2001). In
other words, the Symbolically-organised pursuit of desire involves
perpetuating the anxiety-inducing lack that, in turn, mobilises the
subject towards the relentless yet ultimately fruitless attempts at
satisfying desire (Van Haute, 2001, pp. 106-107; see also Glynos, 2001;
Hook, 2017). For Lacanian ontological security scholars, this
[**anxiety**-**induced** (and infusing) yet **incessant motion**
**towards our desire is** what marks the subject as "becoming" rather
than "being]{.underline}" (Kinnvall, 2017). Lacan argues that desire is
withheld or arrested by the figure of the Other, which is both "other"
persons (i.e., experienced in our desire for their recognition to
produce stable identifications) and the "big Other": [the manifestation
of the Symbolic authority]{.underline}, the "space containing the
[signifiers]{.underline} that precede and [undermine any
subjectivity"]{.underline} (Hoens, 2020, p. 48). Thus, [**desire** is
the **politically** **mobilising**, **socially conditioned possibility**
of both **filling the subject's** **lack**]{.underline} and
[perpetuating]{.underline} it (Stavrakakis, 1999, p. 44; Lacan, 2006,
pp. 450-451). Meanwhile, the [Imaginary Order]{.underline} governs our
identities and fantasies, which are [shaped by the Symbolic structure or
chain of signification]{.underline}. It provides meaning and narratives
to the [otherwise abstract and ungraspable structure of the
Symbolic,]{.underline} [allowing it to work through and as
us]{.underline} (Hook, 2017; Žižek, 2009). The [Imaginary]{.underline}
is the domain where the [subject's ego]{.underline} -- the [inherently
unstable narrative of the self]{.underline} (Homer, 2020, p. 99) -- [is
formed]{.underline} through imaginary identifications with images, and
thus, where constitutive and differentiating [fantasies about the self
and Others are constructed]{.underline} (Lacan, 2006, p. 40-41; Machin,
2020; Mouffe, 2005). It is through the Imaginary that ["the symbolic
takes hold in even the deepest recesses]{.underline} of the human
organism" (Lacan, 2006, p. 6). Given its vast complexity, the Symbolic
Order cannot be fully grasped by the subject but rather is encountered
in, or partially accessed through, historically-contingent, [imaginary
articulations like "modernity", "late capitalism", the liberal
international order, or "the nation"]{.underline} (Machin, 2020;
Walkerdine, 2020; Wilson, 2017) -- i.e., "imaginary impregnations
\[acting as\] [partializations of the symbolic alternative that give the
signifying chain its appearance" and meaning]{.underline} (Lacan, 2006,
p. 6). **[Fantasies of ontological security]{.underline}** are [thus
powerful "symbolic]{.underline} [partializations]{.underline}" that
shape the [Symbolic order of **liberal modernity** -- i.e., the modern
liberal order under neoliberalism]{.underline} (Waklerdine, 2020;
Wilson, 2017). These ideologically interpellate its subjects (cf. Žižek,
2009, pp. 15-16), conditioning the affective normalisation of the
farright (Kisić-Merino & Kinnvall, 2023). The final and most complex
component of the Lacanian Borromean knot, the **[Real]{.underline}**, is
the order of the un-signifiable and [traumatic]{.underline}, what
[exists prior to and **beyond language**]{.underline}. The [Real is
not]{.underline} reality -- the [subjective experience shaped by
fantasies in pursuit]{.underline} [of stabilising pleasure]{.underline}
(Lacan, 2006, p. 324; Evans, 2006, p. 164) -- but the realm of the
inassimilable, which resists symbolisation and is [manifested and
experienced in fantasmatic and symbolic dislocation]{.underline} (Evans,
2006, p. 162). The Symbolic Order and the fantasies that give it meaning
are inherently incomplete and limited due to the limitations of its
signifiers (language and, broadly speaking, law) since they exist
negating or in opposition to other articulations (Glynos, 2001). The
[Real is a structure encountered when Symbolic]{.underline}
[identifications and imaginary concoctions fail.]{.underline} However,
for Lacan, this [process of the]{.underline} Real is what,
paradoxically, also [supports fantasy as much as the latter **"protects
the real"**]{.underline} (1998, p. 41). The Real's indifference to and
transcendence of the Symbolic Order implies its equal disregard for the
subject and its pleasure. [The Real triggers anxiety and trauma by
submerging fantasmatically-articulated reality into
overwhelming]{.underline} (im)possibility (Lacan, 2006, p. 324), which,
in turn, [mobilises]{.underline} the ([re)generation of fantasy
as]{.underline} a defence mechanism. Bruce Fink argues that the
[Real]{.underline} is what [precedes language, what constitutes the
"blissful" pre-sociosymbolic state of]{.underline} the
([future]{.underline}) split subject where its lack and (thus) desire
have not been developed (1995, p. 24; see also Homer, 2020, pp. 98-99).
[Lacan's statement that "the **lack of the lack makes the real,** which
emerges only there]{.underline} [as a cork"]{.underline} (1998, p. ix)
attends to the Real's "blockage" of the motion of desire resulting from
"lacking lack", which leads to sociosymbolic dislocations caused by (and
experienced as) anxiety. For Yannis Stavrakakis, the [**renovation of
lack** through **anxiety** implies the **renovation of** **desire** in
the]{.underline} [form of **new political articulations**]{.underline}.
In other words, the encounter with [the Real is a
quintessential]{.underline} [moment of political and historical movement
since it leads to "the antagonistic articulation of]{.underline}
[different discourses that attempt to symbolise \[the Real's\] traumatic
nature,]{.underline} to **[suture the lack]{.underline}** it creates"
(1999, p. 74; see also Burgess, 2017). [In turn, discourse is the
articulation of signifiers that]{.underline} [shape intersubjectivity
against the Real,]{.underline} mediated by fantasmatic meanings, and
regulated by the Symbolic's demands, i.e., "a social bond, founded in
language" (D. Evans, 2006, p. 45; Lacan, 1999, p. 17). The multifaceted
and complex interaction between [Lacan's three orders]{.underline} of
existence points to the crucial role of the [interplay between fantasy,
desire]{.underline} (and its object-cause), and [anxiety]{.underline}
for the **[analysis of political dislocation]{.underline}** -- i.e.,
[the **generation of crisis**]{.underline} -- embedded in phenomena like
far-right normalisation. For [Lacan, the subject is fundamentally void,
perpetually changing and reconstituting its identity and symbolic
attachments according to the demands of the Symbolic]{.underline} Order
(2006, p. 84). This subject [constantly attempts to stabilise
itself]{.underline} [against]{.underline} its constitutive
[lack]{.underline} -- the void that binds it to [unrealisable desires
and prospects of fulfilment]{.underline} -- **[via ontological
security]{.underline}** **[fantasies]{.underline}** (Browning, 2018;
Eberle, 2019). These fantasies should not be understood as an
abstraction but rather as stabilising responses to our inescapable lack
and otherwise overwhelming Symbolic commitments (Lacan, 2006, pp.
84-85). Fantasies like ontological security constitute what we interpret
as political, social, cultural, and affective reality (Evans, 2006, p.
164). [Fantasy is a "narrative that structures our experience of
reality, organizing our pursuit of desire"]{.underline} (Maher, 2023, p.
2), and thus, it constitutes a core analytical and [ontological
ordinance of antagonistic political reality]{.underline} (Sharpe &
Turner, 2020; Stavrakakis, 1999, pp. 108--109). For Slavoj Žižek, the
political kernel of fantasy can be prominently attested in how ideology
operates through the subject, shaping its desire. [For Žižek, ideology
is a form of fantasy that transcends knowledge and instead is found in
the structure of the unconscious]{.underline}. The subject becomes
sociopolitically bound due to its misrecognition not of social reality
but rather of the illusion that structures said reality -- i.e., the
ideological fantasy (2009, pp. 29-30; see also Hook, 2017).

#### The aff's attempt to suture the void via unconscious desire, mobilized via the reading of the 1AC. Refuse to engage in aff systems of ontological security, and traverse their fantasy. 

Pasko **Kisić-Merino** 20**25**. He was a PhD candidate at the
Department of Political Science, Karlstad University, Sweden. He holds
degrees in Political Science and Global Studies.\" Enjoying the Fall;
The Normalisation of the Far-Right as an Algorithmically-Mediated
Fantasy of Ontological (In)Security,\" Karlstad University Studies,
https://doi.org/10.59217/ygdq9007. 苗

\[alternative access link:
https://www.diva-portal.org/smash/get/diva2:1935131/FULLTEXT01.pdf\]

The [ontological security]{.underline} **[fantasy]{.underline}** is a
[compelling illusion that politically organises]{.underline} (i.e.,
ideologically interpellates; Mandelbaum, 2023) [the subject around the
**unconscious desire to quench** **lack** or recapture primordial
fullness]{.underline} (Kinnvall & Svensson, 2022). In the so-called
Western hemisphere, this "quest" can be attested in the conveyance and
appeal of far-right discourses as vessels of White, "Western"
civilisational anxiety, which [effectuate belonging through
**positioning the "threatening"**]{.underline} **[Other]{.underline}**
(Le Monde, 2023; PA Media, 2023; Schultheis, 2018) as, e.g., "poisoning
the blood of our nation" (Michael, 2023). In Lacanian terms, the
[subject's quest or desire for ontological security is]{.underline}
[oriented by the object-cause of desire]{.underline} (or objet petit a;
Lacan, 2006, p. 571). This [object-cause is the]{.underline} remnant or
[surplus from the subject's entrance into the sociosymbolic]{.underline}
[realm]{.underline} -- it is what [simultaneously pulls and causes
**desire** yet cannot be fully re-integrated into the
Symbolic]{.underline} (Glynos, 2001). The [position of the object-cause
of desire at "the centre of the symbolic framework of the \[subject's\]
self-understanding causes us to desire it]{.underline}" (Vulović &
Ejdus, 2024, p. 126), which evidences a "push-and-pull dynamic between
the agent and the object" (ibid.). [Desire manifests in insatiably
seeking **fulfilment via the objectcause of desire**,]{.underline} a
"longing to retrieve \[the\] primordial bliss" (jouissance) lost in our
painful entry to [the Symbolic Order, **manifested politically** in,
e.g., an ethnically homogeneous and **purportedly harmonious**
**past**]{.underline} (Pohl & Swyngedouw, 2023, p. 3). [Ontological
security recasts **security** as **fantasmatic fullness**]{.underline},
a [promise of recapturing the longed-for]{.underline} [objet petit
a]{.underline} that simultaneously [forces the encounter with its loss
and, thus, with]{.underline} [overwhelming]{.underline} (yet
**[politically-mobilising]{.underline}**) **[anxiety]{.underline}**
(Stavrakakis, 1999, p. 82; Van Haute, 2001, pp. 115-116). Vulović and
Ejdus argue that anxiety is about the lost object-cause of desire around
which fantasy is structured. [Thus, anxiety is a way to sustain desire
when its object-cause is absent, **which conversely makes desire --
through fantasy** -- a "remedy for anxiety" (Evans, 2006, p.
11).]{.underline}

### \*Links

### 2NC\-\--L\-\--Climate

#### Climate change is the affirmative's objet a, the target of the Lack's desire. Their attempt at climate policy is a self-sabotaging fantasy to suture the void, which only reifies the Real. 

Erik **Swyngedouw** 7-15-20**22**. Erik Achille Marie Swyngedouw
(pronounced \[ˈeːrɪk ˈsʋɪŋɣədʌu\]) is professor of geography at the
University of Manchester in the School of Environment, Education and
Development and a member of the Manchester Urban Institute. \"The
unbearable lightness of climate populism,\" Environmental Politics,
Volume 31, Issue 5: Operating at the Frontiers of Democracy? Mitigating
climate change in times of populism, pages
904-925，https://doi.org/10.1080/09644016.2022.2090636. 苗

[Lacanian psychoanalytic]{.underline} [theory]{.underline} is
notoriously complex, jargon-filled, and occasionally inconsistent (see,
for example, Žižek Citation1992, Fink Citation1995, McGowan
Citation2004). Nonetheless, it has become [increasingly influential in
recent years to account for political transformations]{.underline} (or
lack thereof) (Žižek Citation1999, McGowan Citation2013, Stavrakakis
Citation1999, Citation2007, Tomšič and Zevnik Citation2015, Pohl and
Swyngedouw Citation2021a, Citation2021b) and to [situate]{.underline}
and explain [particular socio-ecological and political-ecological
conditions (]{.underline}Fletcher Citation2013; Healy, Citation2010,
Watt Citation2021, Dodds Citation2011).

In what follows, we shall [mobilize the Lacanian]{.underline} notion of
[fantasy]{.underline} and how [this operates through]{.underline}
[different subject positions that organize the social bond.]{.underline}
For Lacan, these [subject positions]{.underline} (or discourses)
[operate in relationship to **object a**]{.underline} (the object-cause
of desire) on the one hand and to the Discourse of the Master on the
other (Fink Citation2018). We shall briefly explore the key Lacanian
insights as they relate to the particular forms of denial that infuse
different climate positions and discourses. As suggested above, [climate
populism is predicated upon a **process of denial**]{.underline}
[whereby a trauma/desire too painful to be symbolised and represented
becomes locked into the subconscious]{.underline}, [but nonetheless
exerts an **irresistible pull**]{.underline} -- precisely because
unsymbolised [-- on personal and political acting, despite]{.underline}
its [inconsistency with respect to the climate]{.underline}
[knowledge]{.underline} that is [explicitly]{.underline} symbolised and
fully [endorsed]{.underline} (Weintrobe Citation2013, Orange
Citation2017). This perspective provides the basis both for a [**radical
revision** of the theoretical place of ideology]{.underline} (fantasy in
Lacanian parlance) and for [situating its functioning in the structuring
of everyday private and collective life.]{.underline}

In the long historical genealogy of the theoretical debate on ideology,
the dominant view is one whereby [ideology is understood as a veil
between people's understanding]{.underline} of the world on the one hand
and the true or real situation, [which is obscured by an 'ideological'
gaze, on the other]{.underline} (Sharpe and Turner Citation2019). [It
was]{.underline} the task of both ideology critique and of the sciences
more generally to excavate the truth of the situation, thereby
[piercing]{.underline} [the]{.underline} ideological [veil]{.underline}
and [open a space]{.underline} that [permitted a non-alienated and
transparent relationship between knowing and acting**, freed from
ideological 'distortions'.**]{.underline} Of course, this view was
fundamentally [predicated on the possibility of rendering reality fully
symbolizable and, therefore, knowable.]{.underline}

Slavoj [Žižek]{.underline} transformed this perspective by
[insisting]{.underline}, following Lacan, [that everyday life itself
is]{.underline} [structured as fantasy.]{.underline} In other words,
[everyday practices are structured as an (ideological)
fantasy]{.underline}, one that **[represses an underlying
Real]{.underline}**, which remains elusive. For Žižek, [the Real refers
to the unsymbolizable but nonetheless active **movement of capitalist
relations**]{.underline} and their associated class conflicts and
tensions (Büscher-Ulbrich Citation2018). For him, therefore, [ideology
does not function]{.underline} as a [false representation]{.underline}
that covers a hidden, but recoverable, reality, but is [the actual
lived]{.underline} [experience]{.underline} through [which a semblance
of social order is produced,]{.underline} one that **[locks the
Real]{.underline}** of the [situation in an unconscious
register]{.underline} (Žižek Citation1994).

[Social order]{.underline} is, for Jacques Lacan, [established by the
Symbolic (and the Imaginary]{.underline}) and is
[discursively]{.underline} [structured]{.underline}. However, he insists
on the [void at the heart of the Symbolic.]{.underline} For Lacan, the
[world]{.underline} of the symbolic order is always
**[lacking]{.underline}** (Newman Citation2004a; Citation2004b). It is
an [unstable, shifting]{.underline}, and [necessarily incomplete
register]{.underline}. There is [always excess, a remainder or
reminder]{.underline}, that cannot be symbolised or represented; a hard
kernel that sticks like a fishbone in the throat and exerts an
unalienable scratch or pull. This [lure of the excessive
remainder]{.underline} is what [Lacan calls the Real, a]{.underline}
[complex, shifting spectral presence that 'resists symbolisation
absolutely']{.underline} (Lacan Citation1991a: 66). There is always a
gap between the Real as the 'unassimilable' (Lacan Citation1977, p. 55)
and any attempt at its symbolic assimilation.

Within this slippery and inconsistent configuration, [the Imaginary
provides an illusionary sense of]{.underline} [coherence or completeness
of the world]{.underline}. It operates through a **[performative
fantasy]{.underline}** that [permits]{.underline} [life]{.underline} to
be [lived in an illusion of coherence]{.underline} and fulfilment. The
[Imaginary operates through fantasy]{.underline}, the potent operator
that [makes us experience life as full and somehow
meaningful]{.underline}. Of course, for **[fantasy]{.underline}** to
[integrate successfully the individual into an illusion of an undivided
subject,]{.underline} it must [deny]{.underline}, that is repress,
disavow, or foreclose, **[the Real]{.underline}**, covering up 'the
revolt', [locking it into the unconscious.]{.underline} Nonetheless, it
is precisely drive -- or the lack, [gap]{.underline}, excess -- that
will [animate]{.underline} the [Imaginary]{.underline} and
[fuel]{.underline} **[fantasy]{.underline}**, and that keeps on
**[insisting on fulfilment]{.underline}**, but [invariably displaced
into the register of jouissance **(enjoyment**]{.underline}), the
[insatiable (because impossible) search for full satisfaction
articulated]{.underline} [around object a,]{.underline} the 'little
piece of the Real' that sets desire into motion.
**[Fantasy]{.underline}**, in sum, is precisely the [terrain through
which **repressing, foreclosing, or disavowing** the Real is
effectuated]{.underline}, yet in doing so, it [launches the subject into
an abyss that will invariably return in the form of, among other,
anxiety, pain, **insecurity**, **depression**, **melancholia**,
hysterical acting out, or obsessive repetitive rituals]{.underline}
(Pohl and Swyngedouw Citation2021a). This is why one of the [crucial
aims of psychoanalysis]{.underline}, for Lacan, is to '[**traverse the
fantasy'** and to 'encounter the Real']{.underline} (Lacan Citation1977,
p. 273).

The [social bond propels the three registers of the Imaginary, the
Symbolic, and the Real not solely onto the subject]{.underline}, but
also onto the [terrain]{.underline} of social, cultural, and
**[political life]{.underline}** (Wilson and Swyngedouw Citation2014).
For Lacan, there are [four discourses that pertain to different
subject-positions with respect to the practices that structure
social]{.underline} relationships and the social bond, and they reflect
different relationships to desire and enjoyment (Klepec Citation2016).
As Mark Bracher insists, 'discourse is a necessary structure that
subsists in certain fundamental relations that are intra-subjective,
social, or with the nonhuman world' (Bracher Citation1994b: 107). Lacan
names these four discourses as respectively of the Master, the
University, the Hysteric, and the Analyst (Lacan Citation2007).

3.1. The discourse of the master: sustaining unsustainability

The Discourse of the Master operates through the Imaginary and the
Symbolic. The [Symbolic]{.underline} [stands]{.underline} for the
assemblage of [institutions, laws, formal and informal rules,
regulations, and actors that constitute society as a fully closed
configuration]{.underline} (Dean Citation2006). It [quilts a chain of
signifiers]{.underline} (like democracy, state, cohesion, freedom, etc.
for liberal democracy) -- a discourse -- that [sustains]{.underline} the
[order]{.underline} and [assigns everyone a certain place]{.underline}
within the social edifice. It [constitutes the]{.underline}
[Law]{.underline} (the big Other) that [prescribes, enjoins, prohibits,
entices, punishes, or rewards]{.underline}. The Master tells us what to
do and what not, what to desire and, most importantly, how to desire. It
assigns and ascribes a place and a function to all; it tells us who and
what we are or should be (Gunder and Hillier Citation2009). In the
present configuration, the [symbolic order constitutes the pluralist
representative institutions of government, the formal and informal rules
that order the social bond]{.underline}, the [arrangements that organize
**universalized commodity exchange** as the mode of socio-ecological
production and reproductio]{.underline}n. Of course, as implied above,
this is a symbolic representation of something that has neither unity
nor coherence and is utterly contingent.

The symbolic dimension of the Master Discourse that [**sustains the
dominant climate configuration** revolves around particular discursive
enchainment]{.underline}. The master signifier here is of course
'[Climate Change as a Problem']{.underline}, i.e. the [excessive
**accumulation of greenhouse gases** that drives global warming and
causes a significant problem to the present socio-ecological
configuration]{.underline}. Humans are co-responsible and action
requires a combination of individual adjustment and appropriate
[techno-managerial governance such that things can go on as
'normal';]{.underline} '**[sustainability']{.underline}** becomes the
central '[empty']{.underline} Master **[signifier]{.underline}**
(Swyngedouw Citation2007). As Ingolfur Blühdorn (Citation2013,
Citation2017) has repeatedly argued, [the dominant climate discourse and
practice **sustains the** **sustainability of
unsustainability**]{.underline}, referring to the process whereby the
[very socio-technical and governance **procedures mobilized** to
mitigate the problem in fact **sustain**]{.underline} the very process
that produces [**unsustainability**.]{.underline}

The [Imaginary]{.underline} part of the Discourse of the Master can be
identified as the view that [pluralist liberal]{.underline}
[democracy]{.underline}-cum-market-economy is the [only workable,
realistic, and quasi-naturalised social order]{.underline} -- one
without excess, remainder, or gap. The Imaginary is the **[terrain of
fantasy,]{.underline}** of [giving meaning and providing a sense of
coherence and wholeness.]{.underline} It is [constituted through
seductive and **alluring images** of **wholesomeness**]{.underline},
often providing an [illusionary sense]{.underline} of
**[unity]{.underline}** and **[transparency]{.underline}** (Galanopoulos
and Stavrakakis Citation2019, p. 10). It [offers a way through which
things and relations are seen and understood as common sense and
self-evident]{.underline}. The [fantasy]{.underline} screen that
[nurtures the imaginary]{.underline} [promises]{.underline} of the
[environmental]{.underline} Master Discourse [circles]{.underline}
around a [particular **fantasy of what 'Nature' is**]{.underline}
(Stavrakakis Citation1999, pp. 62--63), [articulated through **empty
signifiers** like sustainability,]{.underline} adaptation, [resilience,
socio-ecological inclusion and socio-ecological harmony]{.underline}
(Swyngedouw Citation2007). The [lure]{.underline} of this [**fantasy**
circles around **simultaneously portraying a catastrophic and horrifying
future**]{.underline} to be avoided and [promising the possibility of a
return to a lost but presumable wholesome world for both human and
non-human earthlings]{.underline}, a promise that can be realized within
the framework of the existing system. Of course, [this imaginary of a
**presumably lost arcadian** **environment** or climate that has to be
restored never really existed.]{.underline}

The [Imaginary invariably covers up or **conceals** the abyss between
the Real]{.underline} (of capital) and the [Symbolic]{.underline}.
**[Fantasy]{.underline}** becomes the mechanism through which the gap
that [marks their inconsistency is containe]{.underline}d (Žižek
Citation1997). We can [even consume ourselves out of unethical ways of
producing]{.underline} (by buying environmentally responsible and
ethically sourced products) [or of unsustainable ecological
practices]{.underline} (by offsetting our carbon footprint). Indeed, the
[climate]{.underline} Master [discourse]{.underline}
[displaces]{.underline} the realization of the [fantasy script onto
object a,]{.underline} the object-cause of desire, and this becomes
[crystallized]{.underline} [around a 'thing' -- in this case
**greenhouse gases**]{.underline} -- that is [staged as both the cause
of and the entity around which the realization of desire becomes
articulated]{.underline}. Since enjoyment is never fully attainable,
such a [fantasy]{.underline} screen permits us to [**displace the lack
of satisfaction** on to something or someone else who has 'it' and has
stolen it from us]{.underline} (like the migrants who steal our jobs,
CO2 that makes our planet heat up, elites that revel in pleasure on our
back, etc.) (Žižek Citation1993, p. 203). [**CO2** and other
**greenhouse gases** become as such the fetishized **foe** that requires
sequestration as the means to **achieve a fully harmonious
socio-climatic world** while **sustaining libidinal attachment to the
existing order.**]{.underline}

Indeed, the [**symbolic/imaginary** formation of a world is always
**incomplete**]{.underline}, [fails inherently to suture fully the
social order]{.underline}. This [remainder]{.underline}, the
**[Real]{.underline}**, i.e. that which cannot be symbolised by the
existing interplay of political forces, [disrupts and
destabilises]{.underline}. The Real is [both unaccounted for in the
symbolic order and excluded from the imaginary register.]{.underline} As
it escapes symbolisation, it can only be discerned in a slanted way.
[The Real becomes spectrally sensed through symptoms that both reveal
and hide the truth of the repressed.]{.underline}

For example, in the [climate context, the continuing growth of
greenhouse emissions]{.underline}, the obsession with [sustaining the
'**mad dance of accumulation'**]{.underline}, the [**deepening** of all
manner of **socio-ecological** **attachments**,]{.underline} and the
spectral presences of the [class relation that underpin global
capitalism are customarily negated]{.underline}. The place of the Real
is taken by, for example, [undocumented]{.underline}
[migrants]{.underline} for xeno-nationalist populists [or by carbon for
climate populists]{.underline} and this often figures as the excessive
torsion point, 'an alien intruder' (Stavrakakis Citation1999, p. 108),
which shatters the myth of unity. They are the supernumerary excess,
standing in the way of 'our' enjoyment. [In doing so, it interrupts the
symbolic order as that what cannot be symbolised without changing the
very co-ordinates of the symbolic order.]{.underline}

In the discursive cacophony around the climate conundrum, there are a
series of positions that nonetheless all circulate around and/or support
the Master narrative. The direct opposition to the Master discourse is
its disavowal, the radical denial of the existence of the Master, and
replacing it with an attempt to install another Master discourse that
nonetheless still sustains the Real of the situation. This is the
position of climate change deniers or minimizers. For them, there is not
really a problem; it is staged by a particular set of elites, sustained
by a particular scientific narrative in order to oppress the people and
undermine their enjoyment. This is the location of people like Donald
Trump or Jair Bolsonaro, is classically defined by Lacan as the
pervert's position, and is closely associated with 'mainstream'
right-wing populism (Gunn Citation2018).

3.2. [The **discourse of the university**: **change without change** and
the **repression of the real**]{.underline}

The [Discourse of the University refers not only to the symbolic
universe of knowledge and science, but also to the

Here we encounter the climate activists who suspect and distrust the
Master, and bombard those in power with demands to take seriously

**[further deepened through relentless questioning]{.underline}**,

**[fethishized 'thing called CO2']{.underline}** [rather than **changing

(Fletcher Citation2018). Such forms of **[climate

transforming the socio-ecological relations of production and

4\. Traversing the trauma and encircling the real, or ... . how not to
succumb to the populist temptation

In the Lacanian universe, the fourth discourse, the [Discourse of the
Analyst,]{.underline} revolves around **[nudging]{.underline}** the
[analysands to **interrogate** their **object-cause of**
**desire**]{.underline}. This [requires]{.underline} what Lacan names as
**['traversing the fantasy',]{.underline}** that is to [traverse the
imaginaries that elevate a thing to the object cause of desire (object
a):]{.underline}

The [analyst's **ethical commitment** is to **provoke** the **analyzed**
into a new relationship with their own desires such that they are **no
longer constrained by fantasy**.]{.underline} This point [is reached
when the analyzed traverses the fantasy and arrives at a state of
subjective destitution]{.underline}, where the subject renounces his or
her relationship with a fantasy that prevents "passing to the act" in
relation to desire (Healy Citation2010, p. 502).

Such [re-quilting of fantasy **undermines the hold 'the
thing'**]{.underline} [has as embodying the (impossible) **promise** of
**enjoyment** and displaces it to a different terrain]{.underline}, one
that does not any longer eschew 'the encounter with the Real', i.e.
[those processes and concerns that that have been repressed, disavowed
or foreclosed.]{.underline} It [rejects]{.underline} the Master
[discourse]{.underline} as just another [fantasy]{.underline} and
strives to displace the Master from its pedestal (Žižek Citation2000,
Zupančič Citation2000, Copjec Citation2002). In this section, we [shall
attempt to **encircle parts of the Real** that are repressed through the
lure of certain fantasies]{.underline}, thereby [contributing to
**re**-**orienting** desire around a different 'object-cause of
desire'.]{.underline} Encircling the Real of climate change implies,
among others, the [re-symbolisation of the imaginary upon which the
urgency of environmental action is legitimized and
sustained,]{.underline} while re-articulating the 'thing' around which
desire for a more benign environment in a socially inclusive world
circulates. [The hegemonic base upon which discourse and practice of
both mainstream and more activist climate movement]{.underline}s is
**[predicated rests upon repressed traumas]{.underline}**. Opening-up
different political-ecological trajectories requires transgressing the
fantasy that conceals these traumas.

#### Refuse any demands for solving climate change. It's already happened, and any attempt to fix it is just suturing the void. Only interrogation of the object-cause of desire can break the deadlock.

4.1. The catastrophe has already happened!

[implies that it is **not too late**,]{.underline} that the [projected

support for a process]{.underline} of **[environmental-populist

[presentation]{.underline} of [climate]{.underline} change as
[potentially catastrophic for all **produces a thoroughly depoliticized
imaginary**]{.underline}, [one that does]{.underline} not identify
adversaries in a political process [or **articulates** with **specific
political programs** of socio-ecological transformations.]{.underline}

[Transgressing this fantasy cuts through this deadlock]{.underline}. The
[revelatory promise of the apocalyptic narrative as well as the

behavioural and]{.underline} [**eco-techno-managerial**
change]{.underline} have to be **[rejected fully.]{.underline}** In face

life]{.underline}. Many [humans]{.underline} (and non-humans) [already
live in the post-apocalyptic]{.underline} [interstices]{.underline} of

and social conditions, [renders]{.underline} **[life
bare]{.underline}**. There is **[no Arcadian place,]{.underline}**
[time, or environment to return to, no benign socio-ecological

4.2. 'Humanity' cannot be saved

Second, the [consensual climate discourse insists on the imminent danger
climate change poses to the future of humanity]{.underline}.
[Humanity]{.underline} in this context is **[not just the sum total of
humans living]{.underline}** on Earth but [understood as human
civilization,]{.underline} characterised by a [range of
shared]{.underline} [beliefs]{.underline}, [ethics]{.underline}, and
[principles]{.underline} (such as liberty, solidarity, equality, and
civic rights). As Maurice Blanchot argued[, such view is]{.underline}
[predicated upon the **fantasy that 'humanity' actually
exists,**]{.underline} that there is [a global human
civilization]{.underline}, [one that deserves salvation]{.underline}.
However, the [Real of the human presence on Earth of course exposes the
**empty core of 'humanity'**]{.underline} (Blanchot Citation1971). The
[multiple tensions and conflicts]{.underline}, and the unspeakable
[violence]{.underline} [inflected by humans on humans testify to this
'**emptiness'**,]{.underline} despite occasional manifestations of a
deep humanity shared by some.

It is precisely this emptiness that is denied; it is a [repressed
trauma,]{.underline} [namely the disavowed]{.underline} [knowledge that
there is **no** **such thing as 'humanity'**]{.underline}. The
[pervasive inequalities]{.underline}, the [rampant]{.underline}
[uneven]{.underline} power [relations]{.underline}, the [incessant

[continuous]{.underline} [objective and subjective violence]{.underline}
inflicted by some humans onto other humans are symptoms of the
[radical]{.underline} antagonisms and [conflicts]{.underline} that [cut

**[communitarian 'humanity' has never existed]{.underline}**. It [may
never do unless a **sustained political fidelity** to]{.underline} [the
**possibility**, if not necessity, of its **making is
inaugurated**]{.underline}. The [disavowal]{.underline} in the [climate
discourse of]{.underline} the [barbarism that also characterizes
humanity is a classic form of traumatic repression]{.underline}.
According to Blanchot, the [fundamental challenge is the choice between
an **apocalyptic future**]{.underline} that speeds ahead precisely
because of the [absence of 'humanity' or the actual construction of a
'humanity' now that, in turn,]{.underline} would deflect the course of

Citation2018). [The issue is, therefore, **not to assure the future of a
non-existing humanity as** **we know** it, but first and foremost the
**creation of a humanity.**]{.underline}

#### Coming to terms with extinction as an occurring process and that there is no "humanity" is critical to mobilizing sufficient action, political or not. Their scenario planning good evidence doesn't matter -- we internal link turn. 

Erik **Swyngedouw** 7-15-20**22**. Erik Achille Marie Swyngedouw

Conclusion: 'The people' do not exist

This paper focused on the parallels between populism and a range of
environmental discourses and practices. Of course, there are significant
social movements and intellectual endeavours that cut through the
fantasies that underlie much of climate action. The slogan 'system
change not climate change' captures much of this and is part of a
widening scholarly and activist agenda (see, for example, Malm
Citation2018, Citation2021, Empson Citation2019, Kapoor Citation2020,
Beer Citation2020, Felli Citation2021). The focus of this contribution
was primarily on the phantasmic structures that sustain the dominant
discourse and practices, the parallels between populism and significant
parts of the environmental movement, on the lure that binds many people
to sustaining the status quo despite the precarity of the situation, and
on the subconscious mechanism that produce barriers to embracing a
politics articulated around system change.

Indeed, a [significant post-truth imaginary seeps into the dominant
climate discourse]{.underline}, a **[phantasmagoria]{.underline}** of an
[abstract and virtual]{.underline}, but nonetheless [threatened, global
humanity]{.underline}. In doing so, the [Real of class, capital, and
other antagonisms that **cut through the semblance of humanity** is
considered irrelevant or at least subordinate]{.underline}. [The
fetishistic repression, disavowal, or foreclosure of these
antagonisms]{.underline} that [form]{.underline} the [matrix of the
social assures]{.underline} that **[nothing will really
change]{.underline}**. [Traversing]{.underline} the [present
fantasy]{.underline} of a [just climate transition through
techno-managerial]{.underline} and (neo)[liberal]{.underline}
[consumerist adjustments]{.underline} requires **[recognizing the
trauma]{.underline}** of the [non-existence of humanity]{.underline} and
that it is this [non-existence that has already **caused the climate
catastrophe**]{.underline}. [Traversing this fantasy is]{.underline}
[predicated upon reversing the dominant argument: **recognizing that is
already too late**]{.underline} -- the [apocalypse has already
happened]{.underline} -- and the [only possible thing left to do is to
**engage in a process of** **constructing a real
'humanity'**]{.underline}, of [producing a human world in the
world.]{.underline} This necessitates **[foregrounding radical
politicization]{.underline}**. Or in other words, if we really want to
take the ecological condition seriously, [we have to displace the
question of ecology from a populist frame onto the terrain of agonistic
politicization,]{.underline} animated by a sustained fidelity to what
Alain Badiou calls a passion for the real possibility and necessity of
an egalitarian common world. It is through such political project that a
common and enabling climate might be constituted[. First and foremost,
we have to insist that **there is no alternative.**]{.underline}

### 2NC\-\--AT: Falsifiability 

#### Studies flow neg.

**Bargh 14**, John A. Bargh is a social psychologist currently working
at Yale University, where he has formed the Automaticity in Cognition,
Motivation, and Evaluation Laboratory., 2014, How Unconscious Thought
and Perception Affect Our Every Waking Moment, Scientific American,
<https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/how-unconscious-thought-and-perception-affect-our-every-waking-moment/>.
苗

[[Decision making]{.mark} often [occurs without people giving]{.mark}
much **[conscious thought]{.mark}** to how they vote, what they buy,
where they go on vacation or the way [they negotiate a myriad]{.mark} of
other [life]{.mark} choices.]{.underline}

[[**Unconscious**]{.mark} processes [underlie]{.mark} the [way
we]{.mark} deliberate and plan our [live]{.mark}s---and for good reason.
Automatic judgments, for one, are essential for dodging an oncoming car
or bus.]{.underline}

Behaviors governed by the unconscious go beyond looking both ways at the
corner. Embedded attitudes below the level of awareness shape many of
our attitudes toward others.

Sigmund Freud meditated on the meaning of the unconscious throughout his
career. These newer studies provide a more pragmatic perspective on how
we relate to a boss or spouse.

[When [psychologists]{.mark} try to understand the way our mind works,
they frequently come to a [conclusion]{.mark} that may seem startling:
[people]{.mark} often [make decisions without]{.mark} having given them
much [thought]{.mark}---or, more precisely, [before]{.mark} they have
[thought]{.mark} about them [consciously]{.mark}. When we decide how to
vote, what to buy, where to go on vacation and myriad other things,
unconscious thoughts that we are not even aware of typically play a big
role. [Research]{.mark} has [recently]{.mark} brought to light just how
[profoundly]{.mark} our [unconscious mind shapes]{.mark} our
[day-to-day]{.mark} interactions.]{.underline}

[One of the best-known studies to illustrate the power of the
unconscious focused on the process of deciding whether a candidate was
fit to hold public office.]{.underline} A group of mock voters were
given a split second to inspect portrait photographs from the Internet
of U.S. gubernatorial and senatorial candidates from states other than
where the voters lived. [Then, based on their fleeting glimpses of each
portrait, they were asked to judge the candidates.]{.underline}
Remarkably, the straw poll served as an accurate proxy for the later
choices of actual voters in those states. [Competency ratings based on
seeing the candidates\' faces for less time than it takes to blink an
eye predicted the outcome of two out of three elections.]{.underline}

For more than 100 years the role of unconscious influences on our
thoughts and actions has preoccupied scientists who study the mind.
Sigmund Freud\'s massive body of work emphasized the conscious as the
locus of rational thought and emotion and the unconscious as the lair of
the irrational, but contemporary cognitive psychologists have recast the
Freudian worldview into a less polarized psychological dynamic. Both
types of thought processes, it turns out, help us adapt to the protean
demands of a species that survives by marshaling the mental firepower to
hunt a Stone Age mastodon, face off in a Middle Ages joust or, in the
new millennium, sell Apple\'s stock short.

Post-Freudian psychology has set aside the id and ego for a more
pragmatic take on what defines our unconscious self. Nobel laureate
Daniel Kahneman has described the modern distinction between the
automatic and the controlled. In his best-selling book Thinking Fast and
Slow, Kahneman characterized automatic thought processes as fast,
efficient and typically outside the realm of conscious awareness, making
them devoid of deliberation or planning. They require only a simple
stimulus: the words on this page, for instance, connect effortlessly in
your mind with their meaning. Controlled processes are the opposite.
They require purposeful and relatively slow engagement of conscious
thought---picture the labored effort that goes into doing your tax
returns.

[Similar to [Freud\'s]{.mark} primal **[id]{.mark}** and controlling
**[ego]{.mark}**, the [automatic]{.mark} and [controlled systems]{.mark}
complement each other yet also, [at times, conflict]{.mark}. You need to
react without reflection to dodge an oncoming bus but also need to check
yourself from throwing a punch at the reckless bus driver.]{.underline}

Snap judgments---relatively automatic thought processes---abound in our
daily life---and for good reason. [[Outside]{.mark} of the relatively
[small number of individuals]{.mark} any one of us knows really well,
most people [we interact]{.mark} with are strangers we might never see
again---while standing in line at the bank, say---or others we come
across in the course of their jobs---cashiers, taxi drivers, waiters,
insurance agents, teachers, and so on. The [**default** **unconscious
perception** generates expectations]{.mark} about behavior and
personalities based on minimal information.]{.underline} We expect
waitresses to act a certain way, which is different from what we expect
of librarians or truck drivers. These expectations come to us
immediately and without our thinking about them, based only on a
person\'s social place.

[The [**unconscious** way we perceive people]{.mark} during the course
of the day is a **[reflexive reaction]{.mark}**. [We]{.mark} [must exert
willful, conscious effort]{.mark} to put aside the unexplained and
sometimes unwarranted negative feelings that we may harbor toward
others. [The stronger the unconscious influence]{.mark}, the
[harder]{.mark} [we]{.mark} have to [work]{.mark} consciously to
[overcome]{.mark} it.]{.underline} In particular, this holds true for
habitual behaviors. An alcoholic might come home in the evening and pour
a drink; a person with a weight problem might reach for the potato
chips---both easily casting aside the countervailing urge toward
restraint.

[[Understanding]{.mark} the [tug]{.mark} the [unconscious]{.mark} exerts
on us is [essential]{.mark} so that we do not become overwhelmed by
[impulses]{.mark} that are [hard to]{.mark} understand and
[control]{.mark}.]{.underline} The ability to regulate our own
behavior---whether making friends, getting up to speed at a new job or
overcoming a drinking problem---depends on more than genes, temperament
and social support networks. It also hinges, in no small measure, on our
capacity to identify and try to overcome the automatic impulses and
emotions that influence every aspect of our waking life. [[To make our
way in the world, **we need to learn to come to terms with our
unconscious self.**]{.underline}]{.mark}

#### Substantial empirical support for psychoanalytic theory. It's just rebranded now.

Mark Leonard **Solms** 12-3-20**18**. Mark Solms is a South African
psychoanalyst and neuropsychologist, who is known for his discovery of
the brain mechanisms of dreaming. \" The Neurobiological Underpinnings
of Psychoanalytic Theory and Therapy,\" Frontiers, Front. Behav.
Neurosci., Sec. Pathological Conditions, Volume 12,
https://doi.org/10.3389/fnbeh.2018.00294.

My third and final argument is that **[psychoanalytic
therapy]{.underline}** achieves **[good outcomes]{.underline}**---at
[least as good as, and in some respects better than, other
evidence-based treatments in psychiatry today]{.underline} (see Shedler,
2015). This argument unfolds over four stages:

\(a\) [Psychotherapy]{.underline} in general is a [**highly effective**
form of treatment]{.underline}. **[Meta-analyses]{.underline}** of
[psychotherapy]{.underline} outcome studies typically
[reveal]{.underline} [effect]{.underline} sizes of between
**[0.73]{.underline}** and **[0.85]{.underline}**. (An effect size of
1.0 means that the average treated patient is one standard deviation
healthier than the average untreated patient). An [effect size of 0.8 is
considered a large effect]{.underline} in psychiatric research, 0[.5 is
considered moderate]{.underline}, and 0.2 is considered small. To put
the [efficacy of psychotherapy into perspective]{.underline}, recent
antidepressant medications achieve effect sizes of between 0.24
(tricyclics) and 0.31 (SSRIs).24 The [changes]{.underline}
[brought]{.underline} [about by psychotherapy]{.underline}, no less than
drug therapy, are of course [visualizable with brain
imaging]{.underline} (see Beauregard, 2014).

\(b\) **[Psychoanalytic psychotherapy]{.underline}** is **[equally
effective]{.underline}** as [other forms of psychotherapy]{.underline}
(e.g., **[CBT]{.underline}**). This has recently been
[demonstrated]{.underline} conclusively by **[comparative
meta-analysis]{.underline}** (Steinert et al., 2017). However, there is
[evidence to suggest that the effects last longer]{.underline}---and
even **[increase]{.underline}**---[after]{.underline} the end of the
[treatment]{.underline}. (Shedler, 2010) [**authoritative review** of
all randomized control trials]{.underline} to date reported [effect
sizes of between **0.78 and 1.46,**]{.underline} even for diluted and
truncated forms of psychoanalytic therapy.25 An especially
[methodologically rigorous meta-analysis]{.underline} (Abbass et al.,
2006) [yielded]{.underline} an [overall effect size of 0.97
for]{.underline} [general symptom improvement with **psychoanalytic
therapy**]{.underline}. The effect size increased to 1.51 when the
patients were assessed at follow-up. A [more recent
meta-analysis]{.underline} by Abbass et al. (2014) [yielded an overall
effect size of 0.71 and the finding of maintained and increased effects
at follow-up was **reconfirmed**.]{.underline}

This was for [short-term psychoanalytic treatment]{.underline}.
According to the meta-analysis of de Maat et al. (2009), which was less
methodologically rigorous than the Abbass studies, [longer-term
psychoanalytic]{.underline} [psychotherapy yields an effect size of 0.78
at termination and 0.94 at follow-up,]{.underline} and
**[psychoanalysis]{.underline}** **[proper]{.underline}** achieves a
mean [effect size of **0.87 at termination**]{.underline} and **[1.18 at
follow-up]{.underline}**. This is the [overall]{.underline}
[effect]{.underline}; the effect size that she found for symptom
improvement (as opposed to personality change) at termination was 1.03
for long-term therapy, and for **[psychoanalysis it was
1.38.]{.underline}** A [subsequent study]{.underline} by
Leuzinger-Bohleber et al. (2018, in press) [shows even bigger effect
sizes:]{.underline} between [1.62 and 1.89]{.underline} after 3 years of
treatment. These are **[enormous effects]{.underline}**. Follow-up data
are of course not yet available from this ongoing study. The [consistent
trend toward larger effect sizes at follow-u]{.underline}p (where the
effects of other forms of psychotherapy, like CBT, tend to decay)
[suggests that **psychoanalytic therapy** sets in motion processes of
change that **continue even after therapy has ended**]{.underline} (cf.
"working through," discussed above). This is called the **["sleeper
effect."]{.underline}**

It is important to recognize that these findings concern symptom
improvement only. Psychoanalytic treatments are not directed primarily
at symptomatic relief but rather at what might be called personality
change. [Not surprisingly, therefore, psychoanalytic treatments achieve
much better results than other treatments on this outcome
measure]{.underline}. In Leuzinger et al.\'s ongoing study, for example,
[almost **twice as many patients** receiving psychoanalytic treatment
vs. CBT reached their criteria for "structural change"]{.underline}
after 3 years (60 vs. 36%).

\(c\) The [therapeutic techniques]{.underline} that
[predict]{.underline} best treatment [outcomes]{.underline} make [good
sense]{.underline} in relation to the [psychodynamic
mechanisms]{.underline} outlined above. These techniques are (Blagys and
Hilsenroth, 2000):

• unstructured, open-ended dialogue between patient and therapist.

• identifying recurring themes in the patient\'s experience.

• linking the patient\'s feelings and perceptions to past experiences.

• drawing attention to feelings regarded by the patient as unacceptable.

• pointing out ways in which the patient avoids feelings.

• focusing on the here-and-now therapy relationship.

• drawing connections between the therapy relationship and other
relationships.

It is highly instructive to note that these [techniques lead to the best
treatment outcomes]{.underline}, [regardless]{.underline} of the
**["brand"]{.underline}** of therapy the clinician espouses. In other
words, [these same techniques]{.underline} (or at least a subset of
them; see Hayes et al., 1996) [**predict** optimal treatment **outcomes
in CBT too**, even if the therapist believes they are doing something
else.]{.underline}

\(d\) It is therefore perhaps [not surprising that
psychotherapists]{.underline}, irrespective of their stated theoretical
orientation, [tend to choose psychoanalytic psychotherapy for
themselves]{.underline} (Norcross, 2005)!

#### The unconscious drive automates action before thought. 

Mark Leonard **Solms** 12-3-20**18**. Mark Solms is a South African
psychoanalyst and neuropsychologist, who is known for his discovery of
the brain mechanisms of dreaming. \" The Neurobiological Underpinnings
of Psychoanalytic Theory and Therapy,\" Frontiers, Front. Behav.
Neurosci., Sec. Pathological Conditions, Volume 12,
https://doi.org/10.3389/fnbeh.2018.00294.

Most of our [predictions are executed **unconsciously**]{.underline}. As
we saw above, [cognitive consciousness]{.underline} (short-term "working
memory") is an [extremely limited resource]{.underline}, so there is
[enormous pressure to consolidate]{.underline} our [solutions to life\'s
problems]{.underline} into long-term memory, and then [ultimately to
**automatize** them. **Innate**]{.underline}
**[predictions]{.underline}**---of the kind discussed above18--are
**[effected automatically from the outset]{.underline}**, as are those
acquired in the first 2--3 years of life, before the preconscious
("declarative") memory systems mature (cf. infantile amnesia, which
applies only to episodic and semantic memory). [Multiple
unconscious]{.underline} (non-declarative) [memory systems]{.underline}
[exist]{.underline}, but the ones that are [most relevant to
psychopathology]{.underline} are "[procedural" and "emotional"
memory]{.underline}, which operate according to different rules. These
[stereotyped]{.underline} systems [bypass thinking]{.underline} (cf.
Freud\'s **["repetition compulsion")]{.underline}** and
[define]{.underline} the mode of [functioning of the system
unconscious]{.underline} (see below).

The [ultimate aim of learning is to permanently solve our
problems]{.underline} (i.e., to learn how to meet our needs in the world
reliably). To the extent that this goal is achieved, [preconscious
predictions]{.underline} are [iteratively]{.underline}
[consolidated]{.underline} and **[reconsolidated ever more
deeply]{.underline}**. The [consolidation]{.underline} of such
[automatized predictions]{.underline} centrally [involves]{.underline}
[transferring]{.underline} them from [cortical to subcortical memory
systems]{.underline} (principally but not exclusively located in the
basal ganglia and cerebellum). The crucial thing to note about these
latter systems is that they [entail non-representational]{.underline}
(sometimes called "model free") [action programmes.]{.underline} Here I
am using the term "representation" in the sense in which I used it
above---namely to refer to images. That is why non-declarative memories
simply cannot be retrieved into working memory; they are non-thinkable
executive programmes.

[All]{.underline} of this [implies]{.underline} that [truly
unconscious]{.underline} (as opposed to preconscious)
[memories]{.underline} are [**not subject to updating** in working
memory.]{.underline} This is of crucial importance. They are,
[therefore]{.underline}, in a [sense, **indelible** (LeDoux, 1995). But
they are also highly **efficient**.]{.underline} LeDoux (1995) calls
them "quick and dirty." This is the neural basis of what Freud (1911)
called [the "primary process."]{.underline} Via these circuits,
[stimulus X simply triggers]{.underline} [response Y,]{.underline} with
nothing in between (no delay, no thinking, no "secondary process").19

This does not mean that non-declarative memories are not subject to
reconsolidation. What it means is that they are not subject to
reconsolidation via thinking (via conscious cognition, via working
memory); they are only subject to reconsolidation through action.
Non[-declarative memories can only be activated (and
thereby]{.underline} consolidated/reconsolidated) [through **embodied
enactment**.]{.underline}

Of course, not all automatized memories start out as declarative
memories. The [multiple memory systems]{.underline} [operate both
successively and simultaneously]{.underline}. Some (especially emotional
memories, which arise from purely subcortical associations) are
therefore automatized from the outset. This [applies]{.underline} also
to [innate]{.underline} emotional [predictions]{.underline}. (Instinct
is just another word for innate predictions). Instinctual executive
programmes are all subcortical. But---as we have seen above---they need
to be supplemented by learning. [Fear conditioning is an excellent
example.]{.underline} Here we speak of "single-exposure learning;" e.g.,
we cannot afford to learn twice what happens when we stick our fingers
into an electrical socket.

Learning in each of the different instinctual-emotional systems follows
somewhat different rules. For example, early sexual experiences, as with
fear conditioning, appear to entail single-exposure learning and to
leave indelible impressions. Attachment bonds, by contrast, are
established slowly during the first 6 months of life, but they become
extremely difficult to change after that (cf. the difference between
acute "protest" and chronic "despair" with experiences of separation and
loss).

[Procedural memories]{.underline}, similarly, are "[hard to learn and
hard to forget."]{.underline} What these two non-declarative memory
systems have in common is that they by-pass thinking. But this does not
mean that they by-pass affective consciousness. [Just because we cannot
"declare" our automatized predictions does not mean we cannot feel their
causes and their consequences]{.underline}. (The conflation of
consciousness with conscious cognition---i.e., excluding affect---has
often led cognitive science astray).

Now we come to the heart of the matter. I have [localized **Freud\'s**
system "**preconscious**"]{.underline} in the cortex and his system
"[unconscious]{.underline}" in the [non-declarative memory
systems]{.underline} located beneath the cortex, primarily in the basal
ganglia, and cerebellum (Solms, 2017b). But the [unconscious memory
systems I have just]{.underline} [described]{.underline} are
[conventionally called "the cognitive unconscious]{.underline}," which
is contrasted with "the dynamic unconscious."
[Psychoanalysts]{.underline} [acknowledge]{.underline} the existence of
a [cognitive unconscious]{.underline} (they call it the "unconscious
ego") but they [point out]{.underline} that it [excludes the dynamic
processes]{.underline} that [Freud discovered]{.underline} (which they
call the "**[repressed]{.underline}**").

Freud thought the repressed unconscious was part of the id. This was one
of his biggest mistakes, as I discussed above. [I do not mean that the
repressed unconscious does not exist.]{.underline} I mean only that [the
system **unconscious** and the **id** are **two different things**,
located in **two different parts of the brain.**]{.underline}

The [repressed]{.underline} is [derived from cognitive
(representational) processes]{.underline}, from learning, whereas the
[id]{.underline} consists in **[affective]{.underline}**
(non-representational) **[processes]{.underline}**, and it is
[innate]{.underline}. The [parts of the brain that perform]{.underline}
the [functions]{.underline} which Freud called "id" are [located mainly
in the upper brainstem and limbic system]{.underline}; whereas the
[parts]{.underline} that perform the functions he attributed to "the
[repressed]{.underline}" (or the "system unconscious") are located
[mainly]{.underline} in the **[basal ganglia and
cerebellum]{.underline}** (There are, of course, multiple interactions
between these systems. For example: the amygdala and nucleus accumbens
straddle the tail and head of the caudate nucleus, respectively; and the
basal ganglia, in turn, interact constantly with the prefrontal lobes).

In my opinion the [difference between the cognitive and the dynamic
unconscious is simply this]{.underline}. The [cognitive]{.underline}
[unconscious]{.underline} consists in predictions that are
**[legitimately automatized]{.underline}**. That is, they are deeply
automatized because they work so well; they reliably meet the underlying
needs that they are aimed at. The **[repressed]{.underline}**, by
contrast, is **[illegitimately]{.underline}** (or prematurely)
**[automatized]{.underline}**. Illegitimate automatization [occurs when
the ego is overwhelmed by its problems]{.underline}; that is, [**when it
cannot** work out how to **satisfy id** **demands** in the world. This
happens a lot in childhood, when the ego is feeble.]{.underline}

The infamous Oedipus complex provides an excellent example of an
insoluble problem: it is an almost-inevitable constellation of
compulsive (innate) emotional needs, arising simultaneously, which are
beyond the reach of the child, and irreconcilable with each other.
("Conflict" is just another word for "insoluble problem"). In such
situations, the child has no other choice but to cut its losses. It is
doomed either (1) to obsess endlessly over a problem that it cannot
solve, thereby wasting precious working memory resources which could be
more usefully deployed for problems that it can solve---such as how to
read, write, and calculate---or (2) to make the best of a bad job and
automatize the least-bad childish prediction it can come up with, even
though it does not work so well.20

**[Repression]{.underline}** (through the adjustment of precision
weighting) has the [inevitable]{.underline} [implication]{.underline}
that a deeply [automatized prediction]{.underline} does not manage the
feelings it is aimed at, but [there is nothing the subject can
do]{.underline} [about this]{.underline}; since the [essence of
repression]{.underline} [consists]{.underline} in the fact that the
[prediction is treated]{.underline} as if it does work well, and it is
**[therefore immune to reconsolidation]{.underline}**.21 The [resultant
prediction error]{.underline} is the [constant]{.underline}
[pressure]{.underline} that Freud theorized as the threat of "the return
of the repressed" (This, in turn, leads to secondary defenses---i.e., to
what Freud called "after-pressure").

Where I differ from Freud in this regard is that I do not believe the
repressed ever returns; it is only the affect (which it fails to
regulate) that returns. How many patients actually remember their
Oedipal strivings, even in psychoanalysis, for example? [This is because
non-declarative memories are just that: non-declarative.]{.underline}
Non-declarative memories are **[purely associative]{.underline}** (and
permanently unconscious) action tendencies of the kind described by
LeDoux, as with Pavlov\'s dogs. No thinking occurs, not even implicitly.
This has major implications for how psychoanalytic treatment works.

Before moving on to that topic (of how this type of treatment works), I
want to restate the concluding point of this section, because it is of
utmost importance: [Not only successful predictions are
automatized.]{.underline} With this simple observation, we have overcome
the unfortunate distinction between the "cognitive" and "dynamic"
unconscious. Sometimes a child has to make the best of a bad job in
order to focus on the problems that it can solve. Such
[illegitimately]{.underline} or [prematurely automatized
predictions]{.underline} (i.e., wishes as opposed to realistic
solutions) are **[called "the repressed."]{.underline}** Normally, in
order for predictions to be updated in light of experience, they need to
be reconsolidated; that is, they need to enter consciousness again, in
order for the long-term traces to become labile once more. [This is
impossible to achieve for repressed predictions, because the essential
mechanism of repression entails immunity from (declarative)
reconsolidation, despite prediction errors.]{.underline}

When considering [systematic case studies in the broader field of

The [range of diagnoses, short- and long-term therapies, and successful

is throwing away the baby with the bathwater. While critiques on the
anecdotal nature of clinical case studies may be apt, discarding a
clinical or qualitative description altogether is to disown the essence
and the strength of the psychoanalytic single case study. [Moreover,
with respect to clinical relevance and to possible meta-studies, this
**clinical contextual information is quintessential.**]{.underline}

Concerning [ethical considerations]{.underline} of research in such
[delicate circumstances as the psychotherapeutic]{.underline} setting,
it [appears that even more attention could be paid to informed
consent.]{.underline} While informed consent was mentioned much more
than was found in the overall archive (Desmet et al., 2013) -- where it
was mentioned in only 9% of cases -- still more than half of the studies
did not provide information about informed consent despite their

Surely, our review has certain limitations. Using other search criteria
for example, might result in other cases and probably more empirical

**representative and large** **sample** of systematic case studies could

We [conclude that psychoanalytic **empirical case**
**studies**]{.underline}, although they [were adopted

are of **[increasing and]{.underline}** **[high]{.underline}**
**[quality]{.underline}**. Moreover, [journals currently provide
more]{.underline} [clear guidelines]{.underline} as to what comprises an

et]{.underline} al. (2014), **[Mauck and Moore]{.underline}** (2014) and
**[Cornelis]{.underline}** et al. (2017), set [the tone for a future
where]{.underline} case [aggregation based on scientifically sound cases
that include triangulated data and analysis methods while not

Psychotherapy is and always will consist of a unique encounter between

happens in this process is crucial for the advancement of the

#### Without confronting the Real, all climate policy is doomed to failure. 

Lucas **Pohl** **and** Erik **Swyngedouw** 03-20**23**. Lucas Pohl
(Dr.), born in 1989, is a postdoctoral researcher at the Geography
Department of the Humboldt University Berlin. He works on the
interstices between human geography, philosophy, and psychoanalysis with
a focus on spatial theory, built environments, and political action.
Erik Achille Marie Swyngedouw (pronounced \[ˈeːrɪk ˈsʋɪŋɣədʌu\]) is
professor of geography at the University of Manchester in the School of
Environment, Education and Development and a member of the Manchester
Urban Institute. \"Enjoying climate change: Jouissance as a political
factor,\" Political Geography, Volume 101, March 2023,
https://doi.org/10.1016/j.polgeo.2022.102820.

5\. Towards a [**real enjoyment** of climate change]{.underline}

So far, we engaged with enjoyment as a political factor that sustains
the 'deadlock' of the climate situation today. [Enjoyment]{.underline}
is structured both by transgression and excess, [animated by an
incessant]{.underline} [quest for full satisfaction, to become whole or
One]{.underline} [again]{.underline}, and this [operates both at the
