### 2NC\-\--FW

#### Framework. The role of the judge is to evaluate the 1AC as a topological reflexivity. 

Antti **Saari** 1-20-20**21**. Antti Saari is the leader of POISED
research group and an associate professor (tenure track) in the Tampere
University Faculty of Education and Culture. \"Topologies of desire:
Fantasies and their symptoms in educational policy futures,\" European
Education Research Journal, Volume 21, Issue 6,
https://doi.org/10.1177/1474904120988389. 苗

Introduction: [topological reflexivity]{.underline}

In their influential overview of the topic, Lury et al. (2012) refer to
**[topology]{.underline}** as a means of describing [contemporary
cultural dynamics]{.underline}. They argue that culture is '[becoming
topological' in so far as]{.underline} [capital]{.underline}, technology
and [information alike can be detached from their cultural and
institutional surroundings]{.underline} and mobilised in a 'space of
flows'. All that used to be distant can now be brought close, all that
was once incompatible and heterogeneous in nature can now be brought
onto a plane of comparison. [Topology]{.underline} refers here, [not
just to a set of cultural and **governmental practices** and
**discourses**]{.underline}, but also to a **[mode of
reflexivity]{.underline}** -- a tendency to think about and a[ct with
regard to **policies** and **technological systems** in society in terms
of topological configurations]{.underline} (Hayles, 2016; Parisi, 2019).
This topological reflexivity is reflected in both the well as the more
sensuous, [abstract cognitive modelling of spaces as visceral and
affective registers in which we feel and sense the world about
us]{.underline} (Lury et al., 2012: 28). The idea of friction-free
mobility and communication has the potential to arouse excitement, and
organising space by [removing]{.underline} the [barriers]{.underline} of
work may be thought to [unravel affective powers]{.underline} of
creativity and innovation.

In the reflexive mode, [topological discourses are at once descriptive
and prescript]{.underline}ive. On the one hand, they anticipate the
allegedly central role of innovations and new technologies globally; on
the other, they produce norms as to how nations and organisations should
adapt to these allegedly inevitable developments. '[Lists', 'models',
'networks', 'clouds' and 'flows' are concepts often used to serve this
double role of the is and the ought]{.underline} (Lury et al., 2012: 4).
This can be discerned, for example, in the claims that the global flow
of labour and [capital is transforming European societies to the extent
that they must not only accommodate this flow, but also increase it to
sustain economic competitiveness in the global market.]{.underline}

This millennium, educational policy studies have also been placing
increasing emphasis on the importance of [policy practices]{.underline}
and structures that show a [topological interest in establishing novel
criteria for distance]{.underline}, [continuity and
commensurability]{.underline} (Saari, 2012; Sellar, 2015; Hartong,
2018). Although studies may not always actually use the term 'topology',
attention has been drawn to the ways in which large educational policy
actors, such as the EU, create [policy spaces that transcend cultural
and national distances by **standardising concepts** and
degrees]{.underline} (Coulby, 2002; Hartong, 2018; Lawn and Grek, 2012),
and through [construction of digital education and **policy
environments**]{.underline} (Hartong and Piattoeva, 2019; Landri, 2018).

An emerging body of literature has also begun to [study the spatial
aspects of education policy in tandem with certain **affective** and
**emotional registers**]{.underline} -- analysing how [topological
discourses may also]{.underline} incite, seduce and
**[provoke]{.underline}**; not just dictate or inform (e.g. Sellar,
2015; Webb and Gulson, 2012: 92; Webb et al., 2020). Topological
instruments such as academic rankings and league tables can elicit
anticipatory feelings of pride, anxiety or shame among students and
staff in educational institutions, which in turn [guide]{.underline}
their [expectations as well as individual and institutional
strategies]{.underline} (Espeland and Sauder, 2016; Piattoeva and Saari,
2020). When it works, [governing these anticipatory affects will
reinforce the positive effects of liberal governance]{.underline}
[without]{.underline} the need for **[open intervention]{.underline}**,
**[commands]{.underline}** or **[forbidding restrictions]{.underline}**
(Brøgger and Staunaes, 2016; Sellar, 2015).

[Topological reflexivity]{.underline} is what also [governs policy
futures]{.underline} in education. It would [explain]{.underline}
'[policy prolepsis']{.underline} (Webb and Gulson, 2012) and
'**[anticipatory governance']{.underline}** (Webb et al., 2020[) -- ways
of **managing** **expectations** so that they **prescribe the policies
then enacted**. **Topological discourses**]{.underline} can be [used to
judge or evaluate the present and to decide on required
intervention]{.underline}. Within this governmental configuration, the
[educational]{.underline} [present]{.underline} appears as a [state of
affairs in need of **reform or planning**]{.underline}, that is, a type
of [**intervention** that is **determined** by a **desired future and/or
past**]{.underline}. (Decuypere and Simons, 2020: 5)

In effect, [**topological discourses** can thus be **self-fulfilling
predictions**]{.underline}: they [cultivate anticipatory
affects]{.underline} [and **emotions** that incite **an attunement to
change**]{.underline} (e.g. the inevitable global trend of
digitalisation), [which in turn may fuel the realisation of these
**future visions**.]{.underline}

In this article, I follow Lury et al.'s (2012) approach to
[investigate]{.underline} this [topologisation]{.underline} as a [mode
of cognitive]{.underline} and [affective reflexivity]{.underline} in
educational [discourses]{.underline}. In particular, I look at how [the
topological aspects]{.underline} of [envisioning and enacting
**anticipated futures**]{.underline} in education can be [used as an
overarching narrative]{.underline} framework to describe the transition
from earlier rigid learning environments to new flexible ones. As a case
in point, I focus on Finnish policy discourses that focus on new spatial
innovations as drivers of sweeping change in comprehensive schools (on
pedagogical, architectural and technological levels). These discourses
govern emotions and affects by promising that fulfilment and elated
experiences of creativity and play at school can be achieved through the
careful design of learning environments and school architecture.

I address the [allure of topological future discourses by looking at
them from the perspective of the **government of desire**]{.underline}.
Drawing on the [Lacanian psychoanalytic tradition]{.underline} of
critically analysing political discourse, this should provide a novel
approach to the [dynamics of topological reflexivity.]{.underline} I
[unpack]{.underline} two [prominent aspects of go**verning desires** by
first asking]{.underline} how it is that a [topological
discourse]{.underline} can **[organise]{.underline}**
**[fantasies]{.underline}** that incite and **[sustain
desire]{.underline}** [in a ceaseless quest to address what is
**lacking** in a subject's existence. Fantasy]{.underline} here is
understood as a [fundamental way]{.underline} to [make sense of social
reality:]{.underline} it provides the [coordinates for
desire]{.underline} by presenting something not previously experienced
as being desirable because it promises fullness, [meaning and
**enjoyment in the future**]{.underline}. I describe how these
[fantasies are used in]{.underline} [topological discourse to spur
people into taking action that they believe will eventually eradicate
spatial divisions and boundaries]{.underline} in education, while
enabling experiences of pleasure in schoolwork for pupils and teachers
alike.

The second aspect is to look at the most prominent inconsistencies in
[these topological discourses and explore how they are dealt
with,]{.underline} as [**fantasies** will invariably contain **certain
incompatible claims**,]{.underline} and aporias that **[preclude
consistency]{.underline}**. While [**topological** discourses posit an
**object of desire**,]{.underline} with its promise of enjoyment and
fulfilment in the future, [they are at the same time caught in a
disavowed compulsion to keep the same object at a distance.]{.underline}
Moreover, while such discourses are founded on a subjective autonomy and
freedom to develop learning environments, they also involve an implicit
injunction for teachers and schools to implement new learning
environments and take pleasure in doing so. Such symptoms of fantasy
lead to discursive strategies of disavowal and othering.

#### TAG. 

Antti **Saari** 1-20-20**21**. Antti Saari is the leader of POISED
research group and an associate professor (tenure track) in the Tampere
University Faculty of Education and Culture. \"Topologies of desire:
Fantasies and their symptoms in educational policy futures,\" European
Education Research Journal, Volume 21, Issue 6,
https://doi.org/10.1177/1474904120988389. 苗

Conclusions

Coming back to the existing research on topological reflexivity
mentioned in the introduction to this article, I will conclude with some
theoretical reflections on the value of a Lacanian approach to the
topic, and argue that an analysis of the [fantasmatic organisation of
desire adds important analytical insights to how space and time are
enacted in]{.underline} educational policy [discourses.]{.underline}

Prominent Deleuzian and [new-materialist approaches]{.underline} to
governing education in sociomaterial networks and assemblages have
[recently problematised the naturalised forms of (Euclidean) space and
(chronological) time as being 'already there' and 'given']{.underline},
and have instead focused on the multiple ways of enacting (rather than
representing) national, institutional and individual boundaries, strata
and rhythms in education (Decuypere and Simons, 2016; Webb and Gulson,
2012). This [approach]{.underline} has also [allowed]{.underline} the
[emergent conduits of power and knowledge]{.underline} in education to
be [traced in their digitalised, mediatised and datafied
forms]{.underline} (see e.g. Thompson and Cook, 2015).

For instance, following [Deleuze's]{.underline} (1995) notion of the
[emergence of control societies]{.underline}, several studies have shown
how the [entangled nature]{.underline} of contemporary [education
spaces]{.underline} -- transnational policy arenas, virtual learning
environments, and classrooms (Piro, 2008; Thompson and Cook, 2014;
Watson 2010) -- are [founded on a 'modular' logic of
governance]{.underline}. By [not confining governance to
educational]{.underline} or [workplace institutions]{.underline},
modular logic [involves ever-shifting assemblages of
technology]{.underline}, [materials]{.underline} and
[discourses]{.underline}, as well as [ways of thinking and
feeling]{.underline}. These elements [constantly reconfigure
proximity,]{.underline} [differences and mobility]{.underline}, while
simultaneously [ensuring carefully calibrated modes of
individual]{.underline} [freedom]{.underline}. Today, such modular logic
takes place in education, for instance, in embedding traditional
classrooms into malleable learning ecosystems or digital learning
platforms (Webb et al., 2020).

What a [Lacanian]{.underline} approach can [add to these analyses is a
means for explaining the paradoxical, self-conflicting and disavowing
characteristics of governance enacted in such space-times]{.underline}.
Primarily this stems from the focus on the doubling of a material object
or space. As noted above, there is always a [necessary distance between
**fantasmatic and 'real' space-times**;]{.underline} what [motivates
subjects]{.underline} are not the real [spaces or objects
themselves,]{.underline} but the [ephemeral objects of
desire]{.underline} which [can never be]{.underline} actually
[attained.]{.underline}

This focus enables a particular explanation of the anticipatory or
proleptic nature of policies. As mentioned above, [topological
discourses]{.underline} are **[fuelled]{.underline}** [by a desire for
future fulfilment in the form of]{.underline} [uninhibited
communication]{.underline}, creativity and playfulness -- both in
education and at work. This often [takes the form of overcoming
**various obstacles to enjoyment**]{.underline} -- such as the
**[boundaries]{.underline}** between branches of an organisation,
[between school subjects]{.underline}, between different
company--customer interfaces, or [**between education and 'real
life'**.]{.underline} What is paradoxical here is that, in order to
harness this desire, these discourses invariably require the existence
of those very boundaries that they claim to be abolishing.

Furthermore, the characteristic of space-times as fantasmatic objects
may explain the endless repetition and somewhat 'hysterical' (Clarke,
2019) demands inherent in topological discourses; hysterical insofar as
they incessantly seek to achieve what can never be attained to begin
with, and that is why no amount of digital technology, partitions or
furniture can ever satisfy these demands. The inevitable failure of
these efforts then fuels anticipation of seemingly new ways to overcome
the obstacles to enjoyment. So, in spite of evident changes in material
spaces, at the discursive level, topological reflexivity is caught in a
timeless moment of spinning wheels.

Finally, it is often reiterated in critical education policy studies
that governmental discourses in control societies consistently emphasise
the freedom of those being governed. [This means that **subjects** must
be **lured** into **implementing certain changes** in the name of their
personal freedom, fulfilment and well-being.]{.underline} Yet it is also
[reminded that this form of governance is never totalising]{.underline}
in the sense of [subduing all opposition and]{.underline}
[dissent]{.underline} -- its [**topological assemblages** always **leave
room for evasions** and forms of resistance.]{.underline} What a
[Lacanian approach can add here is that it aids in locating the tension
between domination and resistance at the stage of desire]{.underline};
subjects must be [enticed into desiring certain environments as the
gateway to their enjoyment.]{.underline} It also shows how [political
fantasies grapple with those naysayers by othering them]{.underline} --
not necessarily for hosting false beliefs or violating explicit norms --
but for [refusing to desire in the right way.]{.underline}

## K\-\--Spatiality

#### Engagement within the Arctic Council reinforces geo-epistemological separation between Arctic and non-Arctic, leading to marginalization. 

Eda **Ayaydin** 7-15-20**25**. Eda Ayaydin is a Teaching Fellow at the
University of London Institute in Paris & Research Fellow at the
Malaurie Institute of Arctic Research. \"One Must Imagine Sisyphus
Happy: Geo-Epistemology in the Arctic,\" Arctic Institute - Center for
Circumpolar Security Studies,
https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/one-must-imagine-sisyphus-happy-geo-epistemology-arctic/.
苗

In The Myth of Sisyphus,1) Albert Camus presents Sisyphus as a metaphor
for the futility of repetitive actions, endlessly rolling a boulder
uphill. [This paper uses the Sisyphus metaphor to highlight the
cyclical, often unchallenged assumptions in International
Relations]{.underline} (IR), especially regarding how [knowledge is
produced, transmitted, and legitimized]{.underline}. Focusing **[on
geo-epistemology]{.underline}** in the Arctic, the [paper critically
examines the **symbolic and discursive** **dichotomies**]{.underline}
[embedded]{.underline} in the [Arctic Council's process for assessing
non-Arctic states' observer applications]{.underline}. Drawing from
constructivist IR and epistemological critique, this [analysis exposes
the persistence of **Western**-**centric modes of validation** in Arctic
governan]{.underline}ce. The ambiguity surrounding what constitutes
"relevant knowledge" or "expertise" remains unresolved, despite a
growing body of literature on the Arctic Council's observer criteria. By
engaging with cases such as Turkey, this article contributes to
expanding the geo-epistemological debate, arguing [for greater
reflexivity in how Arctic knowledge regimes are constructed and
challenged.]{.underline}

Geo-Epistemology and the Arctic

Geo-epistemology refers to knowledge and discourses formed in specific
spaces and grounded in spatial thinking. This paper critiques
geo-epistemology in the Arctic, drawing on Foucauldian thought to
emphasize that space itself is also shaped by knowledge, power, and
discourses.2) This paper confronts the imperial perspective inherent in
chronologies and historization, recognizing it as a dominant paradigm
and hegemonic discourse that privileges certain narratives while
marginalizing others.3) This Eurocentric framework deeply embedded
within Western/European academic institutions, perpetuates
geo-epistemological hierarchies that uphold monopolies of knowledge.

[International Relations categorizes world politics by using
location-based references, such as the Western/non-Western]{.underline},
**[global North/global]{.underline}** **[South]{.underline}**,
domestic/foreign or national/international divide.4) [Classic IR then
relies on binaries, which is one of the major criticisms to its
geo-epistemological stance.]{.underline}

[Geographical classifications]{.underline} are particularly [prominent
in the **Arctic**]{.underline}, where the distinction between **[Arctic
and non-Arctic states is deeply ingrained]{.underline}**. This [binary
framework is reinforced consistently]{.underline} in [academic and
political gatherings, publications, and everyday
discussions]{.underline}. Such categorization often ties knowledge
[exclusively to specific geographic locations]{.underline},
**[emphasizing boundaries]{.underline}** [rather]{.underline} than
recognizing the [transnational and **transdisciplinary origins** of
expertise]{.underline}. There is no doubt that scientists from facts
[Arctic countries perceive, experience, and understand]{.underline} and
situations that non-Arctic scientists cannot. However, the reverse is
also true. Non[-Arctic scientists may -though not always- possess a
certain distance from the Arctic that allows them to perceive different
realities, consider alternative approaches, or arrive at distinct
findings]{.underline}. This is even the case between Arctic cities,
Oslo-Moscow relations are different from Kirkenes-Murmansk relations
regarding the Arctic.

This [division and **geo-epistemological** hierarchy **marginalizes**
the **expertise of non-Arctic experts**]{.underline}. But more
importantly, **[it limits Indigenous participation in Arctic
affairs]{.underline}**.5) In [these binaries, the state is the central
actor in decision-making process on defining what knowledge is relevant
or]{.underline} not.(Ellam Y, Raymond-Yakoubian J, Aluaq D & Behe C
(2022). A framework for co-production of knowledge in the context of
Arctic research, Ecology and Society, 27(1):34)) [Criticisms persist
regarding the inclusion of Indigenous voices in decision-making
processes]{.underline}, both within their respective countries and in
forums like the Arctic Council.6) [For instance, when the Arctic
**Council suspended** **cooperation** with Russia in 2022, the
**decision was made solely by the seven Arctic states.**]{.underline}

In examples of [Arctic-specific programs]{.underline}, the [exclusivity
of organizations]{.underline} such as the **[Arctic
Council]{.underline}** or Arctic Economic Council internships and the
[Arctic Fulbright Initiative]{.underline} to citizens of the [eight
Arctic states highlights a **monopolization of knowledge** and
specialization confined to these states' citizens]{.underline}. This
exclusivity, while potentially fostering regional expertise, also
**[limits diverse perspectives]{.underline}** and [hinders the
globalization of Arctic knowledge]{.underline}. Consequently, it
[contributes to an endogenous epistemology]{.underline}, where
[knowledge production in the Arctic predominantly relies]{.underline}
[on literature narrowly focused on the Arctic context.]{.underline}
Furthermore, this exclusivity not only restricts the globalization of
Arctic knowledge but also [raises questions about how the Arctic is
geographically and conceptually defined]{.underline}. For instance,
while an American citizen from Hawaii is naturally eligible for Arctic
expertise, an Estonian citizen living in Tromsø is excluded by the
imaginary boundaries drawn, thereby narrowing the field of expertise.

[Spatial thinking plays an important role in defining
geo-epistemological thinking,]{.underline} which informs the location of
knowledge. In the [Arctic Council,]{.underline} there are criteria used
to assess the suitability of applicants as observer states.7) First,
[observers]{.underline} must [acknowledge the legal
framework]{.underline} of the [Arctic]{.underline}
[governance]{.underline} and the **[sovereignty of eight Arctic
states]{.underline}** and must **[possess relevant Arctic
expertise]{.underline}**---a term that remains somewhat ambiguous and
may benefit from further clarification due to its broad scope.8)
Moreover, [observers are expected to contribute to
strengthening]{.underline} the [work]{.underline} of the permanent
participants, though how this impact is measured is not clearly defined.
It remains unclear exactly how the impact of their contributions can be
measured.9)

This [situation]{.underline} seems to [reinforce]{.underline} the
[Arctic Council's commitment to preserving]{.underline} an
[Arctic]{.underline} [core]{.underline} of the [eight Arctic
states]{.underline} and their relationships with permanent
participants.10) Knecht notes that observers remain as mere observers
within the Council's governance. Kankaanpää and Young, on the other
hand, highlights a significant gap between observers' aspirations and
the reality within the Arctic Council, with minimal evidence to counter
the claim [that "the Arctic Council has not effectively integrated
non-Arctic experts into its projects."11)]{.underline}

Monopolies of Knowledge and Power in the Arctic

The [concept of monopolies of knowledge]{.underline}, first proposed by
Canadian economic historian Harold Innis in the context of
communication, [highlights how **dominant** **classes maintain
political** **power** by controlling fundamental communication
technologies]{.underline}.12) Innis suggests that
[monopolies]{.underline} of [knowledge emerge]{.underline} when
[established hierarchies become rigid and disconnected]{.underline} from
social realities, [**hindering** the development of **new ways of
thinking**]{.underline}. In the [Arctic Council,]{.underline} [holding
the]{.underline} essential [power to evaluate what counts as
expertise]{.underline} aligns with the ambiguous assessment process of
what is considered "relevant knowledge." The term "relevant" has, in a
sense, become a buzzword, [highly open to interpretation, **leading to
the production** not to the knowledge we need but to the knowledge we
**desire**.]{.underline}

The [**Arctic Council** itself **embodies** the geopolitical discourse
surrounding the]{.underline} Arctic, where [ostensibly]{.underline}
[objective binaries shape spatial imaginaries that **prioritize certain
types of expertise**]{.underline} while marginalizing others.
[Monopolies of knowledge, in this context, can contribute to a
separation between the evaluation of Arctic and non-Arctic
knowledge]{.underline}, as such monopolies favor centralization of
power. [**Those who control knowledge** ultimately **possess** the
**power** to define what is to be governed.]{.underline}

Applications for Observer Status from 2015 to 2021

In 2021, Ireland, Czech Republic, and Estonia applied for observer
status in the Arctic Council but were rejected.13) Each of these
countries had prepared detailed applications to align with the Arctic
Council's fields of interest; however, there is not any official
explanation regarding the rejections, which raises questions about the
Arctic Council's hesitations toward expansion.14) Filimonova et al. also
mention that all these applicant states are NATO members (Czech
Republic, Estonia) or individual partner countries (Ireland), and their
applications may have faced opposition from Russia, which is resistant
to NATO-aligned states.15)

In 2015, Turkey, Greece, and Mongolia submitted applications for
observer status but were also rejected without an official explanation.
An interviewee16) from the Arctic Council suggested geopolitical
considerations as a factor in these decisions.17)

These [patterns reveal underlying dynamics of
geo-epistemology]{.underline} and [knowledge monopolies]{.underline}
that are **[rooted in imperialistic ideologies]{.underline}**. By
examining such decisions, we gain insights into how expertise is defined
and who controls what is considered relevant knowledge within
geopolitical bodies like the Arctic Council. As Andreeva and Rottem
argue, active engagement in research not only advances academic
knowledge but also aligns with foreign policy objectives and enhances
visibility.18)

Building on the discussion of knowledge monopolization and
geo-epistemology, Turkey's application for observer status in the Arctic
Council serves as a good example. In Turkish media, there was no
coverage of Turkey's application for observer status in the Arctic
Council, nor of the subsequent rejection of its applications. Similarly,
within the Arctic Council itself, information regarding applications and
rejections of observer status remains sparse.19) This lack of
transparency appears to extend to some scientists and decision-makers
who are unaware of these states' applications.

An intriguing piece of feedback at a panel at the 2024 Arctic Congress
in Bodø regarding Turkey's observer application to the Arctic Council
raised the question: How would Turkey contribute to the Arctic? On a
practical level, the feedback emphasized that ministerial and official
meetings are frequently held in remote areas, making accessibility
challenging. Additionally, a shortage of facilities to accommodate all
participants was cited as a reason to limit the number of observers.

This feedback on Turkey's application is valid to some extent but also
reflects underlying biases. The bias in the comment "What could Turkey
contribute?" stems from an assumption that Turkey is a monolithic
entity, failing to recognize the diversity within its scientific and
critical political communities in opposition to the current regime which
can be called démocrature.20)

This [highlights how **identity and geopolitics** **intersect** to
sustain the dominance]{.underline} [of hegemonic political narratives in
defining legitimacy in international governance]{.underline}. While
Mediterranean countries like Italy, Spain, and France are observer
states, their [Arctic engagement has historically been
framed]{.underline} within the [context]{.underline} of their [European
identity]{.underline} and alignment with the broader Western bloc.
Turkey's Mediterranean identity, however, intersects with its perceived
Middle Eastern identity and its Eurasian positioning. From an
Orientalist perspective21) Turkey is often perceived as a Middle Eastern
country in terms of its identity, and Eurasian in terms of its
aspirations. On the other hand, the acceptance of Asian countries to
observer status reflects geo-economic and geopolitical considerations,
beyond the relevant knowledge criteria. [Essentially, the East is what
the West makes of it.]{.underline}

Interviews and the literature reveal that the opacity of the application
process and decision-making raises questions about the inclusivity and
effectiveness of the Arctic Council. Inclusion is crucial for broadening
knowledge, particularly on global issues like environmental protection
and climate change. However, decisions regarding observer status seem to
be more influenced by politics than by scientific or environmental
factors.

Thus, [geopolitical consideration is still at the forefront of knowledge
production and accepting observers to the Council]{.underline}.22) The
analyses also show that the field of Arctic IR often remains deeply
Western and European-centric, adhering to a worldview rooted in cultural
and regional perspectives inherited from colonial empires.23) [This
approach limits the diversity of perspectives in the field and
reinforces hegemonic views that may not capture the complexities of
contemporary geopolitics.]{.underline}

[Reimagining Knowledge and expertise instead of relevant
epistemology]{.underline}

Such a **[rigid worldview]{.underline}**, [with its emphasis on
**spatial boundaries** and **established political
positions**]{.underline}, risks [**reinforcing** a static, **monolithic
conception** of knowledge rather than fostering the growth of expertise
in the Arctic]{.underline}. At the end of The Myth of Sisyphus, Camus
writes, "I leave Sisyphus at the foot of the mountain! One always finds
one's burden again...The struggle itself towards the heights is enough
to fill a man's heart. One must imagine Sisyphus happy."24) [Much like
Camus's imagining, which he presents as a **rebellion against the
futility of repetition; challenging**]{.underline} the
[established]{.underline} [expertise and methodologies]{.underline} in
the Arctic and in International Relations **[may require a similar act
of rebellion.]{.underline}** [Such **reimagining** is **essential** to
**break away from the repetitive paradigms** since the Arctic is larger
than the Arctic.]{.underline}

From the complex interplay between geo-epistemology, knowledge
monopolies, and geopolitical dynamics, we can conclude that if the
Arctic Council can be understood as a legitimate institution claiming to
set standards based on knowledge and expertise, it should apply more
explicit criteria to non-state observers and provide explanations for
rejections. As examples, there are also other regional platforms who
accept non-regional actors to their governance mechanisms to address
issues. In this sense, for example, limited observer statues or dialogue
partners, three-year partnership models from ASEAN, Pacific Islands
Forum would be interesting to respond to the criticisms about the
acceptance of observers and criteria and to be more inclusive.

We now live in a world where Qatar and Azerbaijan host COP meetings, and
where Saudi Arabia, known for its outdoor air conditioning, is a
signatory of the Svalbard Treaty and negotiates oil prices with Russia.
It is therefore not possible to keep the Arctic isolated from the
others, particularly given what it offers and what it faces. [Thus,
rather than hierarchy, heterarchy is more suitable for evaluating
expertise and shaping the discourse of power.]{.underline}

## K\-\--Hyperborean Imaginaries

#### Hyperborean Imaginaries K / CLS K.
