[Legal imagination]{.underline} -- polar and otherwise -- is [rather
poor]{.underline}. It follo[ws the doctrines of international law which
**transpose** state **sovereign concerns** onto the international
stage.]{.underline} As the **[multiple planetary crises]{.underline}**
unfold, [international law has]{.underline} proven [**unable** to find
adequate solutions]{.underline}. In a field only responsive to crises,1)
the **[overlapping]{.underline}** and **[interconnected]{.underline}**
[planetary **crises**]{.underline} of our times might have become out of
touch. In this context, our article asks whether there is still space
for legal imagination within the field of polar law. We [argue that to
come to terms with current planetary politics in the polar
regions]{.underline}, there is a [need to **re-examine** the
constellations of **legal norms** relevant to regional
governance]{.underline} (i.e., p[olar law as a normative
field]{.underline}) and **[academic discourse]{.underline}** (i.e.,
**[polar law as an epistemic field]{.underline}**) to assess where they
fail to answer the concerns of our times and how we, as the community of
"polar practitioners," and "polar legal scholars" [find space to
speculate and expand polar legal imagination]{.underline} to m[ore
adequately account for planetary governance.]{.underline} To open up
legal imagination in this context can materialise through a multitude of
different epistemic and normative interventions. For instance, [a
complete reimagination of what we consider "good" "governance" for the
poles could mean to think about how normative thought and practice
account for the myriads of more-than-human modes of existence in the
region]{.underline}, **[challenge]{.underline}** [traditional
hierarchical **relationships** between sovereign states and diverse
entities]{.underline}, both living and non-living and propose new modes
of thinking with and against normativity for and in the Arctic.

[One way we have tried to open up the space for thinking along
**theoretical and material critiques** of **polar law**]{.underline} and
building on experiences from three years of engagement between polar
lawyers and the Critical Legal Conference (CLC), we discuss [potential
**alternatives** to law's interaction with Arctic existence and agency
**beyond conventional** state-centric **legalities**]{.underline}. These
alternatives force us to [acknowledge the Arctic(s) and the polar
regions as **non-fixed** and **dynamic** **spaces** where
sovereignty]{.underline}, [legality, and normativity are fluid and
mutable.]{.underline} To conclude we argue that [thinking on speculative
registers]{.underline} can help to **[reassemble legal sovereignty
constructs]{.underline}** in [response to the poles' changing
landscapes.]{.underline} Expanding Arctic legal imagination to the
confines of the Earth provides an aperture to articulate new forms of
coexistence and justice to pave the way for legal theory and practice to
account for molecular-planetary concerns.

A Brief History of Polar Law

To find out whether there is room -- at all -- for legal imagination
within the field, it is necessary to go back to the history of polar law
as a normative field. A history or official hagiography of polar law is
not difficult to do as it follows the lines of international and
domestic laws' evolutions for and in the poles. However, much like any
other field of international law, the [historical principles behind
polar law are rooted in questions of **sovereignty**]{.underline},2)
**[dominion]{.underline}**,3) **[colonialism]{.underline}**,4)
(over)**[exploitation]{.underline}** of natural resources,5)
**[enclosure]{.underline}** (which is arguably linked to colonialism),
and **[exclusion]{.underline}** of people (mainly in the Arctic --
[again linked to the region's colonial past and present) and
**environment**]{.underline}. Again, and without much surprise, these
[reflect the Arctic littoral states' modes of governance]{.underline}.
These [frameworks]{.underline} have primarily been [focused on the
**management of polar spaces** through the lens of **national
interests**]{.underline}, often [**prioritising** and enabling states'
interests of **power projections**, control, and economic
interests.]{.underline}

In this context, [polar law has been a **reactive tool**]{.underline}
**for** managing resources and [delineating geopolitical
boundaries]{.underline} -- reflecting a [certain capitalist mode of
"seeing like a state]{.underline}"6) through which [states']{.underline}
[managerial]{.underline} [processes]{.underline} of geographical
enclosure and [control as well as the exploitation of all
living]{.underline} and non-living entities [for]{.underline} the
[accumulation]{.underline} of wealth [become an **ontological
practice**]{.underline} [to stabilise state sovereignty both globally
and domestically]{.underline}. And although law relevant to the polar
regions has since evolved (albeit slightly), these [**state logics**
remain what **ontologically frame** the **production** of **legal**
**norms** in the region]{.underline}. However, as we all know, the
[challenges facing the poles go far beyond the traditional states' modes
of existence]{.underline}. Polar [geopolitics]{.underline} and
[governance]{.underline} is [driven by ontological framings]{.underline}
of the [regions **as the terrain of states and for states**
--]{.underline} be them Arctic or non-Arctic.7) The
[multiple]{.underline} [ecological crises]{.underline} unfolding in
these regions and beyond **[pose legal and ethical
questions]{.underline}** that cannot be addressed by existing frameworks
alone. In that regard, and as we have said before, [law -- polar
and]{.underline} otherwise [-- fails us]{.underline}.8) So why should we
even try to save it?

International Legal Imagination: From past to present

At times when there are failures of law, where the legal practice has
difficulty catching up with the factual reality, [lawyers have
historically engaged in, what could be loosely termed, exercises of
legal imagination]{.underline}. For instance, as Koskenniemi outlines,
the [founding figures of international law]{.underline} -- French,
Spanish, British, German and so on -- [engaged in **legal imagination**
to shape the making of international norms and principles]{.underline}
to transpose their own visions and understandings of governance onto the
international stage -- again as a means of [**projecting their own**
**vision** of what ought to be]{.underline}. So at historical junctures,
these [legal technicians]{.underline} (i.e., European lawyers) ma[pped
their ideal governance future]{.underline} with the modalities of their
legal times. In that context, they [used]{.underline} [everything at
hand]{.underline} (e.g., **[theology]{.underline}**,
**[roman]{.underline}** **[law]{.underline}**, **[raison
d'état]{.underline}**, **[politics]{.underline}**, even natural
**[science]{.underline}**, physics and biology) [as novel frames to
imagine and argue differently]{.underline}.9) "[Legal]{.underline}
[imagining]{.underline} is [both specific to the time and
place]{.underline} where it takes place and continuous with imagining
that has taken place and will occur in different places and at different
times."10) Yet, [these historical modalities inherently guide
contemporary imagination]{.underline} through a [**framework** of
**objectivity**, **neutrality**, and **universality** in legal
discourse]{.underline}, elements that can be [traced back
to]{.underline} international [law's]{.underline} very
[inception.]{.underline} In other words, international law, including
**[polar law,]{.underline}** [continues to be influenced by a "**deeply
rooted" mindset** that often overlooks the dynamics of
power]{.underline}, the constraints on potential [legal
interpretations]{.underline}, and the universalization of a 'particular'
approach of legal speculation that [**reflects** the imagination of 17th
and 18th century **Western masculinist lawyers** as well as their own
**imperial**]{.underline} and [**colonial** **projects**.]{.underline}

[Historicizing]{.underline} the [values underpinning polar
law]{.underline} by [**dismissing** them as mere '**standards of the
past'** in]{.underline} academic [discourse]{.underline} [overlooks
their **ongoing influence** in the 'present']{.underline}. [Polar law is
'present']{.underline} and is far from flawless. This stance
[consequently neglects the intricate and often overt
connections]{.underline} between modern [legal]{.underline} knowledge
[and]{.underline} various forms of [power]{.underline}, including those
[based on race,11) class,12) and]{.underline} [gender]{.underline}.13)
[Such legal knowledge mirrors the cultural values associated with
modernity]{.underline}. It is evident in the Arctic through a range of
daily challenges faced by the region, impacting the material world,
biodiversity, Indigenous populations, and the broader future of the
planet. [It signifies the **outcomes** of a pervasive **power
structure** that has consistently accompanied international law that
**transcends** the **legal** **orders** of the polar
regions.]{.underline}

Expanding Polar Legal Imagination: Speculation and Prefiguration

As outlined in the introduction, law is typically a reactive discipline;
it responds to crises, disputes, or the needs of states. This is where
speculation comes into play, [but how do we speculate (as a practice)
with a focus on the polar regions]{.underline}? Speculative legal praxis
invites us to [proactively imagine alternative futures]{.underline}.
This is [not just about responding to immediate threats]{.underline} to
the polar environments (environmental law has not managed to do that in
a century of normative developments); it is [about]{.underline}
**[rethinking the legal frameworks themselves]{.underline}**,
[**questioning** their **assumptions**, and **envisioning** new
**possibilities** for polar law.]{.underline} One way of doing
speculation is through the [concept of prefiguration]{.underline}, as a
means of [imagining the future we want]{.underline}. In that sense,
[prefiguration]{.underline} allows us to **[move beyond]{.underline}**
the confines of **[existing legal systems]{.underline}** and ask
fundamental questions: what is the future of the polar regions we want?
And how do we transpose this vision of the future into a normative
endeavour? [Just as the poles are transforming as a result of the
climate and environmental crises, so too must our **legal** **concepts**
become **more fluid, adaptive, and interconnected**.]{.underline}
Traditional categories like [sovereignty]{.underline}, legality, and
[normativity]{.underline}, which have [historically been fixed and
stable,]{.underline} are now being **[challenged]{.underline}**
[by]{.underline} [ecological instability]{.underline}. This demands that
we move away from static legal systems towards more adaptive legal
orders, ones that can respond to molecular-planetary concerns and
ecological limits.

[**Speculative thinking** is not simply a means to escape from the
present]{.underline}. It is a **[political praxis]{.underline}** to
reimagine and [reconstruct]{.underline} legal [knowledge]{.underline}
that can [respond]{.underline} [to the complex]{.underline}, entangled
[realities]{.underline} of the polar regions. It allows us to [think
**beyond** the binaries of **state vs. non-state actors**]{.underline},
human vs. non-human interests, and **[utilitarian resource extraction
vs. environmental protection]{.underline}**. Instead, it [invites us to
consider a normative system that respects Indigenous
knowledge]{.underline}, [non-human sovereignties]{.underline}, and the
[interdependence of all life forms within these fragile
ecosystems]{.underline}. For us, to think of polar law on a speculative
register means not just imagining future scenarios and arguments for
legal governance but an overall rethinking of our way of polar
governance itself. [Speculation]{.underline}, in this sense, is [not
just about creating fictitious futures but an]{.underline} **active**,
[**creative** and critical **process**]{.underline} of
**[prefiguration]{.underline}**, as a means to envision the kind of
polar governance we want to see in the future as both a normative and a
political resituating exercise. [If **prefiguration** becomes a
**political** and normative praxis, then speculative **academia**
becomes the **venue for scholars to politicise**.]{.underline}

Speculative Academia -- [A turn to 'Critical Polar
Jurisprudence']{.underline}

Using prefiguration as a point of analytical departure, we asked
ourselves what is the kind of future that we want to have in the polar
regions? [**Polar Law**, as a subset of international law, is an
**artefact** deeply embedded within the sociocultural paradigm of
modernity and is **inevitably infected** with the methodologies and
ideologies of this paradigm.]{.underline} The field of Polar Law as an
academic discipline is riddled with doctrinal and formalist approaches
to the Arctic and Antarctic legal issues[. As international law
researchers, we are often trained to look for gaps in our polar legal
realities to heal]{.underline} (de lege ferenda), with almost messianic
ambitions, the [ills of our global collective social and environmental
factual realities]{.underline}. Polar law is no exception, and
black-letter methodologies abound. Even scholars whose research involves
environmental law, decolonisation or people liberation movements, whose
themes are perhaps more synonymous with a broad understanding of
'justice', often frame their arguments through a doctrinal lens to gain
legitimacy.

[As we aim to reimagine polar law]{.underline} in ways that are **[not
limited to state modes of existence]{.underline}** b[ut include humans
and more-than-human entities alike in this process,]{.underline} since
August 2022 we have opened the register of speculation to what we term a
'**[critical]{.underline}** [turn' to polar law.]{.underline} In doing
so, we contented that it was perhaps time to think of 'Critical Polar
Jurisprudence.' In this context, we have been convening two streams
annually at both the Polar Law Symposium14) and the Critical Legal
Conference (CLC). The Polar Law Symposium gathers researchers from
around the globe on an annual basis, providing a platform for the
exchange of research-based scientific knowledge on a variety of issues
pertinent to polar regions. Conversely, the CLC, which has been a
pivotal gathering since its inception in 1986, serves as a critical
venue for examining legal and political issues, through a vast array of
theoretical approaches. [That said, the Critical Polar Law initiative
has emerged to bring these two together]{.underline},
[reevaluate]{.underline} the [assumptions]{.underline} commonly held
about the polar regions and to [address emerging issues in]{.underline}
[polar law through]{.underline} the lens of [**critical
jurisprudence**.]{.underline} It aims to **[deconstruct existing legal
realities]{.underline}**, [such as the **premise** of conceptual
**superiority of state sovereignty** and its role **as the moulder of
law** and life in the polar regions.]{.underline}

Our way to [speculate and prefigure]{.underline} the future of polar law
was to [engage on **several topics** from a more **theoretical
perspective** with justice at the centre]{.underline}. While theories of
justice have have recently been advanced for Arctic governance,15) thus
far our theoretical endeavours have focused on broadening this
conversation not to focus neither governance nor on the poles
specifically. In an age where planetary politics are being reassembled,
we thought it was worth addressing what lies beyond. We therefore
[**chose** "**theorising**" as a means of prefiguration and **practice**
to assess why the polar regions can be a site of theoretical inquiries.
Along the way, **we articulated thoughts** around alternative
**theoretical reassessments of polar law.**]{.underline}

First, we advance the term **[Hyperborean Legalities]{.underline}** to
"[summon **dreamy visions** of **an alternative legal** **imaginary**
for the world's northernmost region"]{.underline} and to
[analyse]{.underline} the **[Arctic as a liminal space]{.underline}**,
one that [exists **beyond** **legal**]{.underline} dogmatic
[approaches]{.underline}. Conceptually, [Hyperborean Legalities allowed
us to challenge]{.underline} the superiority of [state
sovereignty]{.underline} and its role as the moulder of law and life in
the region. The idea is to [challenge]{.underline} both the [way the
poles are often imagined as empty, frozen, dark lands]{.underline}, and
think alongside the different shapes, forms, and sources of
normativity/ies in the regions.16)

Second, and building on the Hyperborea, [we analysed how the poles are
portrayed as "common]{.underline} [refuge]{.underline}", and "[areas
beyond sovereignty,"]{.underline} yet also as spaces where [state
sovereignty]{.underline}/ies are often ontologically [reinforced as a
tool of exclusion]{.underline}. In this context, in [mainstream legal
imagination]{.underline}, they become "**[quasi-frozen
sanctuaries]{.underline}**" where [law freezes yet governance
thrives]{.underline}. In these [perceived]{.underline} [liminal
regions]{.underline} with complex legal, environmental, and social
dynamics, [**challenging** dominant]{.underline} [governance
**structures** and traditional]{.underline} **[norms]{.underline}**.
These [regions]{.underline}, with their shifting cryospheric
landscapes,17) [serve as sanctuaries that **bring to light** the urgent
need to address **climate** change]{.underline} and
[reconsider]{.underline} the **[relationship between humans and marine
spaces]{.underline}**. However, [another narrative can be storied; one
where the fluidity, materiality, and disappearance of
ice18]{.underline}) can be thought of as a "sanctuary" that invites a
r[ethinking of environmental protection, legal frameworks, and the
struggle against territorial enclosures]{.underline} in a destabilised
planetary context. To come back to prefiguration, [water]{.underline} in
a frozen state **[can become a source of law]{.underline}**. Its
disappearance and the longing for the cold help speculate a normative
future that [cares for the human and non-human modes]{.underline} of
existence it supports.19) This [care is not merely a passive concern but
an **active relational engagement**]{.underline}, [recognizing the
interconnectedness of human and non-human entities.20)]{.underline}
Thinking about ice as a source of law thus allows us to [leave behind
landlocked]{.underline} [state-bound legal thinking]{.underline},
**[transcend]{.underline}** the [constructed]{.underline}
**[ontology]{.underline}** of a land-sea dichotomy, and [**think
fluidilly about oceanic spaces** in all their passing and temporal
physical states.]{.underline}

Lastly, it is important to acknowledge that any [political and
theoretical engagements]{.underline} with the [governance of polar
regions cannot be detached from multiple unfolding planetary
crises]{.underline}. The [pitfall]{.underline} of structuring the
production [of epistemic and normative knowledge]{.underline} around
delimited geographies is that it can [lead to **tunnel vision** where
one becomes **unable** to see **beyond regional impacts**]{.underline}.
Much like states, [**polar geographies** have been carefully
**constructed** along]{.underline} sovereign lines.21) To [resist the
ever-creeping might of sovereign powers]{.underline}, it is therefore
**[important to prefigure polar governance]{.underline}** [as planetary
politics and part of the Earth system.]{.underline} As such, polar
governance needs to come to terms that nothing is polar yet everything
is. In Cirkovic's words, [this calls for a "**new ontological
understanding** of the global politico-juridical space]{.underline}"
that "[approximate the inclusion and representation of 'everything
considered to be non-human]{.underline}".22) In speculating polar legal
imagination, we therefore argue that the [Arctic and Antarctic regions
are not]{.underline} merely as amalgamates of geographies [subjected to
**climate** **change** and **geopolitical contestations** but as
**conceptual spaces** where the traditional notions of sovereignty,
legality, and normativity are now unfrozen and set adrift in the open
sea of speculative jurisprudence]{.underline}. In this space,
**[speculation]{.underline}** becomes a [creative endeavour]{.underline}
that [seeks to articulate, enact]{.underline} **[visions of
coexistence]{.underline}** and justice that [are attuned to the
specificities of the polar ecologies]{.underline} and histories and seek
**[new paths for legal]{.underline}** **[theory]{.underline}** and
[practice in a **collective reimagining** of what law can and should be
in a broader planetary context.]{.underline}

Critical Polar Jurisprudence -- The Way Forward

To conclude, the aim of this article is to show that prefigurating and
speculating our collective normative futures in the polar regions
requires a commitment to facing planetary politics. [The space is wide
open for legal speculation in polar law; not only as a means to
rethinking legal norms and epistemological practices]{.underline}
(although to some extent it is necessary) but as a means to
[speculate]{.underline} the kind of [future we truly want]{.underline};
[identify]{.underline} and create [relationships as a **community of
planetary care**]{.underline}. At the heart of prefiguration lies a
(re)centring of justice that is not enclosed and bound by state modes of
existence. [Speculating for the polar oceans means turning our thinking
both onto microscopic and planetary concerns as part of relationalities
and deep entanglements]{.underline}. In this context, speculating better
and more just futures for the polar regions potentially means not
thinking about the polar regions in isolation and as a one-fit-all
discipline. It invites the need for polar law scholarship to embrace a
myriad of disciplines. Next year, we hope to launch the Critical Polar
Jurisprudence (CPJ) initiative at both the annual Polar Law Symposium
and the Critical Legal Conference, respectively. We invite you to join
us in summoning visionary alternatives for the legal frameworks
governing the poles.

https://www.anchoragemuseum.org/about-us/stories-and-voices/chatter-marks-journal-podcast/articles/melting-imaginaries-of-the-arctic/

The Arctic in the Anthropocene

[Non-Indigenous people,]{.underline} such as myself, [who will always be
profoundly on the outside when studying the Arctic,]{.underline} must
**[develop new ways]{.underline}** of thinking about the region that
[eschew the dichotomy of wilderness/civilization,]{.underline} which
seems to lead inextricably to the othering of the peoples and
environments of the Arctic. [Vibrant theories and methodologies that
center the experience]{.underline}, [knowledge, and imaginations of
Indigenous people are absolutely essential if we are to avoid
scholarship, policy making, and artistic work]{.underline} that at best
**[reifies pre-existing outsider imaginaries]{.underline}** of the
[region and at worst totally **excludes Indigenous voices** from a
position of knowledge creation]{.underline}. As well as looking to the
field of postcolonialism and scholars such as Bhabha and Spivak, another
burgeoning area of research that certainly breaks from the wilderness
mindset is posthuman studies. At its core it is an area of research that
seeks to disestablish the centrality of the human subject from how we
view the world, promoting a more ecological way of thinking, situating
the scholar as one of many lifeforms in the complexities of the
Anthropocene.

\[image omitted\]

Figure 2: The possibilities and problems of posthumanism in Arctic
studies. Troell, J. (Director). (1982). The flight of the Eagle
\[Screenshot Photograph\]. Sweden: Swedish Film Institute.

Drawing on Timothy Morton's research, I would suggest that we can view
the Arctic as a 'hyperobject', or something that is "massively
distributed in time and space relative to humans" (Morton 2013: 1).
Similar to Morton's example of climate change as a hyperobject, the
Arctic is a huge-scale, confusing, massively distributed social,
cultural and political space that is imagined as a single entity. In
reality it is a jumble of legal frameworks, state territories,
Indigenous homelands, ecologies, visual representations, ice, water,
land, and many other things that confound the simplicity of the term
'the Arctic' and which "involve profoundly different temporalities than
the human-scale ones we are used to" (Morton 2013: 1). Ultimately, the
field of posthuman studies grants new ways of conceiving of the Arctic,
which seek to overcome the limitations of an ontology that is
human-centric and, along with this, refute the notion of the Arctic as
strange, exotic, other and 'outside'. Such posthuman perspectives that
emphatically refute a singular modernist perspective can totally
reposition scholarship and art away from the limiting dichotomy of
wilderness/civilization, because wilderness is entirely a human
construction.

## K\-\--Climate

### 1NC\-\--Ecopsychoanalysis

#### Climate policymaking is an attempt to fill the ecological lack, fixating on returning to a world that never existed.

Joseph **Dodds** 11-28-20**22**. Joseph Dodds, PhD, is a Psychoanalyst
in private practice (CPS, IPA), Chartered Psychologist (CPsychol,
British Psychological Society), Registered Clinical Psychotherapist
(UKCP, CAP), and Lecturer and Researcher (UNYP, AAU). He is author of
the book Psychoanalysis and Ecology at the Edge of Chaos (Dodds, 2011,
Routledge) and various publications on psychoanalytic applications to
art, society, film, neuroscience, culture and climate change. \"Dancing
at the end of the world? Psychoanalysis, climate change and joy,\"
Journal of Analytical Psychology, Volume 67, Issue 5, pages 1257-1269,
https://doi.org/10.1111/1468-5922.12857. 苗

We are [living through a time]{.underline} of strange contradictions,
standing in the [crosscurrent of multiple crises]{.underline}. [Social,
political, cultural, and ecological webs are fragmenting]{.underline},
the prospect of a more complete collapse seems ever closer. Sometimes
our individual work with patients may seem futile given what's going on
outside our consulting rooms, yet we necessarily hold on to the belief
that what we do matters, each individual mind. The p[syche needs healing
on all levels, and sometimes paying attention to the smallest details
are just what is needed]{.underline}, as the [ripples fanning out from
the tiniest changes]{.underline} can at times prove the most
transformational[. Why are we **unable to act** even when our survival
depends on it]{.underline}? Whole regions of the planet are burning up
in flames or sinking into the sea, and [we find ourselves in a
generalized **psychosocial breakdown**, a madness within which it is
increasingly hard to think]{.underline}. This paper aims to attempt to
join the dots between psychoanalytic and post-psychoanalytic
perspectives in relation to climate change and the ecological crisis and
to begin a discussion on the role of joy in sustaining ourselves in the
face of the global catastrophe.

Can psychoanalysis help us now to 'think under fire' (Bion), and does
psychoanalysis also need to look beyond itself? There is an expanding
psychoanalytic literature addressing itself to climate change and the
ecological crisis (e.g. Dodds 2011, 2021; Hoggett 2019; Jordan 2009;
Lertzman 2015; Randall 2009; Searles 1972) which has been very helpful
at exploring the fantasies, anxieties, defences, destructiveness, etc.
around environmental crises, but so far little has been written about
the role of joy, not just in relation to climate change but in general,
a lack I find striking. In this paper I wonder whether this may be a
missing thread we now need. [We are living in a new epoch in which
humans have become a geological force]{.underline}. The [Anthropocene is
accompanied by fractures and systemic collapses, ruptures and
disruptions on many]{.underline} [levels]{.underline}:
**[climate]{.underline}**, **[ecosystems]{.underline}**, **[social
systems]{.underline}**, **[psychological systems]{.underline}**, with
**[nonlinear feedback loops]{.underline}** leading to [complex
interactions and systemic flows]{.underline}, some of which are
**[dangerously self-amplifying]{.underline}**. In my book Psychoanalysis
and Ecology at the Edge of Chaos (Dodds 2011), I argue for an engagement
with Deleuze and Guattari, complexity theory, and ecology as a way to
make sense of these complex connections.

The human-nature relation

I will begin with a brief review of [philosophical positions concerning
the relationship]{.underline} between humans and the rest of nature.
First, [humans see themselves as 'masters of the world']{.underline},
above it, **[dominating]{.underline}**, **[subduing]{.underline}**,
**[apart from, and separate]{.underline}**. This [anthropocentric
narcissism]{.underline} has clearly [played a large role in **creating
the current crises**]{.underline}, and [functions as an omnipotent
**defence against helplessness** in the face of the superior power of
nature]{.underline} (Freud 1927), the [global pandemic again reminding
us]{.underline} of just [how fragile our social]{.underline}
[systems]{.underline} actually [are]{.underline} in the face of
environmental changes. Second, this [view]{.underline} has [been
challenged by both deep ecology and ecopsychology]{.underline} with
their vision of a primary union between [humans and nature
shattered]{.underline} by **[traumatic
separation/disconnection]{.underline}** which thus offers a healing
promise of reconnection. I see this as, at times, leading to an
[eco-mysticism]{.underline} -- [**oceanic fantasies** of
oneness]{.underline} that [not only deny the very real]{.underline}
[differences between humans and non-human]{.underline}, but also
[accepts too much difference in assuming a primal rupture in need of
being overcome]{.underline}. Third, 'post-humanism' and the 'new
materialism' lead us to an acceptance of castration, acknowledging both
the limits of human power and that we are not (and never were) as
special as we think we are. However, I argue that [the Anthropocene
requires a further **shift**]{.underline}. Just as [omnipotence is a
defence against helplessness, '**helplessness'** can be a **defence
against omnipotence.**]{.underline} We are not powerless but have
[immense power]{.underline}, yet without knowing either how to act or
control it, like a toddler with a machine gun. There is an [irony to the
fact that post-humanistic challenges to our narcissism arrived just when
we became an active planetary force]{.underline}. Is there a way to
acknowledge this power without returning to the grandiosity that got us
into the mess in the first place? The fact that we are deeply woven into
the ecological mesh doesn't mean that our actions are absorbed
effectively by it.

In his book Chaosmosis, [Guattari]{.underline} (1995) called for a
generalized science of ecosystems or ['ecosophy']{.underline}, with
'[resonances, alliances and feedback loops between various
regimes']{.underline} (p. 91). In The [Three Ecologies]{.underline}
(2000) he develops this [further]{.underline}, exploring the
interconnected [spaces of natural ecology]{.underline}, [social
ecology]{.underline}, and [mental]{.underline} [ecology]{.underline}. I
argue that even the [word]{.underline} '**[environment']{.underline}**
is [itself part of the problem]{.underline}, making us [imagine a stable
**reliable backdrop** like a stage in the theatre, in front of which
'real' human life and **history takes place**]{.underline}. This
[foreground-background distinction]{.underline}
**[collapses]{.underline}** when we realize how enmeshed we are, and how
fragile the 'fixed' backdrop really is. Timothy [Morton]{.underline}
(2008) [suggests]{.underline} not only that this [stable background is
lost,]{.underline} but that [we need to mourn the fact that it was
**never there to begin with,**]{.underline} that the [very idea of a
stable background is part of the problem]{.underline}, and that we need
an 'ecology without nature'[, a 'dark ecology'.]{.underline} From my
perspective, [ecology is simultaneously the strongest
justification]{.underline} for moving beyond psychoanalysis and why,
despite everything, [psychoanalysis is needed now more than
ever.]{.underline}

Ecopsychoanalysis and climate change

[Psychologically]{.underline}, various [developmental levels intersect
with]{.underline} our [**problematic relationship** with
nature]{.underline}. The phrase ['(m)other Earth']{.underline} suggests
that our experience with the planet relates to the (m)other of infancy.
Following Klein, this [includes not only feelings of love and being
held]{.underline}, but also rage, envy and [destructiveness, and
phantasies]{.underline} of an infinitely giving Earth-breast that we
feel entitled to suck on without limit. In the greed ([one-person
psychology), envy (two-person), and jealousy (three-person) triad of
Klein]{.underline}, we are mainly [still stuck with a greed]{.underline}
that does not even recognize our dependence on the other, the Earth, or
even the existence of that other. Our species is in the situation of an
infant unable to tolerate weaning, where the breast is not only a
provider of nutrition, but a place where we expel unbearable states of
mind (Meltzer 1994). [**Climate change** evokes the **persecutory
anxieties of Klein's paranoid-schizoid position**,]{.underline} leading
to **[omnipotent defences]{.underline}** [to protect against feelings of
helplessness and fragmentation]{.underline} (Jordan 2009).
[Defences]{.underline} against depressive anxiety include the difficulty
in acknowledging human culpability and guilt, and they [interfere with
our capacities to deal with loss and mourning.]{.underline} Drawing on
Freud's (1916) concept of **[anticipatory mourning]{.underline}**,
individuals [and societies may adopt positions of consciously not
caring]{.underline} [about the environment or even their]{.underline}
[survival]{.underline}, or become **[actively destructive]{.underline}**
**[and self-destructive]{.underline}**, as a [defence against the
mourning yet to come]{.underline}. Alternatively, we [fall into
premature despair, preventing the action which might avoid the feared
loss.]{.underline} At the phallic-Oedipal level, on the other hand,
Searles (1972, p. 364) [identifies phantasies of eliminating Oedipal
rivals]{.underline} (including **[future generations]{.underline}**) and
[notes]{.underline} the '[moralistic' tone of **ecological
writing**]{.underline} involving the projection of Oedipal guilt, the
rape of Mother Earth. Furthermore, through relentless advertising,
possessions such as cars become symbols of genital achievement so that
initiatives to reduce [pathological consumerism]{.underline} can feel
like castration (Randall 2005), [one of the **many psychological
barriers to effective change.**]{.underline}

Such [defences]{.underline} [against]{.underline} what is now [called
eco-anxiety]{.underline} [involve unconscious alliances created socially
through]{.underline} [interactions]{.underline} **[at all
levels]{.underline}** giving [rise to **social phantasy
systems**]{.underline}. These [individually and socially
structured]{.underline} [defences can be viewed as
assemblages]{.underline} that emerge through self-organization, where
lower levels interact to form higher level structures [embodying
emergent properties which feedback to lower levels in a
process]{.underline} [of ongoing **recursivity**]{.underline}.
Complexity science studies how biological systems tend to occupy the
edge of chaos, the [fractal borderzone between stability and instability
providing maximum ecological flexibility and the most fruitful ground
for emergent pattern formation]{.underline}. In mental ecology, Palombo
(2007) shows how small pieces of insight in psychoanalysis self-organize
into ever larger structures. The same applies to the emergence of
'self'. For Deleuze and Guattari (2005), [larval subject and partial
objects self-organize into]{.underline} [assemblages]{.underline} with
emergent properties both [below and above the traditionally conceived
human subject,]{.underline} [including human and non-human
elements]{.underline}, organic and inorganic elements, **[both material
and representational.]{.underline}** In the [psychoanalytic
encounter]{.underline} we might [imagine an ecosystem of the physical
and non-physical, of living and non-living parts, of past and future, of
dream and word]{.underline}, of sweat on skin, fold on cushion, falling
leaves, a [gasp of insight]{.underline} [as]{.underline} a 'selected
fact' emerges, slight changes in breath, [thoughts on the extinction of
a species ...]{.underline}

How can we process this? [What changes in our psychoanalytic conceptions
when **we imagine that the 'we'**]{.underline} [**doing** the processing
includes various parts]{.underline} (both human and non-human) [in
temporary connections and exchanges?]{.underline} Can Bion's move from
PS to D include non-human or even non-living parts and couplings,
comparable to the way that the self-organizing eco-mental system
(Bateson 2000) of a termite mound includes both living and non-living
components? [Opening up to this way of thinking we might
understand]{.underline} [that our sense of our capacities as
humans]{.underline}, [including our experiences of joy]{.underline},
could be **[enriched]{.underline}** [through]{.underline} such
[heterogeneous assemblages and productive couplings]{.underline}.
**[Psychoanalysis]{.underline}** itself can be understood as a
'[destabilization']{.underline} of what Busch (2007) [calls pathological
infantile attractors in the coevolving ecosystem of]{.underline}
[analyst and patient]{.underline}, and while most change is confined to
the local level and absorbed by wider defences, as the [system reaches
self-organized criticality the tiniest local shift can precipitate
cascades of disorder and subsequent reorganization throughout the entire
system.]{.underline} Such **[dynamic models]{.underline}** of change are
[crucial]{.underline} for [understanding psychological]{.underline} and
**[social]{.underline}** **[shifts]{.underline}** in response to
ecological crisis. [Periods of **instability** are]{.underline}
**[natural stages]{.underline}** on the path [towards greater
self-organization]{.underline}, but without guarantee that what emerges
will be better (even our idea of what 'better' means can dynamically
change[). Complexity theory helps us understand how psychological,
natural, and social ecologies are organized, and how fragile they can
be]{.underline}. The [task]{.underline} then **[becomes an experimental
search]{.underline}** for 'lever [points' to open up the possibilities
of radical transformation.]{.underline}

Animal becomings and joy

I have previously argued (Dodds 2012) that animality is a crucial area
where these intersections can be explored. The '[animal' returns us to
the clinical origin of psychoanalysis]{.underline}, the rat man, the
wolf man, and Little Hans' horses, as well as to Freud's writings on the
origin of religion and civilization and Jung's on the mythic dimension.
[Deleuze and Guattari (]{.underline}2005) [distinguish between 'Oedipal'
animals (]{.underline}domesticated pets), which we can [relate to
Guattari's 'mental ecology']{.underline}, 'State' or mythic animals
(e.g. the British Lion, the American Eagle) which can be [related to
Guattari's 'social ecology']{.underline}, and 'nomadic' or wild (pack)
animals which are [related to his 'natural ecology'.]{.underline} The
**[ecopsychoanalytic]{.underline}** [perspective]{.underline} I have
been developing suggests it is helpful to see the [three ecologies as
in]{.underline} [constant]{.underline}, [complex non-linear interaction
with one]{.underline} [another]{.underline}. Whenever we are discussing
one or two of them we should wonder what is happening to the other. [For
example, how does an 'idea' **emergent out of the mental system**
**impact** on an ecosystem or **social system?**]{.underline} How does a
[phantasy affect biodiversity and vice versa]{.underline}? How does a
microscopic organism expelled in a sneeze in China shut down the Venice
Carnival (Dodds 2020)? [Our fantasies about animals tell us something
about ourselves,]{.underline} and about [human relationships with the
non-human other]{.underline}, but they also have **[real effects on that
other]{.underline}**, [via interactions with social ecology and natural
ecology.]{.underline}

Deleuze and Guattari's **[geophilosophy]{.underline}** helps us [move
from 'becoming-animal' to 'becoming-ecological']{.underline}, opening up
to **[heterogeneous relationships]{.underline}** and [becomings in
trans-individual and sub-individual webs of connectivities.]{.underline}
As well as individual and social meanings, the animals in both our
dreams and art [reflect the effects of ecological crisis]{.underline},
the Earth dreaming through us. [I see the (human) ecological self as
being **infiltrated by and plugged into wider ecological networks** in a
process with **potentials for joy as well as anxiety**.]{.underline} The
[depressive ambivalence]{.underline} offered by
[psychoanalysis]{.underline} can be [expanded through Deleuze and
Guattari's]{.underline} (neo-Spinozian) geophilosophy, with more
[open]{.underline} connections to the [non-human world and the specific
forms of joy]{.underline} that this makes possible. [We need a **space
for joy** in these times]{.underline}. **[Dancing while the world burns?
Is]{.underline}** this possible? [Can we still have pleasure
today]{.underline}? What is the '[normal' we are going back to
'post'-COVID in the context of accelerating climate and ecological
meltdown?]{.underline}

In addition to terrible suffering, the [global COVID pandemic has much
to teach us in the context of]{.underline} [climate change]{.underline}
(Dodds 2020), it's a kind of trial run. For many, the [lifting of
restrictions of lockdown]{.underline} and the command to [return to
normal life]{.underline} has been an experience as
[traumatic]{.underline} as the lockdown itself. Some felt a kind of
[safety in retreating from the world]{.underline} (for those lucky
enough to have homes safe from eviction). Many felt they [lost what
social skills]{.underline} they had during this time and don't know how
to go about [restarting connections]{.underline}. If the '**[normal' was
never normal,]{.underline}** then to what should we return? We are
living with [considerable anxiety and confusion around the pandemic, our
relations to one another, and to our threatened ecological
systems.]{.underline} There are also many confusing feelings around joy.
I think that many of us have forgotten how to really enjoy ourselves,
especially when the [consumerist superego commands us to 'enjoy!' in
individualized forms]{.underline}, which [only make us feel]{.underline}
more [anxious]{.underline} and guilty (Žižek 2009).
[Lockdowns]{.underline} around the world ended the collective experience
of live music and this was felt as a terrible loss by many. [In the
presence of so much death and pain, and while a **terrible war is raging
in Ukraine** and when **ecological systems themselves**]{.underline} are
[collapsing]{.underline}, it may sound trivial to be worried about
dancing to the beat of a song[. Are we allowed to enjoy ourselves
anymore in the face of the existential threat of climate change, or of
spreading infectious diseases?]{.underline}

A place for joy and music

I think we need to find a place in the conversation about climate change
for [social joy,]{.underline} for the intense connection that music and
other forms of pleasures can bring, especially now. [Ecopsychological
writings]{.underline} (see Mary-Jayne Rust 2020 for an essential
overview) have [explored]{.underline} the **[oceanic
feeling]{.underline}** of [loss of ego-boundaries that can occur in
liminal experiences in natural spaces]{.underline}, as the boundaries
[between self and the more-than-human world merge.]{.underline} Marion
Milner (2010) describes a similar experience, both wonderful and
terrible, in relation to the artistic process. These experiences can be
[profoundly healing]{.underline}. There is also much overlap with
Winnicott's (2005) concept of play and how central this is to life and
to our work as therapists, something that we seem to be forgetting.
Music experienced together in passionate collective movement releases an
enormous amount of pent-up feeling, emotion, pain, love, sadness, and
also joy. For Deleuze and Guattari:

Music is never tragic, music is joy. But there are times it necessarily
gives us a taste for death; not so much happiness as dying happily,
being extinguished. Not as a function of a death instinct it allegedly
awakens in us, but of a dimension proper to its sound assemblage, to its
sound machine, the moment that must be confronted, the moment the
transversal turns into a line of abolition. Peace and exasperation.

(Deleuze & Guattari 2005, p. 299, italics in original)

[But can we allow ourselves to dance in this time, is it not dangerously
selfish and irresponsible]{.underline}, a [denial of
trauma]{.underline}? What about a whole generation of young people who
have been deprived of those first magnificent experiences of finding
themselves and each other over the shared generational moment they are
living through and embody?

[Psychoanalysis]{.underline} likes to talk about ambivalence but, in
practice, [this often means putting an emphasis on the 'hidden' dark
side that's being repressed.]{.underline} However, it should also mean
allowing the **[hidden light to shine]{.underline}**. Tragedy can be
clung to as a defence against the comic, just as much as the other way
around, as the novels of Milan Kundera demonstrate so well. [After all,
tragedy has]{.underline} [much to offer which comedy lacks: certainty,
nobility, finality.]{.underline} Freud's (1905, 1919) description of the
uncanny and the comic are remarkably similar (e.g. dolls, body parts
that move by themselves, uncertainty as to whether something is alive or
an automaton, eyes popping out, etc.) and they can be seen as
essentially implicated within each other (see here Bakhtin's \[1984\]
comic-grotesque 'grave-womb').

[Guilt and shame over dancing while the world burns can understandably
paralyze us.]{.underline} My view is that the [environmental movement
has a problem with guilt and shame]{.underline}. It is often perceived
(partly due to wider societal projections of repudiated guilt onto
activists, see Randall \[2005\]) as being joyless, calling us to
[renounce all pleasures as selfish in the name of defending the future
of the Earth.]{.underline} This is one of the obstacles for the wider
social transformation we require if we are to survive. [We need spaces
of collective joy within the **struggle against climate** **change**, of
possibilities for euphoria and experiences of **imminent
transcendence.**]{.underline}

Bion's (1989) concept of alpha-function and beta-elements is important
in the context of art. The [world can be understood as experiencing an
overload of beta-elements]{.underline}, undigestible fragments -- both
the physical and psychological toxic by-products of our
industrial-growth society -- [with many of our cultural and political
containers failing their work of psychological digestion]{.underline},
leading to system-wide breakdowns and fragmentation. One of the crucial
roles for art, including music, is that it allows a [cultural working
through of undigested beta-elements, a kind of psychological
composting,]{.underline} so that **[fragments can be reused,
developed,]{.underline}** and made a part of other cycles and flows.
[Music]{.underline} contains a rich tapestry of emotional resonance, a
thousand cries of pain and pleasure, creating temporal [collectivities
that ripple through time, space, and dreams]{.underline} (Nagel 2013;
Rose 2004). In Listening Subjects, Schwarz (1997) describes two key
features of music, music as 'sonorous envelope' (drawing on Bick's and
Anzieu's concept of psychic skin), including the enveloping oceanic
feeling of music where the boundary separating body from world
dissolves, and music as 'acoustic mirror'. Schwarz points out that,
despite being the talking and listening cure, psychoanalysis prefers
visual metaphors rather than auditory, [which is ironic since the
musical dimensions of language predate and underpin the emergence of the
linguistic register]{.underline}. For Mauro Mancia (2006), it is the
musical dimensions of the transference that connect us most directly to
the early unrepressed unconscious.

A joyful ecopsychoanalyis?

I argue that we need today to [develop a psychoanalysis of
joy.]{.underline} Winnicott once wrote, in a parody of psychoanalytic
interpretation, that:

one is at a music-hall and on to the stage comes dancers, trained to
liveliness. One can say that here is the primal scene, here is
exhibitionism, here is anal control, here is masochistic submission to
discipline, here is a defiance of the superego. Sooner or later one
adds: here is LIFE. Might it not be that the main point of the
performance is a denial of deadness, a defence against depressive 'death
inside' ideas?

(Winnicott 1935, p. 131)

Deleuze and Guattari, in A Thousand Plateaus, seeking an approach to joy
beyond Lacan, Freud, Klein or Jung, write that:

There is, in fact, a joy that is [immanent to desire as though desire
were filled by itself and its contemplations]{.underline}, a [joy that
implies no lack or impossibility]{.underline} and is not measured by
pleasure since it is what distributes intensities of pleasure and
prevents them from being suffused by anxiety, shame, and guilt.

(Deleuze & Guattari 2005, p. 155)

There are many paths to joy. The masochist, for example 'uses suffering
as a way of constituting a body without organs and bringing forth a
plane of consistency of desire. That there are other ways, other
procedures than masochism, and certainly better ones, is beside the
point; it is enough that some find this procedure suitable for them'
(ibid.).

Searching on PEP-Web, I found only 58 references with 'joy' in the title
in the whole psychoanalytic literature (and about a third of those refer
to authors called 'Joy'!). Such a central emotion, and psychoanalysts
with so little to say about it? For Heisterkamp (2001) joy is
complementary to anxiety (and there's certainly no lack of writings on
anxiety in the psychoanalytic literature), with the former representing
successful restructuring, a new beginning, and the latter a failure of
structuring. Given the intensity of anxiety about climate change --
which we need to feel so that the energy used in our defences against it
can be employed to more effectively deal with the threat we face (Dodds
2021) -- and the urgent need for psychological, social and ecological
restructuring, there needs to be space for joy to oppose terror, just as
Bakhtin (1984) described as one of the key purposes of carnival.
Heisterkamp (2001) describes joy as a basic form of 'resonance', from
the child seeing herself reflected in the gleam of mother's eye (Kohut
1971, p. 16), to the implicit goal of psychoanalytic treatment to
(re)discover the joie de vivre, reminding us of Winnicott's emphasis on
aliveness. In his review of the psychoanalytic literature, Heisterkamp
found only three reference to joy in the general index of Freud's work,
and none at all in Jung's, which is extraordinary. How could they have
missed something so vital?

[Heisterkamp argues that this has extended to the wider psychoanalytic
literature,]{.underline} amounting to something of a taboo against joy,
which is terribly limiting as: 'joy lies at the pivot of psychic
transformation, where the scope of psychic self-articulation and
self-regulation expand. Joy is the feeling of self-discovery, of new
beginnings (Balint 1968), and of restoration of the self (Kohut 1977)'
(Heisterkamp 2001, p. 858). Joy is inhibited by fear, insecurity,
trauma, shame, and guilt. Joy may also be avoided due to a fear of a
loss of control, or a loss of the prestige of being serious (although
both Freud and Winnicott knew that play was very serious). Joy can serve
several functions in the analytic session, which certainly include that
of defence, collusion, and resistance, but it can also offer powerful
moments of relational resonance over the shared humanity (I would also
add animality) of analyst and patient (Biancoli 2002), affirming
uniqueness (Buechler 2002), of excitement over developmental progress
(Heisterkamp 2001), and a sense of a shared lived experience of this
moment (Stern 1998). In a future paper I would like to further explore
the differences and overlaps between collective, dyadic, and individual
forms of joy, and the joy involving complex assemblages including
non-human and non-living components, with music as rhizome connecting
and constituting collectivities.

Searles (1972) [suggested that the way in which our society is failing
to react to ecological crises reflects a sense that there is 'something
so unfulfilling about the quality of human]{.underline} life that we
react, essentially, as though our lives are not worth fighting to save'
(p. 365). There is therefore an [urgent need in these times of
environmental crisis to allow ourselves to experience joy, with
its]{.underline} [simultaneous affirmation of both our uniqueness and
togetherness]{.underline} (not only as humans but with all forms of
life) as analysts, patients, as activists, and as a species sharing this
world with our fellow living creatures. There is [joy and revelry in
feeling our connection to the web of life itself]{.underline}, and the
possible new assemblages it allows us to form. We need to [develop a
deeper psychoanalytic]{.underline} [approach to joy and find out what
stands in its way]{.underline}. Dancing at the end of the world? Let's
hope the music will go on for a little while longer at least[. Perhaps
then, if]{.underline} we [**were to allow ourselves** to actually
**enjoy** our lives, we just might **fight harder against our
extinction.**]{.underline}

Heather **Greenhalgh-Spencer** 4-14-20**14**. Heather

As Bennett (2010) argues, while [ecosophy neither privileges the

[advocates]{.underline} for **[measurement]{.underline}**, known

[Ecosophy resists becoming a 'recipe' for '**correct'** **pedagogical

#### No link to dark ecology.

Heather **Greenhalgh-Spencer** 4-14-20**14**. Heather
Greenhalgh-Spencer, PhD, is an associate professor in the Department of
Curriculum and Instruction at Texas Tech University. \"Guattari's

[Ecosophy]{.underline}, as a term, has [not only been used by
**Guattari**]{.underline}. For example, the term
'[ecosophy']{.underline} was first [coined by Arne
**Naess**]{.underline} (1973/2010). However, as John
[Tinnell]{.underline} (2011) [points ou]{.underline}t, [Naess's
conception of]{.underline} [ecosophy]{.underline} is **[quite
different]{.underline}** [from that put forward by
**Guattari**]{.underline}. Writes Tinnell: 'At a fundamental level, the
mission of Naess's ecosophy is to expand the sphere of objects with
which people identify' (2011). In other words, for Naess, ecosophy is
about a deep personal commitment to and responsibility for the
environment. One comes to identify with nature in order to preserve it.
For [Guattari, ecosophy instead involves a subjectivity instantiated as
a **process of coming together** with other parts of nature]{.underline}
(human and nonhuman) that [create assemblages of identity, materiality,
and practice]{.underline}. Guattari is [concerned with **remaking
ontology** as an assemblage of materiality, social practices, and the
ideological or mental capacities.]{.underline}

One of the [positions that marks **Guattari** as different from
**Naess**]{.underline}---[and from other scholars concerned]{.underline}
with [sustainability or environmental preservation]{.underline}---is the
[desire to avoid privileging any one]{.underline} [position]{.underline}
or [stance]{.underline}. According to **[Tinnell]{.underline}**,
[Guattari's ecosophy]{.underline} insists that the [world is 'a dance
between chaos and]{.underline} [complexity]{.underline}---a multitude of
**[productive syntheses]{.underline}** between nomadic parts that exist
independent of any fixed structure or transcendental whole' (Tinnell,
2011). For [Guattari]{.underline}, not only is there a [push against
hierarchy, but there is also a]{.underline} foregrounding of
[dynamism]{.underline}. There are [times when materiality and
the]{.underline} non-[human are privileged as shaping powers within the
world,]{.underline} but also times when human and global politics are
pinpointed as shaping powers. That is to say, [Guattari's ecosophy
positions both the human]{.underline} and [nonhuman as actants;
both]{.underline} have the **[capacity]{.underline}** to **[act as
subjects, objects]{.underline}**, and **[somewhere
in-between.]{.underline}** Guattari's **[ecosophy]{.underline}** aims
[toward instability]{.underline}---[instability of
identity]{.underline}, of [relationships]{.underline}, of connections,
of 'knowns'---in order to [create space for something new
and,]{.underline} hopefully, [less enmeshed]{.underline} [within a
predatory **capitalism**]{.underline} that has already colonized many
current forms of life.

[Guattari]{.underline} [argues that **all registers of life** are
**interconnected**]{.underline}; that the [production of material
artefacts]{.underline} are [connected with **social and cultural
production;**]{.underline} that our world is produced through the
interaction of discourse, human practice, and materialities. In this
way, Guattari's work finds affinity with philosophers like Walter
Benjamin (1968), Martin Heidegger (1971) and Henri Lefebvre (1992), who
also examine the connections between culture, practice, and
materialities. [Ecosophy insists on valuing and examining the **material
world**, **technology**, **artefacts**, and the ways that things can
both shape and be shaped by humans.]{.underline} Akin to the work of
scholars such as Donna Haraway (1991), Katherine Hales (1999), and

advocates]{.underline} for an analysis of the [blurred lines between

relationships of human and machine have bearing on the **wider natural
world** and **institutional environments** in which we

When it comes to [constructing strategic narratives]{.underline},

To briefly describe the methods used to create this article, the term
'Ukraine' was searched for in the corresponding websites of both of

heavily on keyword search approach,]{.underline} [concrete
articles]{.underline} were [pinpointed]{.underline}. When the keywords
were identified, the specific article was analyzed, thoroughly reading
it, and confirming the general sentiment of the article. This method
does not completely eliminate the possibility of subjectivity, however,
content analysis provides a systematic framework for the qualitative and
quantitative exploration of textual data, enabling researchers to
identify recurrent patterns and thematic structures. [This rigorous
approach facilitates a nuanced]{.underline} [understanding of underlying
meanings and contextual significance within large datasets.]{.underline}
Complementarily, keyword search functions as a targeted retrieval
mechanism, enhancing the efficiency of data processing by isolating
instances of specific terms or phrases. [This focused retrieval
streamlines the analytic process]{.underline}, allowing researchers to
concentrate on relevant segments of text. The search was done in
Russian, to ensure that the information is intended for the Russophone
population of the Russian Arctic, not the international audience that
might be following the media. [Due to the scope of this research, a
short timespan was selected -- just the first 8 months of

is crucial to highlight the fact that there are hundreds if not

To conclude, there is [a clear difference in how regional media outlets
communicate in the Russian Arctic, and how the national outlets

[The growing taste for violence can be seen in the increasing modeling
of public schools after prisons, the criminalization of behaviors such
as homelessness that once were the object of social protections. A
symptomatic example of the way in which violence has saturated everyday
life can be seen in the growing acceptance of criminalizing the behavior
of young people in public schools. Behaviors that were normally handled
by teachers, guidance counselors and school administrators are now dealt
with by the police and the criminal justice system]{.underline}. The
consequences have been disastrous for young people. Not only do schools
resemble the culture of prisons, but young children are being arrested
and subjected to court appearances for behaviors that can only be termed
as trivial. How else to explain the case of the five-year-old girl in
Florida who was put in handcuffs and taken to the local jail because she
had a temper tantrum; or the case of Alexa Gonzales in New York who was
arrested for doodling on her desk. Even worse, a 13-year-old boy in a
Maryland school was arrested for refusing to say the pledge of
allegiance. There is more at work than stupidity and a flight from
responsibility on the part of educators, parents and politicians who
maintain these laws; there is also the growing sentiment that young
people constitute a threat to adults and that the only way to deal with
them is to subject them to mind-crushing punishment. Students being
miseducated, criminalized and arrested through a form of penal pedagogy
in prison-type schools provide a grim reminder of the degree to which
the ethos of containment and punishment now creeps into spheres of
everyday life that were largely immune in the past from this type of
state violence. The governing through crime ethic also reminds us that
we live in an era that breaks young people, corrupts the notion of
justice and saturates the minute details of everyday life with the
threat, if not reality, of violence. This mediaeval type of punishment
inflicts pain on the psyche and the body of young people as part of a
public spectacle. Even more disturbing is how the legacy of slavery
informs this practice given that "Arrests and police interactions ...
disproportionately affect low-income schools with large African-American
and Latino populations,"(26) paving the way for them to move almost
effortlessly through the school-to-prison pipeline. Surely, the next
step will be a reality TV franchise in which millions tune in to watch
young kids being handcuffed, arrested, tried in the courts and sent to
juvenile detention centers. This is not merely barbarism parading as
reform -- it is also a blatant indicator of the degree to which sadism
and the infatuation with violence have become normalized in a society
that seems to take delight in dehumanizing itself.
