### 2AC\-\--No Root Cause/Turns Case

#### "Root cause" and "serial policy failure" are [theoretical malpractice]{.underline}. Drive lacks [causal force]{.underline}.

**Kornbluh, 23**---Professor of English at the University of Illinois,
Chicago (Anna, "We Didn't Start The Fire," Parapraxis, Issue 3, December
2023, language modifications denoted by brackets, un-underlined portions
reference self-harm dml)

[For]{.underline} a radically interdisciplinary and interprofessional
consensus---**[psychoanalysts]{.underline}** and philosophers,
sociologists and cultural critics, journalists and
lawyers---[[the]{.underline}]{.mark} signifier "death
**[[drive]{.underline}]{.mark}**" [ferries a **compulsively repeatable
[explanation]{.mark}** [of]{.mark}]{.underline} climate
[catastrophe]{.underline}. [Why are we forcing
**[extinction]{.mark}**?]{.underline} [Because]{.underline}, according
to both lay analysis and high theory, meme-able headlines and academic
symposia, [there is so much **enjoyment**]{.underline} in the unholy
convergence of best-practice aggression and the growth imperative of
extractive capitalism.

Speaking of enjoyment, [the very **repeatability** of this explanation
suggests that its satisfactions are **manifold**]{.underline}. It is of
course essential to make sense of the senselessness around us. Yet the
sense here is a little too enjoyable. Like all psychologisms, [it
furnishes the abject succor of **exoneration**]{.underline}: [we
**simply cannot help it**]{.underline}, it is our nature to annihilate
nature. In the face of the oft-extolled sublimity of climate
crisis---its scale and severity, its complexity and
uncognizability---death drive as cause domesticates and rationalizes:
this is why we do it. And up against the speculative, inconsistent
strangeness of the death drive, this prevailing mantra magnetically
assures: drive means. Positivized aggression in the final solution of
heat-seeking destruction. Downright irresistible.

To cut to the chase: [this]{.underline} ubiquitous warming nugget of
contemporary discourse [[commits **theoretical
malpractice**]{.underline}]{.mark}.
[[Psychoanalysis]{.underline}]{.mark} is understandably resurgent in the
quivering void of explanations for rampant immiseration, but the popular
front [should not **misframe** its fundamental concepts]{.underline}.
The death drive is a theoretical construct, Freud steadfastly insisted,
a "limit concept" at the limit of conceptuality, figuring limits to
thinkability and consciousness, corporeal borders, and the divide of
nature/culture; its liminality and speculativeness court misuse,
especially the misuse of taming its wild. One such subdual is this tony
settling [of]{.underline} the death [drive as **cause**]{.underline}.
[Taking]{.underline} the death [drive as a cause of]{.underline} the
climate [catastrophe [is wrong about
**causality**]{.mark}]{.underline}[, [both **politically** and
**libidinally**]{.underline}]{.mark}. [Mass psychology is **not
responsible** for]{.underline} carbon modernity's
[devastations]{.underline}. And [[the death drive is **not a
program**]{.underline}]{.mark}.

There are therefore two corrections to make, the first political and the
second psychoanalytic. Not corrections for correction's own sake, for
schoolmarm rectitude, but to percuss the gratifications of this double
mistake. So, OK, schoolmarm kinkshaming. It is evidently enjoyable [[to
believe that humanity is]{.underline}]{.mark} killing itself \[[[causing
extinction]{.underline}]{.mark}\] [[on **purpose**]{.underline}]{.mark}.
It [[offers]{.underline}]{.mark} the **[consolation]{.underline}** of
our competence, [[**exculpations** of]{.mark} our
[inaction]{.mark}]{.underline}, [and]{.underline} ultimately the
[**reassurance** that]{.underline} enjoyment is efficient, that [drive
is]{.underline} in the last instance not a disturbance in the order of
being, but efficient **[manifest destiny]{.underline}**. We should
question this enjoyment, and as partisans of a renaissance of
psychoanalysis, we have a responsibility to traverse it.

On the political point, we can say quite simply that the [masses of
people]{.underline}---especially those who are already dying, but up to
and including those who, on average, suffer only
#firstworldproblems---[are **not responsible**]{.underline} for carbon
modernity, [and to suggest that they are is an **egregious obfuscation**
that **debilitates mass mobilization**]{.underline}. Carbon capitalist
autocracy, a highly specific and contingent mode of resource management
and power monopoly, is the cause. Many historians and geologists deem
the post-1950 intensification of the human imprint on the earth system
of physical, chemical, biological, and human processes "the great
acceleration." This acceleration tracks closely with the dramatic
decline in top income tax rates and statutory corporate tax rates
globally. It also tracks with the oil companies' own scientific
conclusiveness about anthropogenic climate change. They know it, and
they are doing it. The average billionaire emits one million times more
carbon than the average person living in the bottom 90 percent; this is
not only lifestyles of private jets and yachts and eight houses, but
also the aggregate emissions of their investments---in the mere one
hundred companies that have been responsible for more than 70 percent of
global emissions over the last fifty years. The average fossil-fuel
executive is not ignorant of the lethal impact of their actions, but
hews to the rational calculus that if they do not profit, someone else
will. And these colossal untaxed emitters and wanton oligarchs have in
the same timeframe consolidated outsize influence over politics, media,
and the rule of law. Political leaders cannot champion drawdown, public
infrastructure, or corporate accountability when they are themselves
owned by the oil industry; journalists cannot represent the truth when
they have been downsized, outsourced, and automated; courts cannot
adjudicate rights and freedoms, injury and remedy, when they are only
corrupt kangaroos.

These [[clear **causes** mean]{.mark} there are some [clear
**solutions**]{.mark}]{.underline}. [[Don't believe]{.mark}
the]{.underline} "**[[it's complicated]{.underline}]{.mark}**"
[mantra]{.underline} of Latourian ecocriticism, [[because **it's
simple**]{.underline}]{.mark}: cutting the carbon footprint of the
world's wealthiest people is the fastest way to reach net zero. No more
Exxon Mobil and BP, no more billionaires, no more private planes, no
more private senators, no more oil; yes more taxation, yes more pipeline
sabotage, yes more lowlands mutual aid, yes more federalized
decarbonization and centralized resource redistribution. Yes, [this
[simplicity **falls short of**]{.mark} **communist
[revolution]{.mark}**]{.underline}. [[But]{.mark} it would **[ameliorate
vast suffering in the]{.mark} looming [present]{.mark}**]{.underline}.
[[Psychologizing]{.underline}]{.mark} ecocide [**[rules out]{.mark}**
even those modest remedies]{.underline}.

Psychologizing the death drive, for its part, is an error perhaps
invited by the rends of ambiguity in Freud's speculations, or the
lurching rhythm of all his efforts to inscribe it, including its
maladroit name. Alas, that the drive is not a program has not stopped
proper [psychoanalytic]{.underline} clinicians and
[theorists]{.underline}---among them Freud's own students---from turning
it into one. Many [have **smoothed the lurches** in drive theory
by]{.underline} empiricizing it, [grounding the abstract category in
**content**]{.underline}: aggressive symptoms (negative therapeutic
reactions, violent thoughts and acts, death wishes) or even more generic
expressions like affects of hatred, envy, or spite. While Freud himself
at times made recourse to the odd behavioral example or destructive
wish, his fundamentally speculative theory defies those runaway
thematizations. [When violent phenomena demand explanation]{.underline},
[it is **only remotely** from the clinical context and **rank with
ideology** that the drive answers]{.underline}.

Death drive as explanation for ecocide logically must be levied in
general terms: humanity is the virus, we are destroying the planet---and
if it comes to the world without us, then nature is healing. In his
prominent essay "Death Drive Nation," Blanchfield parries this false
generality with another one by excepting "America" from humanity.
American pandemic attitudes, he argues, distinctively offer "an object
lesson and exercise in the death drive": "the death drive is visible in
aggressiveness, in physical violence, self-destruction, and
self-sabotage . . . the terrain of the death drive is about severing
ties, breaking things to pieces, and even suicide . . . a nation founded
on a continent decimated by settler plagues, cleansed by genocidal war,
and fructified by chattel labor unsurprisingly . . . \[operates\] a
starkly differential calculi of exposure to premature death, workplace
risk, and general immiseration . . . this is the logic of the death
drive, distilled and accelerated, to which we cling all the way to the
very bitterest of ends." The [**original sin** rhetoric]{.underline}
abundant in so many contemporary approaches to American history
[is]{.underline} here [**gilded** with a **teleologizing** of psychic
life]{.underline}. This country wishes for death, the heart wants what
it wants.

[But the workings of the unconscious are **not quite that
orderly**]{.underline}, and even Amerikkka is a tale of two cities in
carbon form. [The pleasures of painting]{.underline}
"**[America]{.underline}**" [tout court as aggressive plague outweigh
the basic fact that **we didn't start the fire**]{.underline}---[and it
is **grossly exculpatory** to incant that we did]{.underline}. [America
is **not homogenous** economically]{.underline},
[ideologically]{.underline}, [or even libidinally]{.underline}. It is
filled with people who struggle tirelessly against autocracy, or who
abide poverty similar (by major wellbeing indicators like life
expectancy, infant mortality, and risk of homicide) to countries with a
tiny fraction of the U.S.'s per-capita economic output---or who do both
at the same time and have for generations. Where America is exceptional
is as the world's largest oil producer and simultaneously the least
representative of democracies; purposely and functionally antidemocratic
institutions like the Electoral College, localized election laws, the
Supreme Court, dysregulated campaign finance, criminalized protest, and
militarized policing cow those struggles.

[In the pretense of facing a bedrock psychic truth]{.underline}, [the
pervasive]{.underline} death drive [thesis operates as a **defense**
against the real]{.underline}: a dissimulation of class antagonism. And
embedded in this political error lurks a psychoanalytic error, a
misprision of drive. If the ideological distortion in this death drive
thesis vindicatingly grafts the particular ecocidal social class onto
the universal subject of the drive, [the libidinal distortion
**gratifyingly represses** the fact that the [drive]{.mark} itself
**[cannot operate as a cause]{.mark}**]{.underline}. [The **pervasive
theoretical malpractice** of psychologizing ecocide as]{.underline}
purposive [drive accords **new swank** to psychoanalysis]{.underline},
[but that comes at the price of **illuminative interpretative
power**]{.underline}.

For its proponents, as we have seen, drive is the effective curriculum
of destruction. But in psychoanalysis, it is something else entirely.
Right away, at the very beginning, the death drive emerges in Freud's
thought as this something else: as he contemplates the mass death event
of World War I, the question that propels him as an already established
inventor of a new science is not "'What caused this great violence' but
'How does traumatic loss constitute the subject?'" In Beyond the
Pleasure Principle, Freud finds a psychic inclination not for death, but
against it---a repression of mortality, a fantasy of heroism, a will to
revisit trauma in order to sublate it, a wish not to die. Shell-shocked
veterans, with their recurrent symptoms, present not wish fulfillment
but the lack of wish, a void where desire magnetically stirs without
object. For a different sort of thinker, the Great War context might
readily have inspired the psychologism we have seen in the contemporary
ecocidal death thesis, but Freud stood vigilant against psychologisms.
Where there is widespread insistence that we wish for our own death,
there is surely repression. And repression, the non-reckoning with the
gap in being and the discontent in culture, is the starting point for
psychoanalytic intervention, not its outcome.

Of course that reckoning is enormous, catapulting the science of
psychoanalysis toward its most speculative domains. Thus did Freud avow
in "Anxiety and Instinctual Life" that "the theory of the drives is, so
to speak, our mythology. The drives are mythical entities, magnificent
in their indefiniteness." [The very signifier]{.underline}
"**[drive]{.underline}**" [marks]{.underline} the magnetism of [what is
**unspecified**]{.underline}, the undertow of the unknown. Can the
practice in the clinic broach or tether the abstract cosmos that is its
subject? Mythology is in the room with us---the necessary fabulation of
the unconscious. [Drive theory is]{.underline} the
**[metafabulation]{.underline}**, sketching the kinesis of the
indefiniteness. In this the theory reflects its object, for, in the
words of Tracy McNulty, "the ungivenness of the death drive" engenders
"the corresponding need to substantiate or construct it." Drive is not a
positivity.

Such not-ness---[drive's [negativity]{.mark}]{.underline} as it
were---[[**constrains** it from materializing as]{.underline}]{.mark}
the unarrested progress of [[annihilation]{.underline}]{.mark} imagined
by the compulsive consensus on ecocide. Paradoxically, the movement of
the negative also constrains the drive from absolutist negativity of the
sort promulgated by celebrated antidialectical accounts of the drive.
Supposing drive as "antisocial," a "radical refusal of meaning," a pure
"emptiness" devoid of temporality, Lee Edelman's conceit founds the
theoretical malpractice in our present, for to posit drive as "the
unintelligible's unintelligibility" requires repressing that the
negative cannot self-identically engorge. Drive is constantly at odds
with itself, a movement of non-immanence that defies instantiation,
including instantiation as absolute defiance, in one direction.

Through this dynamic rather than static negativity, drive circles around
but does not willfully pursue an object. Freud underscores this
generative detour in figuring drive not as line but as short circuit:
Kurzschluss, an aperture and excess. To the drive, the object "is
strictly speaking of no importance. It is a matter of total
indifference," as Lacan, quoting Freud, defines. Radically objectless,
drive is not bullet-journal attainment of a wish. On the contrary, drive
is goal-obstructed or zielgehemmt---variable, contingent, and evincible
only in its vicissitudes. [[If there is a wish to]{.mark} return
to]{.underline} a prior state of [[inanimacy]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[we
are]{.mark} **pretty [bad at fulfilling that]{.mark}
wish**]{.underline}. What is drive but the topology of failing that
return? What is life but the accumulation of the failure to die?
[The]{.underline} death drive ecocide [thesis **represses**]{.underline}
all this failure, obstruction, indirection---all this
[life]{.underline}---[and takes pleasure in a **fantasized facility** of
the unconscious behind an **imagined immutability** of]{.underline}
climate [calamity]{.underline}.

### 2AC\-\--Psycho False

#### Psychoanalytic explanations of behavior are either [wrong]{.underline} or [unfalsifiable]{.underline}---[conscious]{.underline} choice determines behavior.

**Newell and Starks, 23**---Professor of Behavioural Science in the
School of Psychology at UNSW Sydney; Professor of Psychology and Deputy
Dean of the Faculty of Brain Sciences at University College London (Ben
and David, "Reclaiming the Science of the Mind," Open Minded: Searching
for Truth about the Unconscious Mind, Chapter 1, pp. 10-12, dml)

The modern fields of psychology and psychotherapy have not been
particularly kind to Freud's views, often regarding psychoanalysis as a
pseudoscience.14 But it is worth distinguishing between his conception
of the unconscious on the one hand and his empirical methods on the
other. Today we tend to regard dream interpretation as akin to
storytelling: [[the psychoanalyst]{.mark} provides an
[interpretation]{.mark}]{.underline} of the dream in terms of
psychoanalytic theory, [but [since]{.mark} this is necessarily done
**[after the fact]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [such interpretations [are
**untestable**]{.mark}]{.underline}. [The [**hallmark** of]{.mark}
scientific [theories is]{.mark} their ability to make]{.underline}
potentially **[[falsifiable predictions]{.underline}]{.mark}**,
[something that [psycho]{.mark}analysis [**struggles** to
do]{.mark}]{.underline} ([and even when it does]{.underline}, [[they
**rarely emerge in credit**]{.mark} when tested]{.underline}). None of
this means that the concept of the unconscious is itself incoherent,
although modern conceptions of the unconscious are rather more general
than those accepted by Freud. For example, we have no difficulty today
in accepting the possibility of unconscious perception, in which events
and objects in the environment around us can in theory influence our
behavior even when we are unaware of them (as in subliminal perception).

[Reportable]{.underline}, [conscious states seem to underlie **many** of
our decisions and behaviors]{.underline}. To state the obvious,
[[we]{.mark} often [do things because we have **conscious
reasons**]{.mark} for doing them]{.underline}. This may seem trivial but
is nonetheless worth dwelling on, not least because many influential
schools of thought---most notably behaviorism---have been deeply
skeptical about drawing straightforward links between conscious reports
and behavior. Some of this skepticism is partly justified. For example,
we need to be very careful about the reactive effects of verbal reports:
the very act of giving a description of our mental states may change
those states.15 However, modern theories and methods make an emphatic
case for the importance of conscious knowledge, beliefs, and attitudes
in determining behavior.

[This is exemplified in the theory of planned behavior]{.underline}
(**[TPB]{.underline}**), one of the most influential and truly deep
theories in all of the behavioral sciences.16 [[TPB offers a]{.mark}
**deceptively [simple]{.mark}** [explanation]{.mark} for why we act as
we do]{.underline}. It says that [[only **two** things
matter]{.underline}]{.mark}: [our [**intentions** and]{.mark} our
**[control]{.mark}**]{.underline}. [When we have a **strong intention**
to engage in a behavior and perceive that we have **control** over that
behavior]{.underline}, [then **we will do it**]{.underline}. [Take
**smoking cessation** as an example]{.underline}: [people who intend to
give up smoking and perceive that they have control over whether they
smoke will give up]{.underline}, [whereas those without either the
intention or the perception of control]{.underline} (or both) [will
not]{.underline}.17 The theory in addition says that [[intentions come
from]{.mark} **three** sources]{.underline}. First, we are very strongly
influenced by **[[subjective norms]{.underline}]{.mark}**, our
internalization of the views of other people whose opinions matter to
us. If your partner's views matter to you and your partner takes a dim
view of your smoking, you're more likely to form the intention to give
up. Second, [our **[attitudes]{.mark}**]{.underline} to the behavior are
important. The better you feel about giving up smoking, the more likely
you are to form the intention to give up. Finally, [your estimation of
[how **easy** or **difficult**]{.mark} it will be]{.underline} to give
up will also influence your intention. This sense of control is the same
factor mentioned above: it is assumed to have a direct impact on
behavior as well as an indirect impact via intentions.

According to TPB, it is [**only** these factors]{.underline}, [and **[no
others]{.mark}**]{.underline}, that [[matter]{.underline}]{.mark}. You
might object, for example, that surely personality matters. Aren't
extroverts less likely to quit smoking than introverts? The theory
doesn't deny that such factors could be associated with giving up
smoking, but it says that the only pathways by which they can do so are
via subjective norms, attitudes, or perceived control. Thus, if
extroverts do indeed struggle to stop smoking, it must be because they
feel less pressure from other people's views, or they have a less
favorable attitude to stopping smoking, or they do not feel they have
adequate control.

The precise details of the theory are not crucial; what matters is that
[[the theory]{.mark} is **eminently testable**]{.underline}
([and]{.underline} indeed it [[has survived]{.underline}]{.mark} more or
less intact despite [[**thirty years** of]{.mark} extensive
[testing]{.mark}]{.underline}) and that [all its ingredients are
**conscious**]{.underline}, [**reportable aspects** of
behavior]{.underline}.18 When researchers set out to test the theory,
they do so by constructing questionnaires comprising many items, all of
which attempt to measure different aspects of the person's subjective
norms, attitudes, and perceived control. Each item (such as Most people
who are important to me think I should give up smoking in the next 12
months) is accompanied by a rating scale on which the respondent
indicates their agreement or disagreement with the statement, and the
different items under each type are averaged to give a single measure of
the three predictors for that person. Then an analysis is conducted to
determine whether those individuals with strong subjective norms,
attitudes, and perceived control for quitting smoking are indeed more
likely to quit. Clearly, the questionnaires elicit conscious, reportable
beliefs and attitudes; [this]{.underline} is a theory that fundamentally
[attributes behavior to a combination of **fully conscious**
factors]{.underline}. [The theory]{.underline} could be supplemented by
additional unconscious factors, though these would of course have to be
measured by some means other than explicit reports (the implicit
association test, discussed at length in chapter 5, would be an
example). What is remarkable is that TPB [has achieved]{.underline} the
[explanatory successes]{.underline} that it has even
[**[without]{.mark}** the addition of [**unconscious**
factors]{.mark}]{.underline} (the jury is still out on whether there are
domains in which such factors would extend its power even more).

### 2AC/1AR\-\--Psycho False\-\--Extra

#### The negative's use of psychoanalysis applied beyond the individual level is [wrong]{.underline} \-\-- Freud agrees.

Siegfried **Zepf** 3-1-20**23**, Siegfried Zepf, MD, is a Training
analyst (DPG, DGPT) and the former director of the Institute of
Psychoanalysis, Psychotherapy and Psychosomatic Medicine, University of
Saarland (Germany). "Metaphors in psychoanalytic theory -- Do we need
them?" https://doi.org/10.1080/0803706X.2022.2075565 //MDC

Final remarks

Without a defined target domain, [the metaphorization of psychoanalytic
concepts allows every psycho analyst to create his/her "individually
chosen array of metaphor]{.underline}" (Wallerstein, 2005, p. 102).
Wallerstein′s "scientific metaphors" lead to a situation in which
"admitted metaphors \[which\] have no specific content can be filled in
to suit one's fancy" (Nagel, 1959, p. 41). [When psychoanalysis presents
individual conceptions in the form of general metaphors, it promotes
what Kubie (1975, p. 14) has criticized as "sources of fallacies" and
the danger Freud (1912, p. 117) once warned his colleagues of, namely
"of projecting outwards some of the peculiarities of his own
personality, which he has dimly perceived, into the field of science,
**as a theory having universal validity**]{.underline}."

As the informational content of Wallerstein′s "scientific metaphors" is
zero, since [there are no absent concepts on which the majority of
psychoanalysts have agreed, care should be taken to ensure that
"psychoanalytic theory ... is freed from this plague of metaphorical
language, which has been **mistaken for scientific
theory**]{.underline}" (Kubie, 1975, p. 14).

I end by indicating that following Kubie's criticism does not
necessarily mean that closure and coherence will disappear from the
psychoanalytic community. The realization of his intention simply
demands that we do not exhaust our tolerance in accepting mutually
exclusive concepts, but anchor it firmly in a common search for truth,
remind ourselves that [Freud (1933, p. 156) did "not want to
commend\[psychoanalysis\] to your interest as a method of treatment but
on account of the truths it contains" and accept "that there is more
than one way to seek the truth" (]{.underline}Jurist &Orfanos, 2016, p.
S3).If we were to familiarize our selves with Freud's recommendation, we
would be obliged to pursue truth as the goal guiding our search for
knowledge, and to regard conceptual pluralism as a field in which we
continue to seek truth in solidarity in differing conceptual views.

#### Psychoanalysis is totally false. Even if it's not, it's pretty much useless. 

David **Stewart** 11-15-20**18**. David G. Stewart, PhD, ABPP, is a
board-certified clinical child and adolescent psychologist and Dean of
the California School of Professional Psychology. Dr. Stewart received
his PhD in Clinical Psychology from the University of California San
Diego and completed his internship and postdoctoral fellowship in Child
Community Psychology at the University of Washington, School of
Medicine. \"Are Freud and Psychoanalysis Still Relevant?,\" Alliant
International University,
https://www.alliant.edu/blog/are-freud-and-psychoanalysis-still-relevant.
苗

[Sigmund Freud]{.underline}, 80 years after his death, is
[still]{.underline}, arguably, the [most well-known name in
psychoanalysis]{.underline} in the world. [Considered the father of
modern psychology]{.underline}, his theories and ideas on the
connections that exist between the conscious mind, the subconscious
mind, the body, and the world around us are still as widely known as
they were when he first espoused them at the turn of the 20th century.
However, as we have had, now, a century-plus of building off of Freud's
theories, how relevant are his ideas with what we now understand about
both the human mind and the way in which we can (and maybe should) go
about treating the illnesses contained within it? Are the foundations of
psychoanalysis that Freud established still the foundations that modern
psychotherapy should be built off of, or do his ideas lack the nuance
that modern mental health care and analysis demand?

**[Freud Is an Outdated Fossil]{.underline}**

It's [important]{.underline} to [remember]{.underline} that
[psychoanalysis is about revealing deeper insights into a person's
psyche,]{.underline} and this is **[vastly different]{.underline}** from
how we view the **[modern methodology]{.underline}** and usage of
psychoanalysis and psychotherapy. Especially for those working in the
field and engaged in the process itself, the [idea of]{.underline}
[basic id/ego/superego breakdown]{.underline} and dream analysis are
**[deeply simplistic techniques]{.underline}** and ideas [that will **do
little** in the way of addressing the issues that modern mental health
care exists to address.]{.underline}

Many of [Freud's methods]{.underline}, [techniques]{.underline}, and
[conclusions]{.underline} have been [put into question,]{.underline}
even to the point where some of his [theories]{.underline} have even
become **[viewed as damaging]{.underline}**---and even dangerous---[to
certain segments of the population, such as his views on homosexuality
and women.]{.underline}

The [trouble with Freud]{.underline} is that, [while his ideas appear
intriguing and even appeal to our common sense]{.underline}, there's
**[very little scientific evidence to back them up.]{.underline}**
Modern [psychology]{.underline} has [produced]{.underline} very [little
to support many of his claims in the decades since their initial
presentation.]{.underline} For instance, there's no scientific evidence
in support of the idea that boys lust after their mothers and hate their
fathers. Also, there's [**no proof of the id, ego, or superego**, and
their respective aspects of control over the human psyche,]{.underline}
much as we still like to use those terminologies today.

And this is [just a small sampling of his theories and **ideas that have
been debunked** in the decades since their presentation.]{.underline}

Freud is Still Relevant, But Only as a Reference Point

[Freud's legacy has transcended science]{.underline}, with his ideas
permeating deep into Western culture. As psychologist and Freud critic
John Kihlstrom himself admits, "More than Einstein or Watson and Crick,
more than Hitler or Lenin, Roosevelt or Kennedy, more than Picasso,
Eliot, or Stravinsky, more than the Beatles or Bob Dylan, Freud\'s
influence on modern culture has been profound and long-lasting."

But, [Freud has, for the most part, **fallen completely out of favor in
academics**]{.underline}. Simply put, [no one taking]{.underline}
[psychology seriously would use him as a credible source.]{.underline}
In 1996, [Psychological S]{.underline}cience reached the conclusion
that, "[There is literally nothing to be said, scientifically or
therapeutically,]{.underline} to [the advantage of the entire Freudian
system or any of its component dogmas.]{.underline}\" **[As a research
paradigm]{.underline}**, it's pretty much **[dead.]{.underline}**

But wait, how does that mean he's still relevant? Well...

Freud was absolutely correct in his assertion that we are not masters of
our own mind. He showed that human experience, thought, and deeds are
not exclusively driven by our conscious mind, but by forces outside our
conscious awareness and control---ones that we could eventually
understand through the therapeutic process he called, "psychoanalysis."
Today, very few would argue against the idea of the unconscious mind,
and Freud's claim for the central role of the unconscious mind in human
actions is as relevant to psychology today as it was then (see the
following collection of essays called Frontiers of Consciousness).

[Freud also argued]{.underline} for the idea that the [brain can be
compartmentalized,]{.underline} that brain function can be broken down
into individual parts. His take on this, of course, was **[incredibly
primitive]{.underline}**, [as Freud mostly spoke of the ego, id, and
superego---ideas **we don't really accept any more,**]{.underline} as
mentioned above. But, his [larger]{.underline} [thesis]{.underline} of
psychic compartmentalization has gone to [influence such thinkers as the
cognitive scientist Marvin Minsky, who talks about the society of
mind.]{.underline}

As well, Freud\'s take on defense mechanisms still holds relevance. Few
people, including psychologists, would deny that we all too regularly
employ such defenses as denial, repression, projection,
intellectualization, and rationalization. The same can be said for his
ideas on transference and catharsis. And though we no **[longer
subscribe to Freudian dream interpretation]{.underline}**, some of our
dreams are so blatantly driven by our conscious and subconscious desires
and fears that it's obvious Freud was onto something with that idea,
too.

[Ultimately, Freud's modern **contributions apply more to attitudes**
than any actual therapeutic methods,]{.underline} though this is not a
bad thing. He established foundations that modern mental health care are
built off of, and the modern attitude of those engaging in the mental
health care process certainly seem to stem from his main thesis
statement as to what patients should be getting out of therapy, which,
simply put, is, "Know thyself."

#### Triune brain model is false, and all theories based in it are wrong.

Patrick R. **Steffen** **et al.** 3-31-20**22**. Patrick R. Steffen;
Department of Psychology, Brigham Young University, Provo, UT, United
States. Dawson Hedges; Departments of Psychology and Neuroscience,
Brigham Young University, Provo, UT, United States. Rebekka Matheson;
Departments of Psychology and Neuroscience, Brigham Young University,
Provo, UT, United States. \"The Brain Is Adaptive Not Triune: How the
Brain Responds to Threat, Challenge, and Change,\" Front. Psychiatry,
Sec. Psychological Therapy and Psychosomatics, Volume 13,
https://doi.org/10.3389/fpsyt.2022.802606. 苗

[Theory impacts how research is conducted]{.underline}. An
[influential]{.underline} [theory]{.underline} used to [conceptualize
brain function]{.underline} and drive research has been the **[triune
brain theory]{.underline}** (1--4). The triune-brain approach to
understanding the brain takes an evolutionary perspective about how the
brain has developed under environmental pressures and how that
development impacts our responses, particularly our responses to stress.
Describing the triune-brain theory, MacLean (3) states that:

The [human forebrain]{.underline} [evolved]{.underline} to its great
size while [retaining features of three basic formations]{.underline}
that reflect an [ancestral relationship to reptiles]{.underline}, early
mammals, and recent mammals. The [three neural]{.underline}
[assemblies]{.underline}... are radically different in structure and
chemistry, and in an evolutionary sense, countless generations apart.
[Psychological and behavioral functions depend on the interplay of three
quite different mentalities]{.underline}. The [three **evolutionary
formations**]{.underline} might be [popularly regarded as
three]{.underline} [interconnected biological]{.underline}
[computers]{.underline}, each having its own [special
intelligence]{.underline}, its **[own subjectivity]{.underline}**, its
own sense of time and space, and its [own memory, motor, and other
functions]{.underline}." (p. 264).

From the [perspective of the triune-brain theory, these three brain
regions]{.underline} evolved separately and function somewhat
independently: the [basal ganglia and brain stem]{.underline} are
involved in movement and [basic life functions]{.underline}, the [limbic
system]{.underline} is involved in [emotional responses]{.underline}
that are seen more prominently in mammals as compared to reptiles, and
the [cortex is involved in cognition]{.underline} and executive
functions and is most prominent in humans. In this perspective,
evolutionary development begins with basic behavioral responses, then
adds emotional responses that can alter these basic responses when
threat or challenges arise, and then adds on cognition to alter
emotional responses using reason, logic, and planning.

There are several [key problems]{.underline}, however,
[with]{.underline} the **[triune brain theory]{.underline}**. First, the
[brain did not evolve in]{.underline} [successive stages]{.underline} as
MacLean hypothesized (5). The idea that vertebrate evolution has
consisted of "newer brain structures being superimposed over and on top
of 'older\' brain structures, tracking development of complex
cognition," is [not evolutionarily justifiable]{.underline} (6). [Basic
neural regions are shared]{.underline} [among all
vertebrates]{.underline}. Where they differ is in proportion and extent.
Just as an elephant\'s trunk is not a new structure superimposed over a
snout but rather is an analogous structure differing in proportion (and
consequently, in functional adaptation to the animal\'s needs), [the
human brain is not superimposed on a reptile brain but consists mostly
of proportionally different analogous structures.]{.underline}
Furthermore, the [gradation of proportional]{.underline}
[shifts]{.underline} is **[not necessarily a linear
progression]{.underline}** from reptile to human (7).

Second, [brain structures do not function independently of one another
(]{.underline}5). During [emotional responses]{.underline}, there is
[activity in the amygdala]{.underline} and in the limbic system, but
there is [also activity in cortical areas]{.underline} and in the
brainstem (8). Additionally, [emotion and cognition]{.underline} are
**[not independent events]{.underline}**; rather, they are [interrelated
functions working in concert.]{.underline} For example, Bush et al. (9)
and Shackman et al. (10) both note that emotional responses and
cognitive responses in the cingulate cortex are interconnected and not
separate as previously believed. [Perhaps more importantly, the limbic
system is not a purely emotional center in the brain]{.underline}.
LeDoux (8) notes that the [hippocampus]{.underline} is considered part
of the limbic system but that it is not considered an essentially
emotional brain region; [instead]{.underline}, it is a key area
[involved in memory,]{.underline} which is more [closely associated with
**cognition**.]{.underline} Because of these and similar problems, the
term "[limbic system" is no longer a commonly used term to describe how
the brain functions.]{.underline} "[Limbic system" also loses its
utility in a clinical setting;]{.underline} because affect is a
culmination of a wide range of interrelated processes, including
synthesis of internal and external stimuli, arousal, and memory,
[approach **to disorders characterized** by affect dysregulation is
**limited** by the triune brain approach]{.underline}. And, finally, the
[brain does not act by simply responding to a stimulus]{.underline}.
Instead, it **[predicts]{.underline}** **[internal and external
needs]{.underline}** and adapts accordingly. [Incoming stimuli interact
with the current state in which the brain is (11).]{.underline}

Third, [current]{.underline} [neuroscience]{.underline}
[research]{.underline} findings [provide further evidence of the
inaccuracy]{.underline} of [triune brain]{.underline}
[theory]{.underline} and open [new ways of understanding]{.underline}
how the brain responds to stress and adapts to changing internal and
external environments. Fear [research provides an instructive specific
example]{.underline}. There is [no]{.underline} [fear brain
circuit]{.underline} that turns on during a fear response but otherwise
lies dormant (8). [Brain networks]{.underline} [always have some level
of activity (]{.underline}12) that affects how they process incoming
information (11). What changes is the [relative activity of different
brain networks,]{.underline} with [networks]{.underline} being
[differentially activated]{.underline} based upon need (13--16). As
these [findings show]{.underline}, [triune-brain theory does not match
current research]{.underline} [findings]{.underline} and **[using
triune-brain theory]{.underline}** as a general theoretical approach
**[can lead to faulty hypothesis creation and poorly developed
studies.]{.underline}**

A more useful evolutionary theory of how the brain works needs to
integrate accurate knowledge of brain structure and function.
Adaptation, survival, and reproduction are at the heart of evolutionary
theory, and interdependent brain networks have evolved to increase
adaptation to be able to survive and reproduce. [Further, emerging
findings suggest that the brain uses interoceptive and exteroceptive
information to predict future conditions and needs to enable optimal
adaptation to continuously changing internal and external
environments]{.underline} (11--20). Based on better understanding of how
the brain works, we propose replacing "triune brain" with a term that
better captures current understanding of brain function: the adaptive
brain. In this conceptualization, the term adaptive brain emphasizes the
interdependence and plasticity of brain regions and the brain\'s ability
to predict and adapt to future needs and conditions. Instead of three
relatively independent brain regions, or any number of independent brain
regions, [brain networks work together interdependently; instead of
purely "emotional circuits" or "cognitive circuits]{.underline}," the
b[rain uses interconnected networks to optimize maintenance of the
body\'s internal state, emotion, and cognition to adapt to continuously
changing needs]{.underline} (11). The brain\'s summated approach to
[these priorities regulates affect, and dysregulation of these
interdependent circuits has important implications for
psychopathology.]{.underline}

#### Psychoanalysis is universally not accepted in psychology. Prefer -- ev comes from APA.

Amy **Novotney** **citing** Nancy **McWilliams** 12-20**17**. Amy
Novotney is a freelance writer based on Chicago. Nancy McWilliams; PhD,
a psychology professor at Rutgers University and author of several books
on psychoanalysis. \"How psychoanalysis got a bad rap,\" Monitor on
Psychology, Vol 48, No. 11,
https://www.apa.org/monitor/2017/12/psychoanalysis-bad-rap. 苗

Why did the treatment get set aside, even disdained, among psychologists
in the mid-1900s? Several factors were at play, says Nancy McWilliams,
PhD, a psychology professor at Rutgers University and author of several
books on psychoanalysis.

For one, in the [1950s and 1960s]{.underline}, the [psychodynamic
approach lost ground because those who were studying and practicing it
were **too dismissive of other methods**,]{.underline} according to
McWilliams.

\"[Psychoanalytic work got to be very prestigious in
psychiatry]{.underline}, and it attracted a lot of people---mostly
white, heterosexual males---[to the field who were **more interested in
obtaining status** as the head of a psychiatry
department,\"]{.underline} says McWilliams.

At the same time, [many psychoanalytic institutes moved out of
university settings]{.underline}, cutting themselves off from broader
intellectual work, and [began including only psychiatrists in their
training there]{.underline}, says Pratyusha Tummala-Narra, PhD, a
professor of counseling, developmental and educational psychology at
Boston College. In [addition, psychoanalytic theorists **did not attend
adequately** to the **need for disseminating research** on the
effectiveness of psychoanalytic psychotherapy]{.underline}, while
studies on **[CBT]{.underline}** and **[other similar therapy
approaches]{.underline}** [showed promising results more
visibly.]{.underline}

No quick fix

[Another turnoff for American psychologists was the method\'s less
optimistic]{.underline}, [more European orientation]{.underline},
McWilliams says. \"Freud\'s belief that psychoanalysis can help people
turn neurotic misery into ordinary human unhappiness doesn\'t fit well
in a culture where we are given to utopian movements and self-help books
and quick fixes,\" McWilliams says. \"[Americans have been ingrained
with the idea that things should be fixable.\"]{.underline}

Also[, unlike Europeans]{.underline}, Americans often
[emphasize]{.underline} the importance of individual
[independence]{.underline}, says McWilliams, and much of
[psychoanalytic]{.underline} theory is about [letting yourself feel
deeply attached]{.underline} to another person---the therapist---and
then seeing what emotions that brings out.

**[Outdated theories]{.underline}**

In addition, in their original form, [many of the constructs that Freud
introduced,]{.underline} such as the Oedipus complex and the idea of
[psychosexual stages,]{.underline} seem [dated]{.underline} and
[peculiar]{.underline} and often do not resonate with people today[,
says Ken Levy, PhD]{.underline}, a [clinical psychology
professor]{.underline} at **[Penn State University]{.underline}** who
maintains a small psychodynamically oriented private practice.

\"When you read some of the secondary sources, such as textbooks, that
mention Freud, it does often seem silly, but many of his ideas have been
integrated into broader psychology, often without sufficient recognition
or credit,\" Levy says.

#### Psychoanalysis is based on one person, and Freud didn't even treat her. 

Tomiwa **Owolade** 10-18-20**21**. Tomiwa Owolade is a freelance writer
and the author of This is Not America, which is out in paperback in May.
\"Psychoanalysis doesn\'t work,\" UnHerd,
https://unherd.com/2021/10/psychoanalysis-doesnt-work/?us=1. 苗

In 1881, a [young woman]{.underline} in Vienna [discovered]{.underline}
she had [lost her native German tongue]{.underline}. She could speak
nothing but English. Bertha [Pappenheim was diagnosed by her doctor with
hysteria]{.underline}. The symptoms included contractures,
hallucinations, and aphasia ― the [loss of basic functions such as
language]{.underline}. They were [resistant to conventional
treatment]{.underline}; and so Pappenheim became [the first ever patient
of psychoanaly]{.underline}sis. Her name, in one of the **[practice's
foundational texts]{.underline}**, Studies on Hysteria, **[is Anna
O.]{.underline}**

The [original patient of psychoanalysis]{.underline} was [never
treated]{.underline} by the father of psychoanalysis,
[Sigmund]{.underline} [Freud]{.underline}; she was treated instead by
its John the Baptist, Josef Breuer, who co-wrote Studies on Hysteria.
"The discovery of Breuer's," wrote Freud of his colleague in 1917, ["is
still the foundation of psycho-analytical theory]{.underline}". What
Breuer discovered, and Freud later developed, [was that neurotic
conditions could be treated by a "talking cure".]{.underline} By talking
about her childhood experiences to the analyst, the patient can relieve
herself of the disorders that burden her --- and subsequently alleviate,
with the help of the analyst, the physical manifestations of such
disorders.

[Psychoanalysis]{.underline}, then, is [medical treatment by
language.]{.underline} Adam Phillips, one of the leading psychoanalysts
in England, writes that "[Freud was as much, if not more, of writer than
a doctor]{.underline}". Like the [Fin-de-Siècle writers who uncovered
the dark undercurrents of bourgeois conventions]{.underline}, or the
[modernist writers who questioned coherent narratives]{.underline},
Freud was an integral part of the lively intellectual culture that
witnessed the collapse of the Austro-Hungarian Empire and much of
continental Europe. And it was [very much a literary
culture]{.underline}.

To briefly psychoanalyse: as a child Freud was gifted at languages,
became familiar with Greek, Latin, Spanish, Italian, French and English.
And yet it was medicine he ultimately studied at the University of
Vienna: as the firstborn son, he wanted to be dutiful and serve his
impoverished family.

#### It also just doesn't work. Case study.

Tomiwa **Owolade** 10-18-20**21**. Tomiwa Owolade is a freelance writer
and the author of This is Not America, which is out in paperback in May.
\"Psychoanalysis doesn\'t work,\" UnHerd,
https://unherd.com/2021/10/psychoanalysis-doesnt-work/?us=1. 苗

Mikkel Borch-Jacobsen, in Freud's Patients: A Book of Lives (out last
month), thinks not[. "Freud's cures were largely ineffectual," he
writes, "when they were not downright destructive".]{.underline}
Borch-Jacobsen **[examines 38 patients]{.underline}** Freud treated in
his 50-year practice, [but his book is concerned less with the efficacy
of psychoanalysis and more with portraying]{.underline}, in selective
but vivid detail, [the individuals Freud treated.]{.underline} "We all
know the characters described by Freud in his case studies," he writes,
"but do we know these illustrious pseudonyms?" His book is "an attempt
to reconstruct the sometimes comical, most often tragic and always
captivating stories of these patients who have long been nameless and
faceless".

Anna Von Lieben, referred to as Cäcilie M by Freud, was his most
important patient for a period of six years, beginning in 1887. She was,
Borch-Jacobsen writes, part of the "Viennese Jewish upper bourgeoisie":
brought up in a palace, where her family hosted Brahms, Strauss and
Liszt. She was treated in Paris by Freud's hero, Jean-Martin Charcot,
otherwise known as the Napoleon of Neuroses. In Vienna, she was also a
patient of Breuer.

Von Lieben was obese. She tried to curb this by following a strict diet
of caviar and champagne. And she was an accomplished chess player: she
liked to play two games simultaneously, and, while she slept, she would
have a chess player on standby near her room in case she woke up and
fancied a game. Perhaps not unrelatedly, Von Lieben had facial neuralgia
and attacks of the nerves. For this, Freud was brought in to treat her.

He was her "nerve doctor". He used hypnosis to purge her of childhood
traumas. [Unlike the talking cure Breuer used on Pappenheim, however ---
in which the patient simply remembers her traumatic experiences --- in
Freud's talking cure the patient is invited "to relive and forget their
traumas]{.underline}". But he also **[gave her morphine]{.underline}**
--- and **[this was what stopped]{.underline}** her neurotic
**[attacks]{.underline}**, [not hypnosis]{.underline}. "The [famous
cathartic cure]{.underline} of Cäcilie M", writes Borsch-Jacobsen,
**["was in fact a morphine cure".]{.underline}**

Her husband, Leopold von Lieben[, "finally decided to end her treatment
with Freud, which had spanned almost six years and had **produced no
lasting improvement".**]{.underline} Anna von Lieben died of a cardiac
arrest when she was 53. Her children referred to Freud as der Zauberer
--- the magician --- who performed worthless tricks on their mother.

### 1AR\-\--AT: Death Drive

#### The death drive thesis [oversimplifies]{.underline}, [links to itself]{.underline}, and [decks]{.underline} alt solvency.

**Karbelnig, 21**---Senior Faculty, The New Center for Psychoanalysis
(Alan, "Laying the death drive to rest," International Forum of
Psychoanalysis, May 17, 2021, dml)

Various systems theories, ranging from Stolorow's (1997, 2011, 2013,
2015) phenomenological contextualism to Galatzer-Levy's (1995, 2002,
2009) models, suggest [aggression is an **emergent**, **[complex
phenomenon irreducible to any]{.mark} [singular cause]{.mark}**. These
**nonlinear**, **dynamic**, and other **interactive** systems
models]{.underline}, such as those proposed by Baranger and Baranger
(1979), Coburn (2002), Ferro (1993, 2009), Ferro and Basile, (2009),
Katz (2013), and Miller (1999, 2008a, 2008b), [are **consistent** with
the]{.underline} cross-theoretical, perspectival [psychoanalytic
approach]{.underline} presented in the assumptions section above. [These
theories]{.underline} provide coherent models or metaphors that
[encompass the **multifaceted**, **complex dynamism** of the human
mind]{.underline}. And no reason exists to not incorporate the drives
into them. Schmidt-Hellerau (2005) writes, "A psychoanalytic model of
the mind without drives is like a house without people who live in it,
who use and invest in its equipment in a specifically meaningful way,"
adding that, without the concept of drives, psychoanalytic understanding
"goes rather flat; it loses its depth, its connection to unconscious
strivings and fantasies and to vital bodily needs, its dynamics, and its
directedness (pp. 1024--1025). Mirroring her work, Mills (2002, 2005,
2006, 2012) integrates drive theory into relational psychoanalysis.
Reclaiming eros as foundational drive allows for much the psychoanalytic
opus to survive unscathed. Kline (2018) proposes, as I do, a
"nondualistic interpretation ... that both drives \[eros and thanatos\]
are manifestations of one central current of energy defined as libido"
(p. 68). Such a conclusion also comports with Erel's (2020) organizing
Winnicott's varying concepts on aggression into a singular phenomenon,
"love/strife" (p. 263n).

Concluding remarks

Illustrative of Lacan's (1991) university discourse, [[**controversies**
over]{.mark} the [death instinct]{.mark} will **[linger]{.mark}
forever**. They demonstrate the **problem** with]{.underline} postmodern
relativism, namely [the **ongoing struggle** for an **overarching
truth**]{.underline}. For example, [although [**biology** argues
against]{.mark} a [death drive]{.mark}, the **[ontological
assumption]{.mark}** of the unconscious [allows]{.mark} for
**[interdisciplinary contradiction]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Fortunately,
psychoanalytic scholars enjoy argument. Rich fodder remains --
particularly for a subject as complicated as aggression. However, [the
[death drive]{.mark} concept poses **five distinct
problems**]{.underline} for psychoanalysis. As just noted, [it
[conflicts]{.mark} with the [**basic precepts** of
biology]{.mark}]{.underline}. Freud's (1920, 1924, 1930) and Klein's
(1932, 1946, 1948, 1959) death drive solved a problem for a time -- like
aether did for physics. It explained destructive behaviors before
psychoanalysis matured to better explain them. Justifying the death
instinct with cell apoptosis, like Spielrein (1994) did and Hoffman
(2004) and Ameisen (1999) do, adds more fuel to the argumentative fire.
[On the **one** hand]{.underline}, apoptosis can be interpreted in
support of the death drive. [Biological processes involve **destructive

[cells]{.mark} can equally be interpreted as
**[life-enhancing]{.mark}**]{.underline}.

[Using the [death drive in clinical work]{.mark} proves
**[problematic]{.mark}**]{.underline}. [Many still use it,

Additionally, [the phrase itself, [death drive]{.mark}, invites a

Further, and as also noted previously, systems and related models

### 2AC\-\--AT: Kristeva

#### Kristeva's framing of abjection is self-reifying.

Robbie **Duschinsky** 11-1-20**13**. Robbie Duschinsky is Professor of
Social Science and Health and Head of the Applied Social Science Group
within the Primary Care Unit. He is also Director of Studies in
Sociology at Sidney Sussex College. \"Abjection and Self-Identity:
Towards a Revised Account of Purity and Impurity,\" The Sociological
Review, Volume 61, Issue 4, https://doi.org/10.1111/1467-954X.12081. 苗

[Kristeva]{.underline} suggests that the [basic heterogeneous flux
of]{.underline} [existence]{.underline} is curtailed and [delineated to
form the]{.underline} '[symbolic' order of meaningful language and
personal subjectivity]{.underline}, and that 'the danger of filth
represents for the subject the risk to which the very symbolic order is
permanently exposed, to the extent that it is a device of
discriminations, of differences' (1982a: 69). This curtailment and
delineation of heterogeneity at the level of language repeats, [Kristeva
argues]{.underline}, the [subject\'s original struggle as an
infant]{.underline} to [develop]{.underline} an [autonomous identity
**vis-à-vis**]{.underline} our [mother]{.underline}:
'**[abjection]{.underline}** preserves what [existed in the
archaism]{.underline} of [pre-objectal relationship]{.underline}, in the
immemorial violence with which a body becomes separated from another
body in order to be' (1982a: 10). Kristeva thus [superimposes upon
Douglas\'s structural-functionalist argument]{.underline} the [proposal
that what runs against classificatory systems]{.underline} or the social
structure is impure because of its association with the early, infantile
period of human life before the distinction of subjects from objects,
self from mother: 'defilement is the translinguistic spoor of the most
archaic boundaries of the self\'s clean and proper body. In that sense,
if it is a jettisoned object, it is so from the mother (1982a: 74).

[Kristeva\'s description of abjection is evocative]{.underline}, and
vast in scope. It [promises profound insight on a range of]{.underline}
important [topics]{.underline}: the construction of identity; the
operation of language; the meaning of negative emotions; the psychology
of phobia; horror narratives as a literary genre, the repudiation and
oppression of outsiders, violence against women, inter alia. As a
result, [Kristeva\'s concept of 'abjection' has been widely]{.underline}
[used in the arts, humanities and social sciences]{.underline}. Yet
Menninghaus (2003) has suggested that since the [late]{.underline}
[1980s]{.underline} there has been [high demand for new social theory on
the topic of purity and impurity, and]{.underline} particularly in the
spheres of Anglophone politics, academia, art and cultural production.
These [themes]{.underline} have great [social importance, but have been
**inadequately and insufficiently theorized**]{.underline}. This is a
[point]{.underline} that has also **[been made by
Kristeva]{.underline}** (2004b: 155) herself: 'My [investigation into
abjection, violence and horror]{.underline} **[... picks up on a certain
vacuum']{.underline}** (see also Krauss 1999). It is not a
[question]{.underline} which other scholars or 'the media has bothered
to [develop ... and yet it is truly of capital importance']{.underline}
(2000: 20), and thus '[this venture]{.underline} into the (admittedly)
[dangerous terrain of abjection earned me much support']{.underline}
(2002: 161). [However]{.underline}, [Menninghaus]{.underline} has
[argued]{.underline}, this uptake of the **[concept of
abjection]{.underline}** has occurred in a way that has
'**[reified']{.underline}** the [theory]{.underline}, [in order to
circumvent Kristeva\'s claims]{.underline} that [purity]{.underline} and
impurity [classifications]{.underline} [are 'universally]{.underline}
and biologically [grounded]{.underline} (in the maternal body)' (2003:
390). This '[reified' account has been able to]{.underline} [engage with
themes of purity and impurity]{.underline} -- but does so, Menninghaus
suggests, **[without an adequate]{.underline}** **[theoretical
basis]{.underline}**. Though this certainly does not apply to all
researchers drawing on Kristeva, de Nooy (1998: 289) has concluded from
her review of academic uses of [Powers of Horror]{.underline} that in
practice 'the [abject' has tended to 'become a catch-all term for
"yucky" stuff']{.underline}. Campkin (2007: 79[) has concluded his
review of work on purity and impurity by asserting that 'the topic as a
whole has been **under theorized'.**]{.underline}

### 1AR\-\--AT: "Neuropsychoanalysis"

#### Neuropsychoanalysis is a [pseudoscientific misrepresentation]{.underline} of both neuroscience and psychoanalysis.

Robert **Samuels** 5-8-20**25**, Robert Samuels is a Senior Continuing
Lecturer for the Writing Program at UC Santa Barbara. *The Global
Solution to AI*, p. 77-97, "Neuro-psychoanalysis and the Representation
of Humans as Biological Machines"
https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-90786-9_5 //MDC

Confronting Dualism

[While the neuro-psychoanalysts would like to argue that they are simply
making Freud more scientific by integrating recent findings from brain
studies, the truth is that they are **repressing Freud's insights** in
order to present a reductive, biological model of the human
mind]{.underline}.Footnote24 Part of this strategy is to insist that
they are both maintaining and transcending the distinction between the
physical brain and the immaterial mind: "If we look at it with our
physical eyes, we see a brain, biological organ-like many others. If we
look at it with the eyes of our subjective consciousness, we come into
contact with mental states such as sadness, desire, and pleasure. It is
therefore necessary to keep both points of view (subjective and
objective) open and build dynamic parallelism between them" (3). The
way, then, to overcome the mind-body split is to argue that both sides
are distinct, yet they work in a parallel fashion.Footnote25 [One of the
problems with this theory is that Freud emphasizes how the mind can
imagine things that go beyond material reality, and human desire and
affects make a break with biological determinism]{.underline}. For
Freud, the mind can affect the body, and the body can affect the mind,
but they are not simply two sides of the same coin.Footnote26 [The
argument that the mind and body are distinct yet parallel is a
**rhetorical trick** driven by the desire to combine together two
**opposing theories**: psychoanalysis and neuroscience.]{.underline}

Although we can trace the activation of particular brain regions when
specific mental states occur, we cannot affirm that these mental states
are the pure product of physical structures.Footnote27 Since anything
can become a cause of desire, and people can imagine things that do not
exist in reality, their emotional states and internal mental
representations cannot be directly traced back to physical, biological
causes and structures. However, since Possati wants to produce a
scientific understanding of the human mind, he is motivated to posit
that only physical structures enable an objective, scientific
observation: "If we admit a single entity X 'behind' the terms 'mind'
and 'brain,' then we can say that (a) the mind is X perceived
subjectively---that is, through one's own consciousness---and (b) the
brain is X perceived objectively---that is, through external perception
and objectifying methods of sciences" (3). By arguing that the mind and
the body are separate but parallel, he is able to claim that he can
combine the objective, scientific analysis of the brain with a
subjective perception of the mind.Footnote28 Yet, he also wants to argue
that the mind is determined by emotions, and emotions are the result of
biological evolution, and so by focusing on emotion, he can eliminate
human subjectivity.

I would argue that [Possati is presenting a pseudo-science because he
relies on rhetorical tricks in order to make his supposedly rational,
objective observations]{.underline}.Footnote29 For example, in the
following argument, he presents an important criticism of his approach
only to dismiss it with a metaphorical sleight of hand:

neuropsychoanalysis does not intend to reduce the mind to the brain;
even if it has been accused of biologism, it does not intend to reduce
everything to biochemical processes and anatomy. All mental phenomena
require a biological correlate; this is indisputable. This does not
mean, however, entirely reducing the mental phenomena and their meaning
to supposed biological correlates. Biological and psychological
dimensions must be kept together; they must be considered two sources of
information of the same value. (3)

The [only way he can make this argument is by arguing at the same time
that mental states and biology are distinct, but they are also part of
the same thing]{.underline}. Once again, what is missing from this
theory is Freud's notion that the mind can separate itself from the
body, and the body does not fully determine desire and other affective
states.Footnote30 There is simply no way of moving from the physical to
the mental if one does not account for how the human mind is able to
imagine things that do not exist in reality, and we cannot affirm human
free will if we think that we are completely programmed by
evolution.Footnote31

In [replacing cognition with emotion in the definition of intelligence,
Possati is able to **repress human subjectivity** as he offers a
reductive understanding of human emotions]{.underline}.Footnote32 From
his perspective, his turn to biology and neuroscience helps him to
complete the task that Freud himself pursued:

The separation between psychoanalysis and neuroscience was for him only
a pragmatic, strategic, and temporary solution; it was motivated by the
lack of knowledge about the brain at the time. However, as Freud repeats
in several passages, the inevitable progress of neuroscience would
sooner or later lead to a bridging of the gap between the two
disciplines and to an organic basis for the discoveries of
psychoanalysis. (3)

While it is true that Freud at times argued that the future may bring a
biological explanation for all of his theories, his own discourse tells
a very different story.Footnote33 [By affirming human free will and
mental autonomy, Freud offered a way to see beyond biological
determinism]{.underline}. Interestingly, Possati keeps insisting that he
is not a biological determinist, but then he often returns to a
reductive evolutionary model.

### 1AR\-\--AT: Cards That Don't Actually Defend Science

#### Their evasiveness is proof that their theory is wrong. Only pseudoscientists [shrink from inquiry]{.underline} rather than engaging with it.

Maarten **Boudry** 10-27-20**21**, Dr. Maarten Boudry is a philosopher
of science and first holder of the Etienne Vermeersch Chair of Critical
Thinking at Ghent University. *Journal for General Philosophy of
Science*, Volume 53, p. 83-101"Diagnosing Pseudoscience -- by Getting
Rid of the Demarcation Problem"

5 Discussion and Conclusion

Philosophers of science have no agreed-upon account of "epistemic
warrant" in the sciences, and it is unlikely that we will ever reach
consensus on that matter. Though there are general accounts of epistemic
warrant such as Bayesianism, what it means for a theory to be
epistemically (un)warranted in one domain will differ substantially from
what that means in another domain, depending on the methodological
standards and explanatory norms pertaining to that field of inquiry. For
example, the protocol of randomized and double-blind clinical trials is
the gold standard for epistemic warrant in the medical sciences, but it
is not applicable to many other fields. And even in the medical
sciences, there are several others ways for a hypothesis to enjoy
epistemic warrant, in circumstances where the gold standard RCTs is
unattainable for practical or ethical reasons (e.g. circumstantial
evidence, cohort studies, mathematical models, and so forth).

If we have no general account of epistemic warrant in the sciences, then
a fortiori we can have no such account for the absence of epistemic
warrant. Even within a single field, it is difficult to list all the
possible ways in which a theory may fail to be epistemically warranted,
for the simple reason that in a complex world there will always be more
ways to do things wrong than to do them right. This, as Hansson pointed
out, means that the criterion of epistemic warrant is "not operational
on its own" for demarcation purposes (Hansson 2013, 71). [It is true
that all pseudosciences suffer from a **lack of epistemic warrant** in
one way or another, but until we have fleshed out what (lack of)
epistemic warrant amounts to in each domain, we will have made little
progress in separating the wheat from the chaff]{.underline}.

In cracking the demarcation problem, however, [the **symptomatic
approach** developed in this paper allows us to sidestep the question of
epistemic warrant]{.underline}. While there are myriad ways in which a
theory can fail to be epistemically warranted, [there are comparatively
fewer ways to create a false impression of epistemic warrant, and these
ways are largely similar across different fields of
inquiry]{.underline}. The epistemic flaws of a doctrine like creationism
are quite different from those of Holocaust denialism or astrology, but
the strategies engaged in by their advocates to explain away
counterevidence and solicit spurious confirmations will be largely
similar. In a recent paper, Dawes (2018) developed a "social process"
criterion for demarcation that is also symptomatic in the sense
developed here: it does not directly confront the epistemic defects of a
theory, but instead uses the rejection or exclusion of said theory by
the scientific community as an indirect, pro tanto reason to regard it
as pseudoscientific.

Laudan's principal objection to the demarcation project was that there
is no silver bullet or shortcut to separate good from bad theories.
Whether we like it or not, we have to put in the hard work of actually
evaluating the merits of each theory in a piecemeal fashion (Laudan
1982). The current proposal, however, while nothing like a silver
bullet, does allow us to save some time and effort in diagnosing each
new pseudoscience. [If we see proponents of a belief system
systematically engage in **evasive behavior and spurious moves**, we may
suspect that there is something rotten about that theory. Such a
symptomatic approach is useful for lay people and policy makers, but I
believe it also has some value for the scientific experts themselves: if
you are confronted with a theory that engages in these kinds of evasions
and equivocations, you have pro tanto reasons to **be very suspicious**
of that theory, even if you have not fully investigated all the relevant
evidence]{.underline}. Indeed, if the argument developed in this paper
is correct, the symptomatic demarcation criterion is general and can be
applied across the board: whatever the subject matter, pseudosciences in
all fields of inquiry will exhibit the immunizing tactics described
above, because all of them are confronted with the same problem: how to
survive and keep up appearances despite a lack of epistemic warrant?

Finally, though I believe my approach differs substantially from
Popper's falsificationism, it may also help to explain the enduring
popularity --- some would say tenacity --- of Popper's philosophy among
working scientists. Philosophers of science have poked numerous holes in
falsificationism, but scientists somehow have failed to take notice, and
Popper's stature in the scientific community remains largely
undiminished (Godfrey-Smith 2009). Indeed, many scientists do not
realize that their own scientific conduct contradicts the dictates of
strict falsificationism, which does not even allow for the notion that
theories can be confirmed or supported by evidence. Not only do Popper's
admirers seem to systematically distort his views of science, but more
importantly, they distort them in a more reasonable and sensible
direction. This is because, as I have argued, there is indeed an
important kernel of insight lurking in Popper's conception of science,
which was evidenced in his astute remarks on psychoanalysis and Marxism,
but which was unfortunately burdened with the double baggage of logicism
and deductivism. This kernel is: [good scientists stick out their necks
because they can afford to do so, while **pseudoscientists forever have
to "shrink from inquiry**", as Thomas Paine put it, because their
theories have nothing to do with reality. Science makes progress by
taking bold risks and learning from mistakes, while pseudoscience dig in
their heels and stick to their guns.]{.underline}

This paper does not pretend to have offered the final solution on the
question of pseudoscience, but I hope to have helped to revive interest
in a problem that has long been neglected by philosophers of science.
"[Pseudoscience" carves out a genuine phenomenon in our societies and,
because it causes significant damage, deserves closer philosophical
scrutiny]{.underline}. Philosophers of science can provide a valuable
service to the public interest by helping lay people and policy makers
to identify the false contenders of science. We should not resuscitate
the dream of a grand solution to what Lakatos called "the generalized
demarcation problem", but neither should we pretend that pseudoscience
does not exist.

### 2AC\-\--Psycho Bad

#### Psychoanalysis isn't universal and scaling it up to political conclusions is colonialist

**Rogers, 17**---Senior Lecturer in Criminology in the School of
Political Sciences at the University of Melbourne and Adjunct Professor
at Griffith Law School, Queensland (Juliet, "Is Psychoanalysis
Universal? Politics, Desire, and Law in Colonial Contexts," Political
Psychology, Vol. 38, No. 4, 2017, dml)

[[The **presumption of**]{.mark} **a [universal]{.mark} form of
[desire]{.mark}** [is an important starting point]{.mark} for the
analyst]{.underline} of any patient who arrives on the couch in the
psychoanalytic clinic. The psychoanalyst can only offer certain
parameters, with all their limitations. The patient, if the analyst
allows for an interrogation of their own forms of resistance, however,
can speak back to any frame of desire that the analyst presumes or
proposes. And the analyst---if Jacques Lacan's thoughts on resistance
are taken seriously (Lacan, 2007, p. 497; Rogers, 2016, pp.
183--187)---must listen, attend, learn, and adapt. But [when the desires
of subjects are **extended into the political realm**, when the wants
and needs of every subject are **presumed to articulate** with a
**psychoanalytic notion of universal desire**, then **something is
lost**]{.underline}. That something might be called the desire of the
other, or it might not be called desire at all.

The desire of the other is not easily seen in the wake of European
Enlightenment that has engulfed the imagination of psychoanalytic and
political theorists and practitioners alike. It is not easily seen, and
it is not easily conversed with when epistemological work presumes a
trajectory of desire and then applies it. In this application, [[there
is **little space** for a **radically other**]{.mark} **performance** of
[politics]{.mark} as action or imagination [to appear]{.mark}. [The
subject]{.mark} who is subsumed into this imagination]{.underline}---the
subject Gayatri Spivak (1996)1 describes as "the Other of
Europe"---[[has **little opportunity to do more** than "**utter**" under
the weight of its imagined subjectivity]{.underline}]{.mark}. As Spivak
(1999) says:

[\[I\]n the constitution of that Other of Europe, **great care** was
taken to **obliterate the textual ingredients** with which such a
subject could cathect, could occupy (invest?) its
itinerary]{.underline}---not only by ideological and scientific
production, but also by the institution of the law. (p. 266)

[Psychoanalysis is **as guilty** of exercising such a form of "**great
care**"]{.underline} as many of the occupations of French intellectuals
that Spivak has criticized for doing so.
[[Psychoanalysis]{.underline}]{.mark}, with its attention to the many
forms of the unconscious, can appear otherwise than guilty of this. It
can appear more open, generous, and curious about the many forms that
desire can take. In its later forms of attention to a politically
constituted "symbolic order" under the guidance of Lacan (2007), it can
also appear more attentive to the particularities of desires informed by
a politics of the time. I argue here, however, that attention [is
**already constituted** by an imagination of a subject who wants, who
needs, who desires objects, things, rights, in a mode which **[cannot
not start from]{.mark} a point of origin**, and [a **particular
political form of origin** which then **precludes the
recognition**]{.mark}]{.underline}---in both the clinic and in political
analysis2 ---[[of **other forms of desire**]{.underline}]{.mark}, "with
which such a subject could cathect, could occupy (invest?) its
itinerary." [When practices such as political psychoanalysis **presume a
particular form of desire**, [what is at stake]{.mark} in this
constitution of desire [is the **political subject**]{.mark} or [the
**Other**]{.mark} **of Europe** [who cannot
"speak,"]{.mark}]{.underline} in Spivak's terms. [[What is lost]{.mark}
might be called **radical desire**; it might be an **itinerary which is
cathected** or **invested otherwise**, and]{.underline}, as such, [it
[**might not be recognizable** in psychoanalysis]{.mark} or in
contemporary political psychology at all]{.underline}.

[The nonrecognition of the Other of Europe, in her many forms, is a
**consistent political problem**]{.underline}---documented often and
insistently by critical race and postcolonial analysts such as Spivak,
but also Sanjay Seth, Leila Gandhi, Chandra Talpade Mohanty, Aileen
Moreton-Robinson, Elizabeth Povinelli, Ashis Nandy, Christine Black, and
Homi Bhabha. [Such nonrecognition]{.underline}, however, [when
**repostulated in political psychoanalysis** has another effect. The
trajectory of the symptoms of political
practice]{.underline}---including desires for law, justice, particular
election outcomes, rights, socioeconomic configurations, or even for the
formation of political structures themselves (democracy being only
one)--- [presume a form of desire that **refers to**, and **endures
in**, its constitution]{.underline}. As Spivak (1999) notes in her
critique of power and desire as universal:

\[S\]o is "desire" misleading because of its paleonomic burden of an
originary phenomenal passion---from philosophical intentionality on the
one hand to psychoanalytic definitive lack on the other. (p. 107)

The psychoanalytic definitive lack she speaks of refers to the Lacanian
configuration of desire as always attempting to recover, to master, to
instantiate an identity that is supposedly interminably lost as soon as
language acts upon the subject. [This **[lack]{.mark}** is inaugurated
through the subjects relation to what it cannot have]{.underline}, or,
in Spivak's terms, the "originary phenomenal passion" referring to the
oedipal scene, [which [is **presumed**]{.mark} **to be [the origin of
desire for all]{.mark}**[. This]{.mark} configuration of desire
**renders all subjects** desiring of overcoming that lack. But it [is a
**particular form of desire** and]{.mark} a **particular quality of
[lack]{.mark}**[. The presumption of this
quality]{.mark}]{.underline}---the presumption about what and how people
desire---I argue here, [[**must be accountable** to]{.mark} the
[**politico-historical configurations** which]{.mark} have produced
it]{.underline}.

[Politico-historical configurations, [**by definition**, are **not
universal**]{.mark}]{.underline}. That is, contra Zizek (2006), I argue
that [[**not all the world is a symptom**, but]{.underline}]{.mark} that
[[any psychoanalysis of a **political symptom**]{.mark}, of a
**political [subject]{.mark}**[, or]{.mark} of the [desires]{.mark}
